THE MED PAGE.
ROBERT BURNS.
He felt scant need
Of church or creed.
He took small share
In saintly prayer,
His eyes found food for his love •
He could pity poor devils condemned to hell
But sadly neglected endeavours to dwell
With the angels in luck above : •
To save one’s precious peculiar soul
He never could understand is the whole
Of a mortal’s business in life,
While all about him his human kin
With loving and hating and virtue and sin
Reel overmatched in the strife.
“The heavens for the heavens and the earth for the earth 1
I am a man—l’ll be true to my birth—
Man in my joy*—in my pain*.’’
So fearless, stalwart, erect and tree.
He gave to bis fellows right royally
His strength, his heart, his brains ;
For proud and fiery and swift and bold—
Wine of life from heart of gold,
The blood of hi* heathen manhood rolled
Full-billowed through his veins.
James Thomson.
THE just-arrived fourth volume of Henley and
Henderson’s ‘ Burns ’ completes this best of
all editions of the poet. The contents include 57
pieces attributed to Burns, of which 14 are pro-
bably not his, four are vamped, and 39 wholly
belong to him. None of these is of first-rate
merit; and most have no merit at all. There is
added a lengthy essay by W. E. Henley on
Burns’s life, genius, and achievement—the best
estimate of Burns as man and poet yet printed.
They :ay Henley has been out of health lately,
and the essay seems to want something of his
normal force—the words do not give out tht->r
message as lucidly and vividly as they ought.
There are many flashes of suggestion, but nothing
particularly inspiring or illuminating to those
who have already used Henley’s material to
reach Henley’s conclusions. Yet one can
conceive the horror of the foolish people who have
transformed Burns into a sort of Scottish Mumbo-
Jumbo, ‘a formidable rival to the Almighty
* that oheap decorous chromo - lithograph (as it
were) whioh bids fair to supplant the true Burns
—ardent, impulsive, generous; but hypochon-
driacal, passionate, imperfect—in the minds of his
oountrymen.’
Kegarding the songs of Burns, Henley oan do little more than repeat his former statement—
. . . h;3 work for the Museum consisted largely in the adaptation of old rhymes and folk-songs to modem
tries. Some ho arranged, some ho oondensed, some he enlarged, some ho reconstructed and rewrote. Stray
snatches, phrases, lines, thin echoes from a vanished past—nothing came amiss to him, nor was there any-
thing he could not turn to good account. Hi* appreciation was instant anti inevitable, hi* touch unerring.
Under his hand a patch-work of catch-words Decame a living song. He would take you two frag-
ments of different epochs, seleot the best from each, and treat the matter of his choice in such
a *tyle that it is hard to know where its components end and begin: so that nothing is oertain
about his result except that here is a piece of ark Or he would capture a wandering old refrain, adjust it to
bis own conditions, and bo renew its lyrical interest and and significance that it seems to live its true life for the
first time on his lips. Here, in fnot, iB his chief claim to perennial acceptance. He passed the folk-song of his
nation through the mint of his mind, and he reproduced it stamped with his image and lettered with his super-
scription : so that for the world at large it exists, and will go on existing, not as he found out as he left it. . . .
But a hundred forgotten singers went to the making of his achievement and himself. He did not wholly
originate those master-qualities—of fresh and taking simplicity, of vigour and directnr-ss and happy and humorous
ease, which have come to be regarded as distinctive of his verse ; for all those things, together with much of the
thought, the romance, and the sentiment for which we read and love him, were included in the estate which he
inherited from his nameless forebears ; and he so assimilated them that what is actually those forebears legacy to him
has come to be regarded as his gift to them. Those forebears aiding, he stands forth as the sole great poet of the old
Soots’ world . . .
This statement errs in favor of Burns, doubtless for the reason previously given—that it is hardly
Sjssible for any British critic to wholly rid himself of the Burns tradition, of the accumulated
urnB tnana. Burns’s touch was by no means unerring, as Henley’s essay admits. And, as Henley
also admits, that ‘ Burns lives so intense a lyric life is largely due to the fact that he took to himself,
and made his own, the lyrioal experience, the lyrical longing, the lyrical invention, the lyrical
possibilities of many nameless singers. . . . Again and again his forefathers find him in lyrical ideas,
in whose absence there must certainly—there cannot but have been—a blank in his woik.’ If, under
these circumstances, Burns was ‘ the sole great poet ’ of the old Scots world, it is evident that the
normal idea of a poet will need readjustment. What precise measure of credit attaches to the
unoriginal poetic artificer who borrows nearly all the best of hi* best work from his predecessors?
• • • •
It must be remembered that Burns himself, though he oertainly was not over-squeamish re-
garding his plagiarisms—as when he palmed off ‘ Behold the Hour ’on Clarinda as his own—
never claimed credit for many of the verses which his eulogists attribute to him. If he did, he
might have claimed credit for a Clean-Steal like ‘The Dusty Miller.’
THE DUSTY MILLER.
(At he appears in editions of Burns.)
Hey the dusty miller
And his dusty ooat 1
He will spend a shilling
Or he win a groat.
Dusty was the coat,
Dusty was the oolor.
Dusty was the kiss
That I gat frae the miller I
Hey the dusty miller
And his dusty saokl
Leeze me on the calling
Fills the dusty peck 1
Fills the dusty peck,
Brings the dusty siller I
I wad gie my coatie
For the dusty miller!
THE DUSTY MILLER.
{Am he appears in the Herd MS. whence Burns filched
h*m.)
O, the Duaty Miller, O the Dusty Hiller 1
Dusty was hi* coat, Dusty was his cullour.
Dusty was the kiss I got frae the Miller I
O, the Dusty Miller with the dusty coat.
He will spend a shilling ere he win a groat—
O, the Dusty Miller.
And ‘The Dusty Miller’ passes with thousands of devout Burns-worshipners as hi* genuine pro-
duct—an egg of genius that only his mind could lay ! In the ane Clean Steal category are ‘The
Primrose ’ (ravished from Thomas Carew—Burns himself calls it an ‘old English song, wi boh I dare
say is but little known’—wherefore he ravished); ‘Bonie Dundee’; ‘Whistle an’ I’ll Come to You,
my Lad ’; * Landlady, Count the Lawin ’ ; ‘Laddie, Lie Near Me ’ ; ‘ We’re a’ Noddin ’ ; and some
two-score more. In these cases, if Burns has not forcibly annexed the whole of his originals, he has
annexed so much of idea, phrase, and rhyfchmua that the song would be nothing without them.
* * * *
The Clean-Steal class slides imperceptibly into the Neat-Vamp class, in whioh Burns contributes
so much to the construction that the product it practically a new song. An example is ‘A Red,
Red Rose ’ —
A RED, RED ROSE.
(A* it appears in editions of Burns.)
O, my luve is like a red, red rose,
That’s newly sprung in June.
O, nty luve is like the meWlie,
That’s sweetly play’d in tune.
As fair art thou, my bonie lass,
So deep in luve am I,
And I will luve thee still, my dear,
Till a’ the seas gang dry.
Till a’ the seas gang dry, my dear,
And the rocks melt wi’ the sun.
And I will luve thee still, my dear,
While the sands o’ life shall run.

As the first verse appears in a blackletter ballad, ‘The

That blossom fresh in June,

In this class come many of the songs by which Burns is best known and loved—some 108 in all.
Inoluded are ‘The Birks of Aberfeldie,’ ‘M'Pherson’s Farewell,’ ‘lt was a’ for Our Rightful King,'
‘The Silver Tassie,’ ‘ John Anderson my .Jo,’‘o Were my Love’ (first verse Burns, second and
best old), and many others. Sometimes Burns took only an old chorus or an old fragment em-
bodying an idea to build his song on ; but Henley justly says that ‘ anyone who has tried to write a
song will agree . . . that a lyrical idea—by whioh I mean a rhythm, a burden, and a drift—
once found, the song wiites itself. It writes itself easily or with difficulty, it writes itself well or ill,
but in the end it writes itself.’
* • * •
In the Neat-Vamp class of Burns’s, songs the Derivative class merges. Just where one class
begins and the other ends is a matter for individual taste to determine. An example of the Derived
Bong is ‘ Auld Lang Syne ’ —
AULD LANG SYNE.

Ths writer’s classification of the whole body of Burns’s songs goes thus —
Burns’s Very-Own (as far as known), for which he take* 100 per cent, of what credit there is (and these in-
clude all the numerous worthless item* gathered by editors’ devotion).. .. .. .. 151 song*
Clean-Steal*, for which Burn* takes oto 25 percent, of credit .. .. .. .. 60 „
Neat-Vamps and Derivatives, for whioh Burns takes from 25 to 75 par cent, of credit 108 „
Total .. .. .. .. .. •• •• •• 809 songs
Probably every reader would alter this classification a little —one man’s indignation may tempt him
to call a Clean-Steal the song which another admires as a Neat-Vamp; but the proportion approxi-
mately stands till the evidence alters. And Burns takes credit for all the songs for whioh no originals
are known, including some which it is morally certain are Neat-Vamps. As already pi inted out, some
of his originals have almost certainly been lost : some have been destroyed by his jealous friends and
relatives; and some, probably, have escaped the perspiring editors’ search. But after me has
diligently sifted wheat from chaff all through Henley’s fat third volume, the typical Very-Own song
stands out distinctly from the typical Clean-Steal or Neat-Vamp. Examples—
WHERE, BRAVING ANGRY WINTER'S STORMS.
One of Burnt's very own songs.
Where, braving angry winter’s storms.
The lofty Ocliils rise.
Far in their shade my Peggy’s charms
First blest my wondering eyes:
As one who by some savage stream
A Ion; ly gem surveys,
Astonish’d doubly, mark* it beam
With art’s most polish’d blase.
Blest be the wild, sequester’d glade,
And blest the day and hour,
Where Peggy’* charms I first survey’d
When first 1 felt their pow’r!
The tyrant Death with grim conir 1
May seize my fleeting breath,
But tearing Peggy from my soul
Must be a stronger death.

Surely everybody can see the difference between this Burns paste and that Old-Balladist- diamond ?
And the sluggish flow, the flat forth; the empty idea, and the artificial trope tinselling the trite
sentiment—these are all thoroughly characteristic of Burns when he wrote right out of his
own head. The sincerity, the ring, the intensity (either of pathos or humor), the charm and the
power these are always the property of the rough, so-joyous, so-sad old rhymers from whom thesong-
Burns borrowed. Often his sympathy gave their work a better form ; but theirs was the
living fire. The young Burns had fire, too ; the elder Burns had rare flashes of it; but it was not the
divine fire that those earlier Titans stole from Heaven. So again, and assuredly, Biuns’s permanent
reputation must rest on the Kilmarnock volume. That volume, good though it be, stamps him not
the world-genius which his admirers fondly name him, but ‘ the satirist and singer of a parish ;
narrow in range, in language limited (a bonie lass, birks and hawthorn to lie under, and something
drumlie, something green, are about all his sentimental properties); keen in sympathy, but coarse
in fibre; unable to give or receive the most beautiful messages of Beauty; a groundlark, not a
skylark.
* ♦ * *
Yet again, this notice is not intended to exactly show Burns’s place in the poetic firmament,
‘but only to hint how far the true conception of him is removed from the conception which is
legendary and Scottish.’•
ROBERT BURNS.
Around his soul more sternly warred
The powers that smote for Wrong and Right;
And thunder-scathed and battle-scarred.
Death bore him from the fight.
But now we recognise in him
One of the high and shining race;
All gone the mortal mists that dim
The fair immortal face.
The prowling ghoul hath left his grave,
Hushed is the praying Pharisee ;
His frailties fade, his virtues brave
Live everlastingly.
And such a Crown to bind thy brow
Thy glorious Child hath gained for thee,
Thou gray old nurse of Heroes! thou
Proud mother. Poverty!
Gerald Massey.

Various Verses .
J.D. : “No plutocratically vamped-up
hysterics over Henry George’s death, and a demo-
cracy that thinks is slow to demonstrate. Hence,
several things. Perhaps George has not been
dead long enough to justify the singing of his
praises as the ablest foe to Fatmanism of this cen-
tury. Yet seldom have a couple of decades pro-
duced such a promising crop, and the harvest
songs will be world songs.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
UNFORGOTTEN.
I.
Death lays his hand on trifles, and they grow
Transfigured at his touch and thrill our hearts
With eloquence no tenderest word imparts,
And utterly break down our time-fenced woe.
A flower she loved, a ballad sweet and low ;
A ribbon she had worn and thrown aside ;
An opened drawer wherein her treasures hide ;
A ring or glove—and the pent tide will flow.
And we are back amid the vanished years.
And see the dear old homestead, and rejoice
To hear again each sweet familiar voice;
And then—in half-unconscious reverie—
We tell our beads on Memory’s rosary,
Counting our losses as the gloaming nears.
11.
We wage a constant but a fruitless war
Against Oblivion, passionately strive
Some cherished household name to keep alive,
As night would fain perpetuate some star
That glorifies it. But we strive in vain !
The headstone record, soon with moss o’ergrown,
Is hidden ’mid long grass, its place scarce known,
And as its imprint fades the memories wane.
We chide our selfish hearts, and put away
The innocent joys that new-create our life,
And summon back the past, with sorrow rife,
Intent to keep reproachful thoughts at bay :
But the sun shines, the woods put forth new leaf,
Fresh roses bloom, Love triumphs over grief.
Vic.
A.V.G.
I TOLD YOU SO.
Gladys, I, the rover,
Have come back to you.
Do you want a lover
To love you long and true?
For my heart a cypress grove
With a love-song echoes yet,
Pride said “ Nay,” but straightway Love
Whispered “ You shall not forget.”
Life with you amissing
What were it but death ?
Red lips, red lips were kissing,
With love on every breath ;
But my life was cold and dry,
Every man had maid to mate,
Love said, “Gladys waits, and why,
Why should you let Gladys wait? ”
Gladys lifts a finger,
Merrily laughs, and low
Three sister ringlets linger
And toss on her brow of snow.
Gladys says “ I told you so,
You were proud and I was cool ;
If men go —why, let them go,
That is woman’s golden rule.”
Gladys says the bluest
Eyes are those unseen,
The falsest love turns truest
With lonely leagues between.
“ So if men-lovers wish to stray,”
Says Gladys, “ they are free to go,
But when they come another day
Sweethearts shall say ‘ I told you so.’”
RODERIC QUINN.
“FELLOW FEELING
So, lie you there, you faded, tattered glove,
Cast off, that once enclosed the hand I love,
That shielded it from sun and biting wind,
Or yielded to my hand-clasp not unkind.
How low to fall from such a height! Ah me !
I wot you’ve fallen in good company.
For would not I have served that hand as well
As ever you ? And yet I also fell !
And stili you bear the impress, faint, but true,
Of those small, taper fingers that I knew.
And there you lie, a relic of the past,
And keep the shape she gave you to the last!
You were a faithful friend. In confidence
Have you conveyed full many a sweet pretence ;
Returned the gentle pressure which I gave,
And made me, even like yourself, a slave.
Did you believe her when, with dainty care,
She smoothed you out upon her hand so fair,
And vowed, by Cupid’s dart and Venus’ dove,
That thenceforth she would wear no other glove?
What thought you when she raised you to her lips
As I passed by, and kissed your glossy tips?
Confess ! You dreamed of favor in her sight ?
Alas, my friend ! I shared your sorry plight.
For know, poor comrade in my vain regrets,
Not always faithful service love begets.
Your usefulness is past; my day is over —
She has obtained new gloves and a new lover.
NIL.
LOVE AND DEATH.
A rose grew in a garden fair
With leaves flushed palely through their gold ;
The queen of all the roses there,
As every wooing zephyr told.
Then in a moment of desire
I plucked it from its parent tree,
And kissed its heart with lips of fire—
So dear a thing it was to me.
I saw it to my kiss unclose
In richer bloom, as to the sun,
Yet ere to beauty’s height it rose
It dropped its petals one by one.
Unplucked, perchance a space of days
The rose had longer kept its bloom ;
It died, and unto death gave praise,
That loving lips should be its doom.
And I am wondering with each breath
Why love and death are rivals so,
I loved that which was loved of death—
But did death conquer ? Love says “No ! ”
ZADIG.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. 'The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, 'paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Art icles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

other topical drawings in line or ivash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acce/itance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-draivn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland)is now
£l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage. England £l, with
6s. 6d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

Owing to the imposition of l\d. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.

postage— on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special' Thynne-
paper edition o/The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
SATURDAY, DECEMBER 4, 1897.
Permanent and Reproductive Works.
For the current financial year, ending 30th
June, 1898, the New South Wales Govern-
ment proposes to spend £1,839,605 of borrowed
money on public works. This will be the
biggest single year’s loan expenditure since
1893. It will be the biggest loan expendi-
ture in the Seven Provinces—barring that of
Westralia, and Westralia, being in a state of
irresponsible insanity, hardly counts. Also, it
will be the biggest loan expenditure per head
of population in the Seven Provinces, Wes-
tralia and Queensland alone excepted. Further,
Premier Reid’s loan estimates show the largest
amount of loan money expended on works
which are not permanent, and are wholly un-
reproductive ; and which are not “ works ” at
all in any legitimate sense, but mere current
expenditure ; and which it is a gross swindle to
charge to posterity—that N.S.W. has ever
yet expended in one year. Out of the above
£1,839,605 the sum of £772,000 is for works
which maty fairly be set down as reproductive,
and £200,000 for works which are probably re-
productive. The other £BOO,OOO odd consists
of casual jobs and repairs which any decently-
honest Government would defray out of re-
venue. Only if they were defrayed out of re-
venue Premier Reid would have to show a huge
deficit and admit that his system had broken
down, and then he would either have to tax his
friend, the rich and mainly German importer,
to get more revenue, or treble the land and
income-tax, or do something equally desperate.
And it is so very much more easy to pass the
bill along to posterity and let things slide.
Posterity, not being born yet, hasn’t a chance
to say anything.
• * * *
The swindle which the Reid Government
does under this head grows more awful every
year. Among the items which are set down as
being “ permanent and reproductive works”
are “landing silt, £25,000,” and improve-
ments to the Sydney Botanic Gardens' (pre-
sumably grass and trees and flowers), £BOOO.
The politician who can assert that the grass and
other improvements are “ permanent,’' or that
either they or the silt will bring in a penny of
revenue, has a very beautiful gift of inaccuracy.
Then £lB,OOO goes for lighthouses, and £137,600
for dredging rivers. The dredging isn’t per-
manent ; it is an item that will have to be done
over and over again as the rivers silt up till the
end of time—in fact, it is as much a regular
annual expense as Premier Reid s salary ; and
neither it nor the lighthouses will bring in any
revenue whatever; nevertheless, they also go
down under the “permanent and reproduc-
tive ” heading. Gaols, police-stations, and lock-
ups account for £50,000 ; instead of being repro-
ductive, these will cost a vast sum annually for
maintenance. Also, as N.S.W. will probably
keep on growing till it has a population of
50,000,000 or so, the expenditure for new gaols
and lockups will go on for the next two cen-
turies or so. And, by the time the country has
got all the new gaols and lockups it requires,
it will be time to re-build the old ones, so this
is also a regular annual expenditure which will
go on for ever—or till crime is abolished. New'
furniture for the accommodation - house at
Jenolan is also put down as permanent and re-
productive ; yet chairs and crockery aren’t per-
manent, by any means. About ,£40,000 goes
for hospitals and lunatic asylums ; George
Reid has actually the gall to assert that hos-
pitals and lunatics are paying investments.
Repairs to the Sydney Post Office, to Govern-
ment House, to the Art Gallery, and to the
Museum absorb ,£30,000, and £lO,OOO goes in
arbitration expenses, and Premier Reid
solemnly alleges that these are reproductive
and permanent. The Bdlgetin w'ould like to
know what the great Foreign-trade boss thinks
of his own veracity in the above cases.
♦ * * *
At the end of 1893 the debts of the Seven
Provinces, and the amounts spent on revenue-
producing and non-revenue-producing works in
each province, were given by Coghlan as fol-
lows •
Spent on pro- Spent on un-
ductive productive Total,
works. works.
MaorTTand ... £15,907,261 £23,770,377 £39,677,638
Tasmania ... 3,769,300 3,510,144 7,279,444
Queenslam .... 19,358,202 8,752,510 28,110,712
Westralia ... 1,871,138 560,804 2,431,942
South Australia... 16,531,368 6,006,247 21,537,615
N.S. Wales ... 45,848,869 8,255,431 54,104,300
Victoria ... 42,294,499 2,830,623 45,125,122
£145,580,637 £52,686,136 £198,266,773
These figures show that, up to the beginning of
the Reid regime, N.S. W. loan-mongering had
been comparatively honest, as the comparative
honesty of these provinces goes. Maoriland
was then the horrilde example of Australasia ;
under the policy of the unspeakable Vogel, and
other men almost as mad and unspeakable, it
spent fully three-fifths of its loans in paying
little every-day expenses which should have
been charged to revenue, and in laying up an
awful legacy for posterity. Tasmania was only
a shade better ; Victoria occupied bv far the
best position in the matter of honesty, despite
the Gillies boom-insanity, but N.S.W. at
least filled second place, though it was a
very bad second place, and its record was
three times worse than that of Victoria. At
that date, the Maoriland population owed £35
per head, for which there were no profitable
assets to show ; the people of Tasmania, £22
145.; Queensland, £2O; S.A., nearly ,£l5;
Westralia, £8 125.; N.S. W., £6 155.; and Vic-
toria, only £2 Bs.
* * * *
With the beginning of the Reid regime, how-
ever, things changed materially. For the
single year ending June ’96 the loan expendi-
ture of the Seven Provinces was given by
Coghlan as follows :
Productive. Unproductive. Total.
£ £ £
Tasmania 2,318 110,960 113,278
Queensland 281,781 310,377 592,158
N.S.W. 711,346 559,552 1,270,898
Maoriland 241,988 170,342 412.330
Westralia 409,819 240,889 650,708
S.A. 351,365 181,792 533,157
Victoria 218,796 218,796
£2,217,413 £1,573,912 13,791,325
In that year, under Reid’s guidance, New
South Wales spent more money’ on unproduc-
tive works—in other words, on works which
are properly payable out of revenue and which
are only charged to loans to create a bogus sur-
plus—than any other province, and nearly twice
as much as the next worst province—Queens-
land. It spent more per head of population
than either Maoriland or Victoria. It spent
a larger proportion of its loan money in
the work of making a bogus surplus than either
Victoria, S.A., Westralia or Maoriland, so
instead of having second place for comparative
honesty as formerly it came fourth. And
whereas in all previous years it spent, on an
average, £200,000 a year of loan money for
purely revenue purposes, Reid raised the out-
lay under this head to £559,000.
* * * *
For the year ending June, 1897, things got a
shade worse. Coghlan’s figures were :
Productive Unproductive
Loan Ditto Total.
Expenditure
Maoriland ... £254,530 £835,060 £1,089 590
Tasmania ... 27,000 54,801 81 801
N.S.W. ... 985,447 562,658 1,545!105
Queensland ... 742,357 405,984 1,148 341
S- A -, ••• 239,685 118,973 358,658
Westralia ... 1,943,789 665,880 2,609 669
Victoria ... 212,115 49,417 261’532
£4,404,923 £2,692,773 £7,097£96
The Maoriland figures are hardly a fair cri-
terion, inasmuch as they include £500,000
advanced to the 8.N.Z., which advance’has
since become productive. Taking the figures
as they stand, however, the N.S. Wales ex-
penditure on unproductive works has again
increased, and is the largest except that
of M.L. and Westralia. Also, N.S. W. mis-
applied a larger proportion of its loan
money than any other province save two, and
one of these two—Tasmania—is run on a system
of such ghastly financial profligacy that it has
apparently nothing ahead of it save insolvency.
Also N.S.W. “ blewed ” more loan-money than
any other province save the awful drunk-land of
W estralia ; and it spent more loan-money, in
proportion to population, for items which should
have been charged to revenue than either Vic-
toria, S. A., or Tasmania. Victoria, for the last
three years, has been the one decent example
of honesty, so far as its loan expenditure is con-
cerned, among the Seven Provinces.
» • * *
This year the N.S.W. expenditure on works
which yield no revenue threatens to be £250,000
more even than it was last year. And, seeing
that the loan outlay on unproductive works has
doubled from the time when Dibbs ruled in 93
to Reid’s beautifully prosperous year of 1897-
98, it is about time to ask where this sort of
thing is to end. New South Wales and Wes-
tralia are the only provinces which are
consistently getting worse in this re-
spect. But until every province of Austral-
asia passes a cast - iron law prohibiting
the expenditure of loan - money on any
save reproductive works, every province is
practically at the mercy of any dishonest and
unscrupulous Government that cares to “ fake
the accounts. It is so beautifully easy to make
a surplus by charging £200,000 or £500,000 or
£900,000 of ordinary ex.’s to the capacious loan
fund. There is absolutely no law or rule to
prevent the Government charging anything it
pleases to posterity in this fashion—even alter-
ations to Sydney Botanical Gardens, and the
“landing of silt,” and the law expenses,
and the new chairs and crockery for Je-
nolan accommodation - house. And the sys-
tem tends to keep the dishonest Treasurer
in office and fire the honest one out. The
public judges the Treasurer largely by his
surplus or his deficit; the one who pays his
bills honestly and has £lOO,OOO shortage is a
bad financier, and the man who charges
£600,000 of his current bills to posterity and
has £lOO,OOO surplus is a good one, and hardly
anybody notices that he is really £400,000
worse than the bad one.
* * * •
The Bulletin reckons that a law to shut
down definitely on the practice of charging
non-revenue-producing works to loans is badly
wanted in all the Seven Provinces. In the one
province—Victoria—which is going straight for
the time being it is wanted against the time when
the next boom-profligate gets into office. In the
case of the four regular horrible examples—
Tasmania, Westralia, Queensland, and N.S.W T .
—and the tolerably regular horrible example of
S.A., it is needed as a safeguard against the
boom-profligate already in office. At all events,
the attempt is worth making. It would at
least draw attention to a huge and growing
evil, and it would compel quite a number of
shady politicians to declare themselves de-
finitely. Any number of members annually
pass the iniquitous Loan Estimates, because
their own little parochial job is included
therein, and say nothing, who would not dare
to assert openly and definitely, and back up
the assertion with a straight-out vote, that the
landing of silt, and the new cups and saucers for
Jenolan accommodation-house, are “permanent
and reproductive works.” So far, unfor-
tunately, no one has compelled them to commit
themselves on the point.
Australia’s Duty to J. Bull.
In four of the Australias there is an agitation
to secure the amendment of the mining law ;
and J. Bull is rolling the log in each place.
Lowles, M.P., and Somers Vine are Bull’s
chief representatives in Victoria ; while in
N.B.W. Mr. Charles J. Alford, “member of
the London Institute of Mining and Metal-
lurgy,” has in the same interest been taking
for the text of a series of discourses the legalised
iniquity under which the Wentworth Pro-
prietary might have been forced to en-
gage men in order to fulfil labor cove-
nants. This, in fact, constitutes the pet
grievance everywhere: this inability of the
mine lessee to shut up shop and tell re-
fractory workmen to go and starve until they
shall be in a duly meek aiid submissive frame
of mind. Mr. Alford is good enough to out-
line a scheme which embodies the whole duty
of the “Colonial” man—to J. Bull. And this
is the ninth commandment, which is the last
and greatest of all :
When the holder shall have expended on the ground,
in work and machinery, a sum equal to, say, £5OO per
acre of his area, he should be entitled to apply for and
obtain a permanent lease or title to the mine, with the
obligations only to keep the boundaries adequately
beaconed, and to pay an annual rent of, say, £lO per
acre ; but these titles should not be granted to holders of
less than 10-acre blocks.
Certainly not; they should only be granted to
Fat men.
* * * *
But, going back, the principle is rickety. The
man who legitimately spends <£2oo per acre out
of his own pocket in prospecting is a good deal
more entitled to this perpetual lease than the
other fellow who has raised £lOOO worth of
stone or ore per acre and spent £5OO of it in the
process. The Broken Hill Block 10 co. has, for
instance, spent, no doubt, more than £5OO per
acre ; but it was on ore all the time—in other
words, the development need not have cost
it a penny. On the other hand the adjacent
JNorth Central, which has sunk a shaft several
hundred feet and done much other prospecting,
has got no ore ; wherefore all its expenditure
has come out of shareholders’ pockets. And
now those shareholders are naturally getting
tired ; but as they, who have really introduced
and spent 50 times more capital than Block 10
has done, possibly haven’t yet sunk quite £5OO
per acre, Mr. Alford would not give them the
security of a perpetual lease, though he would
not hesitate to give it to Block 10. Which
seems pretty rough on the North Central, and
a beautifully soft thing for B.H. Block 10.
* * * »
it isanother plan, in short, for Greasing the Fat
Bull. At present, it is true, no distinction in
the matter of rent, &c., is made between the
two classes of mines illustrated ; but, then
under the present law (for which nobody’ claims
perfection , no permanent title is conferred.
But plainly, Mr. Alford wants the State to
part with all interest in even the richest of its
mines its mines, please-as soon as the lessees
ha\ e spent out of the proceeds of such mines, or
otherwise, a sum equal to £5OO per acre !
When Mr Alford can find a private landlord
such an idiot as to do anything of the kind,
he will oblige Ihe Bulletin if he will
arrange an early introduction— The Bulletin
wants to bow him badly. It is as
it a ground landlord in a young city should say
to a tenant: “You collect the rents and stick
to them ; and when you have got enough to put
up a building worth .£lOOO you can have the lot
for ever for £1 a jear—always provided ” (Thb
Bulletin had almost forgotten this important
concession) “ you put up a fence to keep every-
body else out.” Simply because the landlord is
the State in an Australian province, while the
tenant is occasionally J. Bull, it is seriously
proposed we should be guilty of this idiocy.
These provinces are, under the influence of the
“ crimson thread,” capable of a great deal, hut
not quite that much.
* * * *
It is part of Mr. Alford’s mission to prove
the exceeding great value of Bull’s capital to
Australian mining. “In many cases,” he says,
“ a mining property for which a working
capital of £50,000 would be none too large is
taken up by a party of men who, between them,
could not raise £5OO for any object.” Quite true.
But it is equally true that in more cases a pro-
perty for which a working capital of £5OOO
would be ample has been secured by men who
have formed a company with a nominal capital
of £50,000, ostensibly to work it, but in reality
to manipulate it until such time as they' could
ease themselves of their promoters’ shares.
Over-capitalisation is a bigger curse than under-
capitalisation. There are mines which “re-
main undeveloped for want of capital ” ; there
are more which have been damned because they
could not pay reasonable returns to companies
burdened with non-contributing shares, expen-
sive boards of utterly ignorant directors, extra-
vagant officers, and so on. The mine that
really wants a working capital of £50,000
occurs so very rarely, and is still more rarely
recognised when it does occur, that it isn’t a
fair basis for a new law. J. Bull has a pro-
digious idea of “ putting money into mines.”
The Australian experience lias been that mining
is most successful where the mine worries along
and provides its own money. Capital is a help
to speedily develop a property, no doubt, and
get returns ; but those are advantages which,
in nine cases out of ten, are bought at far too
high a price.
* * * *
But when Mr. Alford declares that “one
very fruitful source of disaster is the law
which allows the formation of no - liability
companies in which shareholders cannot be
obliged by law to pay the calls on the shares
which they have taken up,” he says something
that The Bulletin has said several times be-
fore ; and he adds that “it is little short of
criminal in any Government to frame laws
which plainly tempt reckless or ignorant people
to invest their money and their work in under-
takings which have not the remotest chance of
ultimate success as legitimate ventures.” The
only justification of the law is that Govern-
ments know mining (on the share-market and
to some extent also elsewhere) is a gamble, and
they allow means whereby people smitten with
funk or conscience or poverty or sense may
withdraw and forfeit at any moment. That is
well enough ; and if onlyproperties “which have
not the remotest chance of ultimate success”
were affected, it wouldn’t matter much. In
fact, it might be a good thing. Unfortunately,
although the excuse for the game of back-up-
or-forfeit (for there has to be an excuse, since
the Godly indulge in it) is very often a wild-
cat, sometimes it is a genuine “show”—a
“show” which has cost some poor devil of a
prospector years of “ battling,” in which he
has sunk every penny he could earn, or beg, or
borrow, in which all his hopes and his girl’s or
his family’s hopes are centered. For his
“ whack ” he gets a few pounds in cash (hypo-
thecated long since at the store or the pub.),
the promoters taking the rest; and 20,000 or
so shares. When the boom collapses and not
enough calls come in to pay directors’ fees and
office expenses, the “show ” is sold by the bailiff
or the liquidator ; and sometimes the prospector
buys it, but generally he can’t. Anyway, the
“show” is damned; it has been tried (and
what a trial !) and found wanting. And Mr.
Alford may observe that this system, the
greatest curse which the mining industry in
this country has to dodge, has operated with
more deadly results in connection with J.
Bull’s Westralian ventures than it did even
in connection with the N.S.W, silver flutter.
And how his scheme (which, broadly, is the
scheme of the other representatives of J. Bull)
is going to improve matters in this most im-
portant respect Mr. Alford has forgotten to
explain.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Libel Law Again.
Dear Bulletin, —The recent case of Kelly v.
Sydney Daily Telegraph is an interesting
example of how the law, as laid down by the
Judges, though professing to be based solely on
precedent, really advances with the advancing
needs of the community. The decision of the
Supreme Court is noticeable for the emphatic
way in which it lays down the privileges of
newspaper proprietors as regards the publica-
tion of judicial proceedings, and more so because
it goes farther than any previous decision of our
Courts. “It is clear,” said Darley, C.J.,
delivering the judgment of the Full Court,
“ that it is the open Court that establishes the
privilege. . . . Certainlyby thestatutelaw both
in England and the colony the press is privileged
to publish a true and correct account of v\hat-
takes place in Courts of Justice,” and this
opinion was fortified by the citation of several
cases. Now, this opinion seems to be, and is, a
boon to the newspaper-man ; but, unless read
carefully, is in reality a pitfall. If by the word
“ Court ” is meant a “Court of competent juris-
diction ” —as a lawyer would understand it—
well and good ; if, on the other hand, “Court ”
is taken to include a preliminary inquiry before
a magistrate—as a layman would think—then
the decision is not good law, and is in direct
opposition to the express words of an Act of
Parliament. By the Justices Acts, the place
where a justice sits to try an information is an
open and public court, and, since the Defama-
tion Act provides that no action or information
will lie for a faithful and accurate report
of any judicial proceedings, a report of these
proceedings before a justice would fall withip


the protection of the statute. On the other
hand, when a man is charged before a justice
with an offence which the justice has no jurisdic-
diction to try him for, but which he must send on
to a higher court, the justice is not “ trying an
information,’’lie is only sitting ministerially and
is not holding a “ court.” This being so, no re-
port of such an enquiry is privileged. Again,
even if it were a court, the Defamation Act,
while making reports of judicial proceedings
privileged, goes on to state “ provided, how-
ever, that these proceedings are not of a pre-
liminary nature.” The reason for this proviso
is obvious; in a preliminary enquiry the
accused’s mouth is shut, the evidence given
against him is an ex-parte statement of the
prosecution, and it is not fair play to a man to
publish the case against him which will pro-
bably be read by the jurors who are to try his
case at a later period. This latest decision of
the court does, however, advance the law a few
steps, and that, too, in favor of the press. The
Daily Telegraph is going to appeal to the
Privy Council —a plucky proceeding, but a
hopeless one ; the decision of the Full Court to
grant a new trial will be confirmed.—Rus.
Roberts.
The Cabbage-Tree Mob.
The Legislative Council of New South Wales
passed the second reading of the Bill
for making Federation impossible with a
loud enthusiasm quite out of the usual habit
of that placid body. There was hardly a
semblance of discussion. The old cabbage-tree
mob was loud and blatant, and hideously
triumphant, and it threw all the decencies of
debate overboard. Every time a Federationist
rose to speak, the voice of some member of the
cabbage-tree brigade was uplifted to move
“ that the lion, member be not heard,” and the
cabbage-tree majority carried the motion every
time. Even Barton, despite the fact that he
represented nearly 100,000 votes at a popular
election, and was the authorised representative
of one-third of the electors of New South Wales,
was refused a hearing—mainly at the instance
of the Reid Government’s own representative,
Attorney-General Want. After this, it would
be beastly ingratitude for Premier Reid to
interfere with the Upper House. So far as
concerns the most important question of the
day, Reid and the nominee Council are of
one mind. It was Reid who suggested
fixing the minimum affirmative vote required to
carry Federation at 80,000 instead of the 50,000
previously agreed upon, and his Attorney-
General supported it with tremendous vigor,
and the Council carried it in a burst of enthu-
siasm ; and, under these circumstances, if the
Reid Government isn’t grateful to the Council
it ought to be. And just about here a curious
side-issue comes in. The Reid Government
assigns, as a reason for demanding not only a
popular majority in favor of Federation, but
also a majority mustering at least 80,000 votes,
that it is necessary to show a reasonable pro-
portion of the people to be actually in favor of
a momentous change before anything is done.
Even if there are only 1000 or 100 votes, or no
votes at all, against it, Reid holds that the
minority should have its way unless the majority
can muster the tremendous number of 80,000
active supporters, simply because the latter
crowd represents a new idea, while the former
represents an old one that is hallowed by the
grime of antiquity—and the grime of antiquity
isn’t to be lightly interfered with. Yet Reid
proposes, by a bare majority if he can get one,
to burst-up the N.S.W. Legislative Council,
which is as old and as sacred and as grimy as
the provincial system. It isn’t to be burst-
up only provided 80,000 voters declare by
a referendum that they want it destroyed ;
if the Reid Government comes back with a
majority in the Legislature, even if that
majority is returned by only 50,000 votes or
less, the process is to go ahead. Therefore,
Reid obviously doesn’t believe, except when it
suits him, in his own assertion that important
existing institutions shouldn’t be interfered
with unless by the direct mandate of 80,000
electors. But the Council probably does believe
in it, and when the Government tries to shift
it on the strength of an indirect mandate from,
perhaps, 70,000 electors, it will probably refuse
to go on the ground that old institutions are
not to be thrown overboard in this fashion.
And when Attorney-General Want boils over
at the recalcitrant fossils who won’t abolish
themselves, old man Dangar will probably move
“ that the hon. member be not heard.” Which
will be an interesting situation.

A Matter of Evidence.
The value of the average witness’s evidence in
courts of law may well be reckoned by the
various stories told by alleged eye-witnesses as
to the time at which the big Melbourne fire
broke out. The fire-brigades were called at
2.20 a.m., and when the call was received the
watchman on the tower was unable to see any
sign of smoke or fire even with the aid of a
powerful night-glass. Police are constantly
parading the locality, cabmen are flitting about,
and, as the present writer’s labors do not cease
until 2 a.m., it is within his own knowledge
that in the streets surrounding the gutted block
there are never less than 20 or 30 persons. And
all these nocturnal pedestrians and drivers
agree that the first flame was not visible until
2.15. The man who broke the fire-alarm, a
home-going meter-inspector, reached the locality
and was passing it at that hour when the
cracking of a window aroused his attention
and he walked right up to the burning
building before he saw its condition. Now
comes the other side of the story. A caretaker
in an opposite building saw the fire blazing so
brightly as to illuminate the whole city at 1.35
a.m. ; another caretaker and his whole family
and the night-watchman of the building saw
the same thing at 2 a.m. ; two more watchmen
on the opposite side of the burnt block
saw it at 1.45 ; six nurses at Melbourne Hos-
pital saw the blazes rising high in the air at
1.30, one nurse distinctly hearing the Town
Hall clock chime the half-hour (which clock, it
has since been shown, does not chime), and the
look-out man on a timber-boat on the Lower
Yarra supports this view. Rebutting evidence
supporting the police and the alarm-giver is
given by more watchmen, the passing sub-editor
of a Melb. daily, and a resident hotelkeeper
opposite the fire, and at present the matter ends
there. There is not the slightest end to he
gained by lying by the majority of these wit-
nesses ; it is probable that all fully believe
themselves to be telling the truth ; but when
honest people vary in such a manner as this the
worth of judicial evidence, which is largely
tempered with dishonesty, falls ninety per cent.,
and makes one wonder whether it would not be
better to do away with evidence altogether in
courts of law, and go back to the ancient and
more simple method of determining guilt by
chucking the prisoner into a tank and seeing if
he will float.
The Dry Sunday Crow-Eater.
The moral legislators of S.A. are trying to tear
the Sunday liquor business up by the roots, and
if they don’t succeed it won’t be through lack of
vigor. The measure now under consideration in
the Holy City still allows the bona-fide traveller
to be supplied with refreshment under suspicion
and after microscopical examination into his
antecedents, but it aims at making life a burden
to the ordinary Sunday drinker. It is proposed
that any person, not a lodger or bona-fide
traveller, found attempting to get into a hotel
on Sunday may be fined as much as .£2O.
There is no definition of what constitutes
“ attempting,” so even leaning up against the
wall of a S.A. hotel on Sunday may be costly.
Anybody not being a bona-fide lodger or
traveller who is found in a hotel on Sunday
is to be run in and fined up to .£2O unless
he can prove that he (or she) was not after
liquor. It is not necessary to be found drink-
ing, or asking for drink, or in the bar, or even
to be discovered wearing a thirsty expression ;
merely being found on the premises, unless the
accused can legally prove that he wouldn’t drink
even if pressed to do it, is sufficient. Also, the
landlord is heavily fineable for the person being
on his premises. This clause provides for the
wholesale running-in of the individual who
only wanted dinner, and of the landlord’s
mother-in-law, and of the regular lodger’s
brother, and of the doctor who called to see
after the lodger’s lung, and their incarceration
until they clear their characters to the satisfac-
tion of the bench. Moreover, though the
lodger may buy drink for himself on Sunday, if
he buys drink and gives it to someone else he is
to get particular Gehenna. Also, the landlord
who sells the drink, if it is afterwards given to
someone else, is to get particular Gehenna
also. Further, the bona-fide traveller is to
walk twice as far as he used to
do, or else tell twice as large a lie about
the distance he has walked as he used to do.
If this law passes, the South Australian will
absolutely have to lay in his liquor on Saturday
night. In this paper’s opinion, nothing proves
more conclusively the shiftlessness and want of
foresight of the average Australian than his
neglect to lay in his liquor on Saturday night.
All the worry of all the Sunday-drink legisla-
tion in all the seven provinces is caused by the
fact that 500,000 adult males, who are thirsty
every Sunday, can’t remember once a week that
they will be thirsty next Sunday also.
Melbourne Argus, on the Sandhurst Cup :
The owners of the majority of the horses had been laid
something- out of the sweep. The owner of Ayrshire was
not similarly fortunate, and though the horse was on the
ground he was scratched three-quarters of an hour before
the announced time of starting.
Which is practically an expression of news-
paper sympathy with the blackmailing system
practised upon ticket-holders by these alleged
sportsmen, whereas the aim of the daily press
should be to abolish the scandal. “ Tatter-
sall,” surely, is losing customers through the
notorious rapacity of the men who have
assumed a right to tax his sweep prizes. How
comes it that horse-owners are in a position to
make terms with Tatt.’s private clients?
Whence do they derive their knowledge of the
names and addresses of the parties they wish to
bleed ? In the case of Ayrshire, for instance,
it is clear that the ticket-drawer didn’t make
overtures to the stable. And if the offer of a
cash inducement was not hurled at Ayr-
shire’s owner we may assume that his
more fortunate brethren were, for the most
part, blackmailers (on the strength of in-
formation received from Tatt.’s office?)
rather than honorable recipients of gratui-
tous offers. In a negative way Tatt. is
responsible for the dirty business that grows out
of his lotteries. He has the power to curb it
by advising ticket-holders to refuse any impu-
dent demand for a share in their luck—when
they get it. A ticket in a Tatt.’r sweep is
advertised as an investment worth 90 per cent,
of its face value, but if the drawer of a pro-
bable starter is to be robbed entirely of his
three great chances, unless he agrees to forfeit
about the half of them, then the price stamped
on each ticket is a public delusion. If Tatt.
were to suddenly reduce his Ist, 2nd and 3rd
prizes by one-half or even one-third of their
present amount his clients would object to
being plundered, yet it makes no difference to a
winning ticket-holder w hether the 30 or 50 per
cent, reduction in his prize is wrought by the
promoter of the sweep, or the horse-ow'ner.
The public will never again get a fair chance to
win the full amount of the prizes they gamble
for until “ Tattersall ” takes measures to safe-
guard them from the blackmailers.
Apropos the Suffield case. At Central
Criminal Court, Sydney, on Nov. 24, before
Mr. Justice Stephen, William Booth was
charged wdth criminally assaulting a young
woman at Hamilton, near Newcastle, on Octo-
ber 30. There was a second count of common
assault. Mr. R. Windeyer, instructed by Mr.
J. Windeyer, appeared for the accused, who
pleaded not guilty. The jury found the accused
not guilty. (According to S.M. Herald) “ His
Honor said he thought the jury had done the
right thing. Had accused been found guilty it
would have been with very great reluctance he
would have sentenced, him. It only added
another to the many instances of a large propor-
tion of that kind which broke dovm for want of
reliance to be placed on the evidence of the pro-
secutrix." Booth was thereupon discharged from
custody. Nothing, of course, happened to the
woman wdiom the jury disbelieved. Nothing
ever does. In such cases the woman says
“Heads I win, tails you lose.” If the jury
believes her, and the man is found guilty, he
goes to gaol or the gallows ; if the jury doesn’t
believe her, she goes scot-free. This, however,
is not what the Shriek-Female means when she
raves of the inequality of the laws for men and
women. By the way, Suffield’s alleged out-
rage was also said to have been committed near
Newcastle; but he was not “ defended by Mr.
R. Windeyer, instructed by Mr. J. Win-
deyer.” In his case, the Windeyer was on
the bench—which made all the difference.

What is known to the members of the N.S.
Wales Assembly as the “ Stephens’ case,” is a
matter worth noting, and should receive serious
attention from that portion of the community
which the respectable daily press describes as
“right-thinking persons.” The facts of the
case are these : On 29 Dec., ’93, a draper named
Stephens reported to the police that his store
in Liverpool-street, Sydney, had been robbed of
goods to the value of .£l5O. Two men were
arrested, Sugarman and Fernandez. At the
trial, Fernandez was found guilty and sen-
tenced to two years imprisonment, while
Sugarman was discharged. The latter then
sued the unfortunate Stephens for malicious
prosecution, claiming £3OOO damages ! The
Crown Law Department was promptly notified
of this monstrous claim made on a man for an
arrest with which he had nothing to do, he
having madeno chargeagainst Sugarman, nor in
any way hinted to the police that Sugarman had
stolen his goods. All that Stephens did was
the simple reporting of the robbery to the
police, they making the arrest on their own re-
ponsibility. At this point the Law Depart-
ment washed its hands of the whole business,
and consequently Stephens, acting on legal
advice, had to defend the case which he, of
course, won. A couple of days after, Sugar-
man went insolvent—assets 15s.— Stephens
being £9O out of pocket defending the action.
He applied to the Attorney-General’s Depart-
ment to have this sum refunded to him, as he
was in no way to blame for the arrest, he being
simply bound over as a Crown witness during
the Quarter Sessions trial. A Select Committee
has reported in favor of Stephens’ claim,
but the Government refuses to accept
the Committee’s recommendation ! When the
adoption of the report was submitted it was
talked out, and there is every likelihood of its
being burked this session, and eventually drop-
ping into a peaceful oblivion, which is the
stereotyped way out of departmental difficul-
ties. Had Stephens not defended this case, he
would, no doubt, have had a very large verdict
recorded against him, and so he is mulct in
£9O costs over a matter which he had no hand
in, and which was directly caused by the
Government’s own officers ! Such is justice in
N.S. Wales, and no member has the backbone
to seriously tackle the iniquity.

Premier Reid’s scheme of Upper House re-
form will be miserably incomplete if it does not
do away with the present privilege enjoyed by
the barrister members of that body. These
may, and regularly do, hire themselves out to
Government, thus sinning in a way which, if
other tradesmen legislators copied it, would be
denounced by lawyerdom as a high crime
against the Constitution. Why should one
member be prevented from selling bread or
bacon to the Government, while another is
privileged to sell law ? Reid, who looks with
favor on the present practice, complains that if
a Government were not authorised to employ
Legislative Council barristers it would often
have to forego the best talent; but the com-
plaint is as applicable to every kind of contrac-
tor as to the legal one. Further, the amount
of patronage the present practice confers on a
Government has an unhealthy effect on patrons
and patronised.

Q.N. Bank Committee makes it clear, among
the other shocking things, that the bank was
practically insolvent in 1892 or even earlier,
and was only saved from ruin by the £600,000
loan hastily raised by Government at the in-
stance of the bank’s casual overdrafter, Trea-
surer MTlwraith. In the light of this,
present-Treasurer Nelson’s certificate of 23rd
June, 1893, is pleasant reading :
With regard to the main question—whether this par-
ticular hank (the Q.N.) is sound or otherwise, I may state
that the duty—a duty which I would like very much to
have passed over, but it was inevitable in my case—has
devolved upon me of making A MOST MINUTE AND
SEARCHING examination into the affairs of that insti-
tution. THAT I HAVE DONE, devoting nearly the
whole of my time to it since the House adjourned a fort-
night ago. I have given the matterthe MOSTTHOROUGH
AND SEARCHING INVESTIGATION. I have had an
opportunity of seeing ALL the securities of the bank,
AND EVEN THE CONFIDENTIAL BOOKS BELONGING
TO THE INSTITUTION. I have also had the able assist-
ance of my colleague, the Secretary' for Lands (Mr. Bar-
low), who has had a large experience previously in bank-
ing concerns ; and the result of our investigation is that I
am able to report, as I have already reported to my col-
leagues, that, in my opinion, in which my colleague, the
Secretary for Lands, concurs, the assets of the bank,
taken at a fair and reasonable valuation, and assuming
any fair method of realisation, are more than sufficient to
pay the debts of the bank to the Government, as well as
to the outside public. This investigation, I CAN ASSURE
THE HOUSE, has not been performed in any perfunctory
manner, but has been one which enables me to say with
full conviction that I am speaking the truth THAT WHAT
I HAVE STATED WITH REGARD TO THE SOUNDNESS
OF THE BANK IS CORRECT.
And that man Nelson is now Treasurer of
Queensland !
All through the Q.N. Bank censure debate the
Nelson Government and its barrackers per-
sistently evaded the matter really at issue.
Premier Nelson and his colleague Barlow, in
1893, overhauled the affairs of the bank, and
reported that it was perfectly sound, whereas
it had lost all its capital and some £2,000,000
besides. And the plea put forward is that
they merely over-valued the securities, at a
time when it was very difficult to make a
correct valuation, and put down six or seven
millions worth at nine millions. But nothing
is said about the many big disastrous loans for
which there was no security at all, and about
which Nelson and Barlow said nothing.
Supposing A and B each borrow £lO,OOO from
a bank, A giving no security, while B’s loan is
covered twice over. And supposing Nelson
and Barlow value B’s security at £30,000, then
it is quite correct to say that the bank has ad-
vancecl £20,000 and holds £30,000 worth oj
security. But the fact that B’s £lO,OOO loan is
covered three times over doesn’t make A’s
£lO,OOO loan anything else than the horrible
bad debt or bad swindle that it was before.
No matter how much or how guilelessly Nel-
son and Barlow over-estimated the securities
held against good accounts, that couldn’t make
the bad accounts against which there was no
security look one shade or one fraction better.
And Nelson and Barlow said nothing about
the ruinous losses on unsecured, or practically
unsecured, accounts. Tozer’s wail on their
behalf is that they merely over-valued things
in the aggregate to the extent of 2s. 9d. in the
£—which is wholly irrelevant. A bank might
advance two millions or ten millions worth of
security, and yet be utterly ruined—if the ten
millions worth were deposited to cover one
million of the advances, and the other million
was lent on the personal I O U’s of a crowd of
insolvent politicians.
From Moree (N.S.W.) News, of 19 Nov. :
That Licensing Case. —In re the recent refusal of the
local Licensing Bench to grant a transfer to Mrs. Brooker
and comments since made by the Bulletin thereon, we
may point out that the Police-Magistrate intimated his
own opinion before reading those of the Attorney-General
and Crown-Solicitor, and his opinion was that a divorced
woman could not hold a license. The opinions men-
tioned were only read in confirmation, and in reality had
nothing to do with the Court’s decision, which would
have been the same in any case.
A little more talk about this evil of sending
opinions to “ guide ” country police-magistrates
may do good. The P.M. undoubtedly gave bis
opinion, and then immediately read tiie Depart-
mental opinions and said that they strengthened
his opinion. Why did not the fact that the
attorney who represented the applicant thought
otherwise weaken that same opinion ? It does
not matter a bit whether the P.M.’s opinion
was identical with the Departmental one’s, nor
does it matter that the result, as the P.M.
alleges, would have been the same in any case ;
the fact remains that the Bench had-those
opinions before it and read them in open
court—which, to an evenly-balanced mind,
would certainly seem to indicate that the
Bench dispensed “ justice by dictation.” The
Bulletin is quite prepared to admit that
the Magistrate’s decision would have been the
same in any case, or at least that the Magis-
trate fully believes it would have been the
same; but it contends that the only guides a
magistrate should have are (1) his own common-
sense ; (2) his head full of legal knowledge ;
(3) his text-books ; (4) the decisions of the
Supreme Court. Any Departmental opinions
should, if a man’s back-bone be strong, be
delegated to the uttermost depths of the
w.p.b.
Speaking at Melb. Presbyterian General
Assembly, Rev. J. Neilson maintained, as a
missionary of 30 years seasoning, that it isn’t
any good going to the expense of another holy
craft like the Dayspring, deceased, because the
work is getting easier. There are 30,000
fewer heathen to work on than when he first
went to the New Hebrides, and he doesn’t be-
lieve there will be 15,000 left at the end of
another 30 years. Which must be pleasant
news for the godly, because it is clear that this
rapid diminution of population is traceable to
the circumstance that Providence whipped off
the converts as fast as they were made. Lest
they should backslide, maybe. Think of it !
Seventy thousand heathens get hold of the
“ Terewth ! ” and it kills ’em off at the rate of a
thousand a year ; and the man who was there
at the beginning of this great plague of glory
supplies the very best argument for avoiding
any further expense, because in 50 years or less
the whole caboodle will be cracking cocoanuts
in Paradise. And yet Paton and RENTOULand
other spendthrifts of the Lord’s vineyard want
to waste a lot more money in running a show
that goes of itself. The "thing is done. The
heathens are saved of soul and doomed of race.
Why hurry those who remain ?

Persons who have written desiring further
information in regard to the action of N.S.W.
Dept, of Justice in the notorious Suffield case are
referred to a 64 pp. pamphlet just issued by The
Bulletin, giving the evidence in full and a plan
of the scene of the alleged outrage. Post-free for
7 stamps —proceeds, after expenses, in aid of
Suflield’s demand for an open enquiry.
In a vigorous and graphic account of
Klondyke matters given by S.M. Herald’s
special correspondent, Skagwag is described as a
magic city of 4000 inhabitants—sprung into exis-
tence in a couple of months. To condense :
Gambling is rife, drinks 50 cents, and Idol, for a dance
with one of the five houris at the dancing-saloon. Skag-
wag is the starting-point for the White Pass, but it is
estimated that out of the thousands who have essayed
that track not 200 have got through. In one place" 50
dead horses, packs on backs, are lying where they have
fallen over a bluff. Men are sitting dejectedly all along
the trail, many crying miserably over the loss of their
pack-animals, which means exclusion from the golden
region. The stench from the thousands of dead animals
along the trail is murderous.
And, in short, the writer sums up, the only men
who should attempt this frightful journey are the
men who—having splendid health and plenty of
money—have no need to do so. At Dawson
gambling goes on night and day, the dancing-
halls are thronged, and the price of necessaries
runs from 600 to 2000 per cent, above ordinary
figures. A winter of famine is dreaded, and
already new arrivals are being forcibly deprived
of their food supplies, which are paid for and
added to the common winter-stock. And, to get
there, only this :
Swamps to be traversed, and mountains to be climbed,
all the while those infernal flies biting until strong, hardy
men shed tears of impotent rage at their innumerable
enemies. Miner’s gumboots on reaching to your thighs,
each weighing 31b. or 41b., a heavy suit of woollen wear,
blankets, provisions for perhaps 20 or 30 days, axe-pick,
shovel, and other articles—in all 50, 60, or even 80 or
1001 b., on your back, wading, climbing, scrambling, often
in a blistering sun. Perspiration running o f you in
streams, and all the time too the mosquito assailing you
at every 7 vulnerable point. Then, traversing the top of a
divide, the winds almost solidify 7 your perspiration-
saturated garments, and chill the marrow in your bones.
And, after a day of this, sitting down in a cloud of mos-
quitos to sodden bread, beans half-cooked, bacon same,
and a poor grade of coffee or tea ; then a roll in your
blanket, .your head covered up to exclude the mosquito,
for he never rests.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.

Grattan Biggs is doing a turn of the
“Shaughraun” at Sydney Lyceum, and more
Irish drama is expected later on. Nobody in this
world really knows for certain what a shaughraun
is, or whether it should be taken boiled or raw,
but the drama is a good old stand-by, and always
commands a certain amount of support. Grattan
Riggs, even in his old age, and he must be
something phenomenal in the way of old age
now, makes a very satisfactory Conn. He is,
perhaps, a shade less agile on his legs than some
younger and duller rivals, and his lower notes,
when he breathes soft nothings and whisky into
the shell-like ear of Moya, are a trifle rough, but
otherwise there is nothing to show that Riggs
isn’t just thirty. There is no falling-off in his
humor or in his fine old Sligo brogue, and there is
probably no man in Australia at present who
could play a really aggravated Irish character much
better than G. Riggs. One absolute novelty in
the present rendering of the “Shaughraun” is
that the base informer is a fat man. Every other
Harvey Duff The Bulletin ever saw has been
gaunt and fleshless, but T. P. O’Neil plays him as
a short and immensely blubbery man, who rushes
a huge corporation through a distracted land.
This paper takes rather kindly to the innovation,
and distinctly admires O’Neil. The lean informer
in rusty-black, with snail-tracks on his seams, is a
stereotyped spectre who crawls in a groove ; the fat
one, who is dressed anyhow, is fresh and original.
Besides, no one can show any good reason why an
informer shouldn’t be fat, therefore The Bulletin
reckons the present Duff a great improvement,
even apart from his acting, which is very good.
Miss Eugenie Duggan’s Clare Ffolliott is a pretty
and very nice Irish girl (this lady should have
good prospects if she would call herself something
more melodious than Duggan), and Messrs. Wil-
lard and North are two other characters whom the
writer regards with a favorable eye. The rest of
the cast, represents various grades of respectable
mediocrity. “ Colleen Bawn ” will probably
follow.
* * * *
The “Royal Divorce” looks capable of
holding its own at Her Majesty’s till Xmas. Re-
garded as a spectacle it improves on further
acquaintance. The two pictures of Waterloo, and
the dark red panorama of the fallen potentate on
his rock, took the public fancy from the first, and
the retreat from Moscow appears to meet with
more approval as it goes on. As a series of his-
torical tableaux there is one thing wanted—the
gory smash at Leipsic, which really decided
Napoleon’s downfall, and which, for blood and
howl and chaos, was fully twice as awful as
Waterloo, is altogether left out. But there wasn’t
room for everything, and, anyhow, Waterloo
looks picturesque enouuh. A character who
deserves honorable mention, not because he
has anything to do, but because he makes a
glittering panorama all on his own, is Marshal
Augereau. But, apart from Napoleon himself,
the most really creditable personage in the piece
is Atholwood, as the one inhabitant of Jenappes
who stays behind and gets brilliantly drunk when
all the rest of the population has fled. That Last
Lone Drunk is a gleam of humor in a tragic story.
W hen the fate of Europe is being decided he is
busy in the background running after imaginary
beetles on a deal-table, and probably he never
heard the great downfall, being too busy missing
the last beetle. This solitary inhabitant has an
original hiccup such as the writer never heard
before. Probably it is an ancient hiccuD which
has since gone out of general use.
* * * *
“Binks the Photographer” came to a
well-merited conclusion on Friday night, and
Sydney Palace firmly but unobtrusively closed up.
The closing up of Sydney Palace is now one of the
regular events of the show industry ; at the same
time the opening and shutting of that establish-
ment, and its brilliant re-opening some time after-
wards, and its re-shutting almost before the
re-opening has had time to become known, grow
bewildering. The Bulletin has no desire to lay
any wreaths on the grave of “Binks.” It was a
very flat and undeserving show which consisted of
an uninteresting old man dreaming at great
length about nothing, and it wasn't the sort of
thing J that anybody could consider seriously with
the hot weather coming on. A good company
gave it a far better chance than it deserved, and
held it up until decomposition set in so obviously
that it was absolutely necessary to let go. For
all the public support there was the co. might just
as well have let go the first night.
* * * *
D. H. Caston, vocalist, top-boot dancer,
and various other things,, has added himself to the
Tivoli programme this week, and up till now the
man has impressed the public favorably. Ven-
triloquist Winton has returned, and the loose-
legged comedian, the three Hayters, the two
Waldrons, and Miss Nellie Sheldon make up a
strong bill.
* * * *
There will be three pantomimes in Sydney
this year. At Her Majesty’s the firm will stage
“ The Babes in the Wood,” the Messrs. Macmahon
will produce “ Dick Whittington ” at the Lyceum,
and Harry Rickards’ “Jack the Giant-Killer”
will occupy the Royal. In addition, the Criterion
will be engaged by the Thornton Co. ; at the
Tivoli there will be variety, as usual; Fitzgerald's
Circus will be at the Railway-station ground, and
with the sundry other shows which spring np at
holiday times, the public will have to respond
very liberally to satiate all the managers.
** * *
“The French Maid,” new to Melb. Prin-
cess’s, on Saturday night, may be expected to
keep business humming till her time is up, for, if
“ The Gay Parisienne ” was as good as many
people considered it, “The Maid” is probably
better. On the other hand, if the G.P. wasn’t
a delightful musical comedy, the “ Maid ” is
probably less so. The real merits of these two
similar trifles must remain wrapped in mystery.
They are not strong enough to bear examination.
If one tries to think about them seriously they
disappear in vapor, and leave the mind a blank.
The present writer has a vague recollection that
“ The French Maid ” depends largely on a festive-
looking chorus, whose work is a handsome com-
pliment to everybody concerned in it. Why nob
call the chorus, the musical-director, and the
stage-manager before the curtain, and let the
principals rip, by way of a change? Some of
them ought to bo more often ripping than they
have been'of late.
* * * *
Two or three clever songs, notably “The
Twins” in the first act, appear to have got into
the libretto by accident. It is not at all an invari-
ably clever libretto. The plot is a somewhat dull
arrangement of misunderstandings which arise
from a rather bulky gendarme, and the flirtations
of his misguided girl, and the dense deceitfulness
of a doleful British waiter who suffers from Scotch
blood in his veins. One hollow mockery of a mis-
understanding is created by a baritone naval
lieutenant in love with Juliet Wray. Immedi-
ately on his return from foreign shores the lieuten-
ant rejoins his Juliet in a duet, and they
seem about to get married, but because the
damsel’s sketch-book contains 25 sketches of him-
self this monotonous young man feels persuaded
that an unknown rival has cut him out. Where-
upon he remarks, in a loud, commercial voice,
“ My dream is over,” after the manner of a stock-
broker hailing a cab. The Bulletin once
complained that Charles Ryley was too heavy for
a musical comedy ; but, 0 ! we have been getting
theni heavier since then. The burden of a modern
baritone lover is almost more than vve can bear.
* * • *
Bert Gilbert, as a youthful sailor, and
twin brother of a rather elderly waiter, is again a
bright and spi’ightly comedian, also Coleman (the
waiter aforesaid) displays his pessimistic humor to
the best advantage. Miss Ada Reeve, in the
name-part, is a slightly-revised edition of the
Parisienne—-winsome, dainty, and petite—not
large in any respect. Howard Vernon is awfully
misplaced as the French gendarme. Our old
friend Bunthorne can be humorous when he gets
the chance, but never airy and vivacious. Even
his French accent had to be borrowed from Rip
Van Winkle. Some of the other principals might
be more effective, but the incidental dance by Miss
Cobb and co. is a first-class tonic.
** » *
The Biograph Vaudeville Co. proceeds
along its expensive way, at Melbourne Bijou,
under the particular patronage of the local clergy
and shining lights. There is somewhat of a
solemn, respectable appearance about the name
“Biograph.” Also, it sounds instructive, and
conveys the notion of a Good Show for Good
People;. therefore the elect and the anointed
rolled up steadily to the pay-boxes last week,
whilst bad, ordinary people were mostly in a
hesitating frame of mind—which absurd figure
of speech is plagiarised from the daily press.
In order to dispel any doubt as to the frivolous
tendencies of the Bijou bill it was varied on Satur-
day, when Bellman, and Lottie Moore did a
“ turn ” in place of Phil Newbury, who took his
robust tenor to the Opera House. ' All things con-
sidered, the Biograph has been looming too large
in the Bijou advts., for, although it is a remark-
able improvement on the Cine., it isn’t a new sen-
sation. Moreover, the Bi. makes a sad mistake in
producing some jubilee pictures that don’t seem
much superior to Cine, views on the same
exhausted subject. Miss Fanny Wentworth’s
clever little musical entertainment is a large,
growing success, and Miss Lilian Tree’s top-notes
mount higher in public estimation every time she
faces the music and puts the orchestra to shame.
Just now the tremendous prima-donna is sharing
the miserere from “ II Trovatore ” with Fred
Duncan, so the friends of that pleasant, silvery
tenor had better take measures to prevent him
getting swept into space by the soprano hur-
ricane. Professor Morritt is a bright item who
improves on acquaintance, and other “turns,” in-
cluding the rough-edged boy, are cheerfully
accepted.
** * *
The “Trilby” Burlesque didn’t fall due
at the Royal last Saturday, although evidence in
favor of something different to melodrama has
been observable there for some time past.
“Trilby’’will make a re-appearance next Satur-
day (4th), and the interval is occupied by “ Col-
leen Bawn,” a sound, reliable drama which has no
charm for Maggie Moore’s faithful subjects just
at present. “ Trilby ” ought to shake things up
a bit and clear the air for a pantomime on
Boxing Night. It was said that Melbourne
wouldn’t get any pant, at Xmas, but Maggie has
made arrangements to disprove the assertion, and
the order for a sudden Grand Annual is in the
hands of the builders.
** * *
Frank Leon, the mighty clog-stepper, and
Miss Lizzie Kirk, the latest Rickards’ importa-
tion, came to the Opera House on Saturday, and
were voted valuable additions to the co. at that
favorite summer resort.

Dear Bulletin, —R<? the opinion of “Ignoramus” upon
“Two Little Vagabonds.” 1 would suggest that he is a
shocking waste of time as a critic. The man who says
there is no human interest in this famous French melo-
drama, and no pathos in the third act, is a man unfit to
discuss any subject softer than blue metal. Or else he is
incapable of considering stage characters apart from the
performance he witnesses. The two little vagabond parts
have been poorly acted in Australia. —8.8.
The boy soprano imported by Rickards is a musical
item that naturally suggests a question. Why hasn't he
been taught to phrase his lines and pronounce his words
as effectively as he produces his clear, strong voice? Any
capable choir-master could soon coach the lad up in a
pretty rendering of half-a-dozen easy ballads.
The Brothers Fitzgerald had a good time in Ade-
laide with their circus. After the first two nights (which
also showed profitable receipts), the tent was not big
enough to hold the people who desired to get in.
“ Ada Crossley made her first appearance in London
this season,” writes Tue Bulletin's London correspond-
ent, “at a 3 p.m. * morning ’ concert in the Queen’s Hall
to a pressed beef-audience. The occasion was the first
Boosey (capital b, please) ballad concert, and Australia’s
Ada shimmered to the man eye in a pale green concer-
tina-d crbpon, topped with a black hat pasturing more
delicate verdure. Fresh from the Birmingham Festival—
to which she goes next year as also to Leeds—the con-
tralto contralted superbly, but will have to keep going
to hold her own with Clara Butt, whose work in Italy has
much improved her voice. Small hot houses of flowers
were shed upon A.C., for some of which a bearded object
in a red shirt and wearing top-boots with red dust
thereon, a condenser in his hip pocket, and a brass-plate
saying ‘ Mayor of C ,’ was responsible.”
