S.S. : “ Melbourne is in for another depression. The
Firm puts up its hilarious Xmas panto, in Sydney, and
Wilson Barrett and an imported crowd will lift heavy
masses of melodrama at the Princess. With Plain Bill
Holloway’s small brother at the Royal also hanging out
the same old suffering hero, and the same old villain, and
the old familiar ‘ papers,’ the Smellville digestion will
have no assistance in its struggles with the damp, un-
wholesome duff of Merry Xmas. The management which
offers patrons melodrama on the top of cold, boiled duff at
Yulelide has no conscience and no human sympathies,
and Rickards will probably have the cream of the busi-
ness with his frivolous variety-show. I have a theory
that melodrama should never be produced in Australia
duringsum mer.”
The Bulletin has received extracts from Dutch papers
commenting very favorably upon Lalla Miranda’s appear-
ance, at the Hague Court, Theatre, as the Queen, in “ Les
Huguenots.” She is credited with an excellent voice and
method, and has predicted for her a distinguished future,
when she shall have developed more passion and become
more familiar with the operatic stage.
“ Edison’s phonograph,” writes a Melbourne correspon-
dent, “is about to boom again in the form of the Berliner
Gramophone, a little box of clockwork with an ear-
trumpet arrangement attached. 1 found the ‘ Gram ’
working in Melbourne the other day at the warehouse of
a musical-instrument importer (A. P. Svkes), who has the
Victorian agency for this droll article. It is so easilv
' worked that a child could run it as a show. A thin cir-
cular ‘recorder’ (of black wood or vulcanite), 7in. in
diameter, and holed in the centre, is slipped on to a steel
pivot at the top of the box. Then the sound trumpet is
fixed in position, you turn a handle at the side of the
box, the ‘ recorder’whirls around, and the Gramophone
talks, sings, or plays a full brass band with much better
effect than the original phonograph was capable of pro-
ducing. Half-a-dozen ‘recorders’ amount to a ‘well-
selected programme for balf-an-hour of drawing-room
entertainment. The dialogue pieces are good specimens
of American humor. You get the eloquent speech (with
natural voice inflections) of the travelling vendor of a
certain cure for rheumatism at 25 cents per bottle, and
you listen with awe to his story of the old lady, aged 115
who, after being bedridden for 17 years, applied haif-a-
bottle of the embrocation to her limbs, and now earns her
own living by dancing in the ballet. The Gramophone
recorders will soon be turned out in an immense
variety, like magic-lantern slides, and when the machine
comes a little cheaper (the present retail price is ten
guineas) it will take the place of the musical-box in all
directions. Such a level-headed showman as Phil Stuart,
lately over from Westralia, is struck on the Gramophone
and intends to go around with it. Philip takes the
religious side of the apparatus quite seriously. I watched
him whilst a rich, full voice was reciting ‘The Lord is
my Shepherd and * Our Father ’ through the trumpet
and his face would have done credit to a bishop.”

The Mercadool (N.S.W.) land cases are
hung up by order of the court till “sometime
next year . ’ Some member should obtain from
Govt, a full list of the expenses incurred in these
cases up to date, with the names of the principal
beneficiaries.
Melbourne fire-boss Stein as a prophet.
Six years ago he told the Argus : “A fire breaking
out in Craig, Williamson and Thomas’s, in Eliza-
beth-street, might, if a wind were blowing, sweep
the block through to Swanston-street.”
Brisbane (Q.) Courier magnanimously
limits its verdict upon Premier Nelson and
“ Charity ” Barlow (in their misleading ’93 report
on the Q.N. Bank) to the expression—“the two
most obstinate men in Queensland.”
Lady Nelson, interviewed by Brisbane
Courier, says_: “The Hotel Cecil ... I think
the Australia in Sydney is equal in all points to
any of the hotels in the old country.” Nice pat
on the back for Manager Moore.
“ Loraine and Other Verses,” Geo. Essex
Evans’s new volume, will shortly be published
by Geo. Robertson and Co. Mr. Evans has a
large circle of appreciative readers, who are look-
ing forward with interest to the appearance of
this, his second volume of verses—-several of
which, by the way, have already commended
themselves to readers of The Bulletin.
If N.S.W. Minister Just-Ice Gould again
contests Greta electorate, the best proof of his un-
fitness for a seat in Parliament will be The
Bulletin’s pamphlet detailing his callous obsti-
nacy in the Sufiield case. When every Greta
elector has marked and digested that pamphlet,
the narrow little lawyer will never again shed a
frozen smile over the Treasury benches.
The art of the log-roller ! Hall Caine
notified Dr. Parker that he was going to publish a
religious novel. Presently Parker preached on
“ The Christian ”at the City Temple. Mr. Caine
at once telegraphed to the Daily Chronicle word
of the fact, and added : “I should be profoundly
grateful if you could give a report.” The
Chronicle complied, but cruelly printed Mr.
Hall Caine’s telegram.
One thing : no matter whether he gets out
of gaol this year or nex£, Sufiield will not have
suffered in vain. The impression which his case
has burnt into the public mind will for a while
make it morally impossible for any N.S.W. jury,
at all events, to convict a man upon ludicrously-
insufficient evidence such as was found sufficient to
convict Suffield.
Corner Man : “Jack Murray, if he misses
the Vic. Assembly Chairmanship of Committees,
will do so because of his habit of guying his part,
as they say on the stage. The misfortune is, the
legislators will have it, that this really able man
is successor to the fatuous Jack Gardiner, of
‘Sugar, as it were,’ fame, as clown to the arena.
He is like the comedian who gets a laugh when
his voice is heard at the wing.”
What on earth does Rockhampton (C.Q.)
Record, (edited by the virtuous C. H. Buzacott,
M.L.C.) mean by this ?
There are things which cannot be spoken about in the
public press and such are the sinister rumors which are
continually coming to hand of late from the South touch-
ing certain proceedings in high life. We should rejoice
to be assured that these rumors are baseless, for they tend
to sap the very foundations of loyalty among the humbler
classes of the community, whatever effects they may have
upon polite society. One thing is quite certain, which is
that,there is no person in Australia so distinguished, or
so highly placed, as to be able to defy the moral law with
impunity. The materials for a “Nonconformist con-
science” mav yet he found among the undeveloped
resources of Queensland.

The WILD CAT COLUMN
Last year S.A. had a surplus of =£2l4o—not
very much for a country which has ±‘1,254,000 of old
deficits to wipe off ; but the Government was so inflated
about it that it made some small reductions in taxation.
After making these the next surplus, at 30th June, 1898,
was estimated at rather below ±2OOO. At the time Tub
Bulletin said that the Kingston-Holder Government was
a miserably improvident fool for sacrificing revenue at
such a time—and the event has proved it. The drought
has knocked Holder’s estimates endways ; the revenue is
already £20,000 less than it should be, and there are eight
bad months ahead, and all is chaos. The finances of S.A.
are hideously mismanaged at all times. Like N.S.W., it
lives by charging current expenditure to loan funds, and
it doesn’t so much as make an effort, as N.S.W. and
Victoria and even Tasmania do, to pay off any of its huge
shortage. And yet the Kingston Government is reckoned
moderately honest as compared with some of its prede-
cessors.
• • • •
The Kingston (S.A.) Government has ideas
about introducing a State Fire Insurance Bill next session
—insurance to be compulsory. A Government which
believes in taxing the absentee can hardly do otherwise —
the British insurance ring is about the biggest and most
expensive absentee in these provinces. All the trouble
about increased premiums arises from the fact that there
are far too many companies—mostly English—trying to
live on the business, so that half the premiums go in
expenses, through the vast multiplicity of officers, and
staffs, and managements. A State Department should he
able to cut the expenses down 60 per cent., and then even
the present rates would yield a handsome profit. Even
in little Adelaide there are 36 companies doing business,
and 36 managements trying to live on the little scrap of
local trade.
• * • •
The Provident and Industrial Insurance Co.
(Maoriland), one of the curious little proprietary life in-
surance companies which have begun to spring up of late
years, issues its eighth balance-sheet for the 12 months
ending 30th June. During that period it gathered in
£6485 in premiums, mostly on the industrial principle,
The expenses were ±'4495, equal to 69 per cent, of the pre-
miums, and in spite of this atrocious figure the report
makes some congratulatory remarks about the cheapness
with which the society is worked. Yet in reality it seems
to be worked even a great deal more expensively than
appears on the surface. During 1896 it seems to have
spent over £lOOO in extending the business, and this item
of expense, instead of being put bodily among the ex-
penses, where it belongs, has been made a suspense
account to be wiped off by instalments spread over
a large number of years. In 1897 it seems to have
expended over ±2OBO the same way, and £1465 of this is
made a suspense account and not charged against the
year’s expenses. At some other date it appears to have
spent another large sum in the ordinary work of estab-
lishing new agencies, and £1163 of this, instead of being
charged to immediateex.’s.is carried forward as asuspense
account. Apparently, if each year had been charged
with the money which actually went out in expenses,
the ex.’s for 1897 would have been about £5727—t0 col-
lect £6485 in premiums.
The Provident and Industrial claims to have
accumulated an assurance fund of only £3054 in eight
years. Against this is the alleged asset, £3562, money
spent in the extension of the business and establishment of
agencies, and not yet charged to expenses, so in reality, as
far as can be gathered from the balance-sheet, it has no
assurance fund—not one single penny—and has spent at
least £5OO of the shareholders’ capital as well. The total
paid-up capital is just £4875, which is a large and beauti-
ful security for policy holders. There is also an item
among the liabilities, “ Advance account, £3320,” about
which the writer can form no theory unless it is money
borrowed from somebody to keep things going. During
the year the funds are alleged to have increased from
£6443 to £7213, an increase of £770 —£477 by calls and
forfeiture of shares, and £293 saved out of a
premium income of £6485. In reality, owing to
the suspense account arrangement, the funds actually
on hand seem to have decreased some £460. The paid-
up capital is set down as £4875, and assuranoe
fund as £3os4—total, £7929. Yet the total funds are
alleged, in the revenue account, to be only £7213. The
interest received (apparently on £5OOO which the co. has
at fixed deposit) is set down as ±168; and the interest
paid (the writer presumes upon the £3320 “ advance
account,” which is apparently money borrowed some-
where, and on £3oodescribed as “deposit ")appears to be
£232. This represents a heavy loss, and looks like very
expensive borrowing. There is no cash at head office and
no bank balance mentioned among the assets—apparently
the co. hasn’t any. It would be interesting to know’ how
far the Provident and Industrial reckons the process of
putting expenses among the assets can be safely
carried. So far as things can be sorted out from the
balance-sheet, it is a strange and awful concern, and the
news that the shareholders have just taken their usual 5
per cent, dividend is very interesting. Isn’t it about time
that Maoriland led the way with a law for the State super-
vision of insurance companies ?
* » * *
An arrangement is announced as being in pro-
gress whereby the Cit.v Mutual Fire Insurance Co. will be
bought up by a Biitish office—the London and Lan-
cashire. If the deal comes off, which seems almost cer-
tain, there will be only four Cornstalk fire insurance
offices left in existence. Nine years ago there were
eleven, but the British offices have eaten up most of
the other seven, and those which were not eaten
up bv the ever-encroaching Bull have, as a rule, been
killed off by Bull competition. Victoria has seven offices
now, whereas it had nine in 1888, so it has held its owm
better than N.S.W. South Australia has one company
left, against four in 1888. And Tiie Bulletin still wants
to know—it has been wanting to know the same thing for
a number of years now—when some of the Protectionist
provinces will start protecting the local fire insurance
industry, which seems threatened with extinction ? It is
a vastly larger and more important industry than a good
many others which are carefully fostered by tariffs, and
the utter neglect with which it is treated is inexplicable.
* » • *.
That quaint little concern, the Tasmanian Per-
manent Executors and Trustees Association, has a half-
year’s profit of £264, against £237 at 30th March ; ±ll7 at
Sept., ’96; and £494 at March, ’96. The usual 5 per cent.
dividend is paid (£262), leaving: £2 to strengthen the
institution. This concern has led a harmless existence
at Launceston for 10 years, and has accumulated £405 in
all agrainst a possible rainy day. Eighteen months ago it
fell into a state of riotous affluence, and had a total
reserve of £574, but since then it has paid away consider-
ably more in dividends than it earned, with the above
painful result.
* • * *
The ruins of the once great and prosperous St.
James’s Building Society, Melbourne —no financial insti-
tution should ever be run in the name of any saint: look
at St. Joseph, in Sydney, too ! —issue a cheerless statement
of affairs. The paper assets amount to £262,592, of which
£118,980 are accumulated losses, and the remaining
£143,612 consist of more-or-less realisable properties
£117,000 being foreclosed mortgagees of uncertain value.
Still, the St. James isn’t in a very bad way as some build-
ing societies go now. The amount owing to depositors
is only £45,574, so they should be paid in full, and there
may even be a dividend left for the shareholders after-
wards. It all depends on whether the depositors can be
repaid before the ever-accumulating loss has swallowed
everything up. In fact, it is a race between the accumu-
lated loss and the depositors. Last year, the depositors
got £43,000 and loss swallowed up £39,000, so the former
had slightly the best of it.
* * * •
The Colonial Permanent Building and Invest-
ment Society (Melb.) having lost £920 for the past year
and £823 in ’96, and £l5Ol in ’95, proposes making the
usual appeal to its depositors for a reduction of interest.
* * * *
Horace Tozer defends the action of Premier
Nelson and his colleague, Barlow, in declaring, in 1893,
that the Q.N. Bank was quite sound, whereas it was bank-
rupt to the extent of some £2,000,000, on the ground
that their estimate of the value of the securities only
differed from that of the present committee by 2s. 9d. in
the £. Which looks very convincing. Also, if Nelson
and Barlow bad gone to inspect 8000 men, and reported
that they were all alive and well, whereas 1000 of them
were dead of cholera, that mistake would only be equal to
2s. 6d. in the £. And 2s. 6d. in the £is even less than
2s. 9d. Besides, the Nelson and Barlow estimate was out
by nearly 7s. in the £, not 2s. 9d.
* • • •
It is interesting to note that the first and only-
audit which revealed the fact that there was anything
wrong with the Queensland National Bank was a State
audit. The private audit kept its filmy eye on the insti-
tution for over 30 years, and never saw anything. The
Fat person who always rises to point out the uselessness
of Stale audits will please note.
* * • *
Ricardo: “Wild horses cannot drag auditors
into auditing. It is a purely stage business, just like the
Dramatic Banker, who hands over a sheaf of securities
with the remark, ‘These represent a miilion of money.’
Auditors complain that they are not sufficiently paid to
make a real audit. That is one thing. Another is that
they are apt to be an inferior class, like schoolmasters,
and quail before the mighty Gorgio Midas, who really
appoints them and is, besides, mixed up with them in
other ways, and puts a lot of business snacks in their
road. What private auditor, for instance, in a small
town like Brisbane could have offended MTlwraith in the
day of his glory, when he had a hand in everything,
without being ruined?”
* * * *
The North Queensland Insurance Co. publishes
a balance-sheet for the year ended 30th September, which
is decidedly its best up to date. Gross premiums
(‘102,014) are £20,000 more than last year. Net premiums
(after allowing for returns and reinsurances), are £15,000
more. On the other hand, losses (£30,239) have only in-
creased by >6OO, and working-expenses have actually
diminished by £230, despite the increase of business.
The only place where the balance-sheet doesn’t seem to
be feeling quite well is in the item “commission, rent,
and interest.” The receipts under this head havedropped
from £5781 to £3084, which is a considerable drop, and,
as it is impossible to tell how much is commission and
how much is rent and interest, it is difficult to estimate
how the investments are paying. Anyhow, the year's
profit is £10,688, whereas last year there was a loss of
£776, and the profit for 1895 was only £3113. A 6 per
cent, dividend (£3206) is declared ; £5OOO is added to re-
serve-fund, making it £15,000, and there remains a
balance of £1982 at credit of profit and loss. Taking it
all round,'the North Queensland Insurance Co. looks re-
markably well this year.
* » * •
The insurances on the properties damaged by
the great Melbourne fire are set down at £700,500. Of
this amount, British offices hold £488,500 ; other foreign
ones, £25,000-; and those of all the seven Australasian
provinces have about £187,000 among them. The local
offices, to all intents and purposes, only get the fire-
insurance business that the blessed Bull doesn’t want.
And by-and-bye Bull will want it all.
* • * *
You cannot persuade Melbourne people to re-
gard the great fire as a catastrophe. The writer has met
no insurance-people, but everybody else he has hit
against has taken quite a cheerful view of the ruin.
In “the Lane” on Sunday morning owners approached
each other, clasped hands, and remarked, with quite a
praiseworthy attempt at due solemnity : “ Bad business
this, old man. Awfully sorry for you, you know.”
“ Dreadful, isn’t it ? ” would he the reply. “ Hope you’ll
pull through all right.” But there was a twinkle in each
eye, and a merry little curl of the lip that belied the
speakers. Even the work-people temporarily suspended
are singularly optimistic, a fact due to the liberality of
many of the burnt-out owners in generously conceding
full pay pending the renewal of operations.
* * * *
Two Australian fire insurance go’s have al-
ready had to make calls in view of losses on the Melbourne
fire. Another co. has had to realise the whole of its liquid
securities, and the hanking facilities of still another will
be fully tested to meet engagements. It will take several
years’ profit! to make good the losses of two Victorian
co's, and it is quite on the cards that one concern in-
terested won't pull through.

£l7so—about the lightest loss of the whole lot.

Coincidences : —The big Melbourne fire of last
week occurred on the first anniversary of the last big
softgoods fire in Melbourne, and the Melbourne fire was on
at the same time as the big blaze in London. Paterson,
Laing and Bruce’s London and Melbourne premises were
within a few yards of the fires, and both escaped.

* * * *
Ed. Bulletin,—Here is a sample of N.S.W.
mining law as served out by Warden Clarke at Wyalong
and Yalgogrin. A syndicate applies for suspension of
labor-conditions on a 10-acre lease on private property at
Yalgogrin, on the ground that they are spending money
and employing nine men on other leases, also the pro-
perty of the syndicate, but not adjoining the lease for
which suspension is asked (these nine men being sufficient
to represent the whole of the leases held by the syndicate
if two men were put to work on each 10-acre block). The
application was opposed by an adjoining party, but appli-
cants were granted three months’ suspension for the lease
—on which they never did an hour’s work. The inference
to be drawn from this decision is that as it only takes
two men to represent 10 acres on private property, any-
one can take up 10a. of private property for gold-mining
purposes in Gundagai and another 10a. in Wilcannia, and,
so long as four men are kept working at Gundagai, sus-
pension can be obtained for the Wilcannia property ad in-
finitum. And that, of course, is the way to do away
with monopoly.—Miner, Yalgogrin.
* * * •
Mt. David--an over-boomed N.S.W. mine—
“ 670 z. gold from 250 tons, of which the first 150 tons was
composed principally of mullock, the balance being
quartz. Did not retort the silver, nor could get all the
gold off the plates without injuring them, and think it a
fair estimate to say there are about 20oz. of gold on the
plates and in the silver.” N.S.W. gold-mining to the
letter. How often ore turns into mullock—after the
crushing? The plates, too, usually carry the burden. A
man who reported like this in Bendigo or Ballarat would
keep the whole populace up at night, laughing. “ Did
not retort the silver.” The writer would bet that it does
not contain 3oz. ! The cruel facts, however, remain : they
are briefly—mine purchased for £ll,OOO cash, and the
balance up to fully £20,000 spent on machinery and mine.
Big reef, given out to he worth an oz. from end to end.
Ten heads running and 20 more going up. Insinuation
that the best part of the ore chute was gouged by the
original owner declared to be a libel on a big mine. ’ First
crushing, sdwts.; and the awful slump in the shares shows
that the poor shareholders are not taking any mullock,
and they are more depressed by the very small cake on
their own plate than consoled with that left on the bat-
tery plates. Let us pra t v—for the next crushing. Share-
holders now asked to sigm a requisition expressing want
of confidence in the management.
• * * •
Sydney Harbor Collieries’ London chairman :
“ You are aware that coal has been found in Kent, and
it has created a sensation in London. We, in the course
of a very short time, are likely to create a somewhat
greater sensation in Sydney, if we have not done so
already.” His idea of a very short time was given in
reply to questions—“ It would take a good deal of
honest work to get down by Christmas two years. The
total distance to be sunk was 2900 ft., and their colliery
would be practically the deepest in the world. The con-
tractor was bound to complete in about two and a half
years from the present time.” The optimistic chairman
proceeded to show what a fine thing it would be when
they got to working the coal, and what a fine specimen of
“ nice bituminous and splint coal” had been taken out of
the bore-hole. “So far, they had travelled down without
any difficulty whatever”—lsoft. below sea-level. Nothing
seems to have been said of the very poor—indeed value-
less-sample of coal got out of the first bore put down, or
of the fact that only two bores have been put down alto-
gether, and that sufficient time has since elapsed to have
got a dozen down. Now, if good coal had been pene-
trated in other bores on the co.’s ground, there would be
every encouragement to pay calls, of which over ±20,000
are now said to be owing, whereas if boring didn’t show a
dead certainty the co. could save a quarter-of-a-million
by shutting down altogether. The Harbor Collieries
business may turn out all right, but it has unnecessarily
been made into a tremendous gamble.
* * * •
Newbridge mine, N.S.W.—“ Stone prospects
better, suspended drives for the present. Ran battery 30
hours; crushed 32 tons, yielding 12oz. 16dwt.” A mine of
that sort can, fortunately, stand a lot of improvement 1
* » • »
Pambula, N.S.W.—“ThePambula Gold-mines
co. has struck what promises to be payable stone ;
assaying 15dwt. from a width of 3ft. The co’s battery
and cyanide plant are nearing completion, and when
finished will treat stone for the public.” This English
co. had a prospectus that cartainlv did not insinuate
it would spend a lot of money before it found payable
ore, and that when the plant was up it would be engaged
in treating ore from the other fellow’s mine ! If this par.
is resented, as others have been, Tub Bulletin will give a
hunk from said prospectus.
* * * *
Another hugely-boomed mine, the Birthday,
Parkes (N.S.W.)—“Cleaned up 120 tons (seconds) for
480 z.” Well, if so what has become of the firsts? Also,
why on earth doesn’t this ten-head mill crush twice as
much dirt? “ What have you done with the ‘firsts'?”
asked the writer of one N.S.W. mine-manager, who re-
plied with a wink, “ We only have ‘ seconds ’ 1 ”
* * * •
Northern Territory Times reports the dis-
covery in the Brock’s Creek Co. mine of a 2ft. reef with
well-defined black slate walls. One authority estimates
the crushing stuff to be worth soz. free gold per ton, with
perhaps another 2oz. or 3oz. per ton to be obtained by
other treatment. “All who have seen it regard it as one
of the richest strikes they have met for a very long time.”
* * * •
Last week an exploring-party started quietly
from Adelaide for Central Australia. Object, to look for
gold, and the syndicate running it is one which is reputed
to have made a bit out of Westralia. The party numbers
seven and is in charge of Allan A. Davidson, a smart
young mining engineer well known at Kalgoorlie in con-
nection with tellurides discovery, and just returned
from a tour in Papua for the' same syndicate—who
“have secured a concession of 8000 sq. miles of country on
and around 17° S. Lat. from the S.A. Govt., and this is to
be prospected for all its worth.”

In the Towers Warden’s-court recently,
“ Johnny Clark ” sought to obtain an order, on behalf of
Clark's Worcester and Victory co., to compel the Victory
co. to allow him to survey the latter's workings trending
in the Worcester direction. Victory co. opposed the ap-
plication, alleging that Clark’s co. desired the survey in
order to boom shares, as (it was further alleged) ‘had
been done before; application refused. White, the Vic-
tory mining-surveyor, stated in evidence that work in the
levels going towards Clark’s ground had been stopped
owing to want of reef, no ore showing in the faces, but
the Victory people refused to allow Johnny to verify—or
disprove this allegation. How the opposition crowd
does hate Johnny Clark !

* « * *
A private co. offers to build a railway from
Mareeba to Chillagoe copper-mine, N.Q. (95 miles). It is
to be built entirely free of cost to Govt.—no grants of
alternate blocks of land —merely the right of road is re-
quired and security for 50 years. Object: Development
of copper industry. The Bulletin merely' quotes this
case to show how the railway syndicate is climbing down
in its preliminary demands.
• * * *
The angry waves of railway gridiron syndicates
have somewhat subsided in Van Dobson land, but occa-
sional seas still rise. The other day, Ronald Smith,
member for Launceston, felt himself hurt by the way the
Mercury had reported him. In return, he moved that
the Governor be petitioned to appropriate £lOOO to pur-
chase certain agricultural sections—named—on the
Rasselas overland route to the West. As it turns out,
these sections belong' to editor Nichols, of the Mercury.
Needless to add, this motion, though appearing in the
Parliamentary papers, wasn’t noticed bv the dailies. The
Mercury is the greatest champion of the Great Western
Grab Railway.
* * * •
The West Coast of Tasmania is still booming in
a mild degree, but, up to the present, there are few pro-
perties that have sufficient capital behind them to work
to the end. The Rosebery is said to be financially Al,
but the ore is very dirty, and the writer is afraid that
there are snags ahead. The Colebrook is in the hands of
Schlapp and co., and ought to be all right as regards
funds. The Hercules, a zinciferous show, has about
- 25,000 a drop on a hot stone with so big' a property.
Mt. Reid isn’t taking any at present—at least, not since
Col. Kitchener left. Other shows boomed largely' of late
represent mineralised schist formations, that may be any-
thing, but not copper-mines. There is a good deal of ore
coming forward from various sources. It is mostly mined
by tributors, and most of the shows want a good deal of
work before they can lay claim to the title of mine.

Dear Bulletin, —You sav you have yet received
nothing authentic re Mt. Deddiek. This is authentic.
The Mt. Deddiek co. property is located on the Snowy
River about 30 odd miles higher up, and on the opposite
the river to the—er—Buchan mines lately managed
by Col. Morgan. The existence of these allegedly' rich
silver lodes has been known for, I believe, over tw'enty
years, but save a few surface trenches from which in-
numerable assays were taken nothing was done to test
their value till their acquisition by the present co.
about 18 months ago, when the lode now known as the
Mt. Deddiek was taken up. The first prospectus issued
described the lode as an immense formation 120yds. wide.
Samples of galena ore taken across and along the lode
from the surface assayed 60 per cent. lead and from 20 to
30oz. silver per ton. 1500 shares were issued at £5, of
which I believe vendors received half (this is from
memory). Since then the co. has acquired three or four
more blocks from James Bros., on which several lodes
have been found. Shortly after the formation of the
above co. I went up to see a large property adjoining,
through which this large Deddiek lode was supposed to
run-in fact, could be easily traced for miles, but I found
on arrival that this immense lode existed principally' in
the imagination of the interested parties. I examined the
Deddiek lode, but could see nothing of such huge propor-
tions There was a lode certainly about eight or nine
feet wide, composed of a gossany quartz with a vein of
galena ore from two or three up to nine inches thick on
one wall, but as there was not more than half-a-dozen
cuts on it and these only three or four feet deep it was
hard to judge what the thing would be like lower down
The Snowy River cuts the lode a few hundred feet from
the cuts mentioned ; the appearance of the lode there
was certainly not encouraging; the rock (granite) en-
closing it was as hard as the hobs of Gehenna, and the
galena vein could only be discovered by the aid of a
powerful glass. The lodes on James’ blocks were similar
in appearance, but perhaps a foot or two wider. Since
then (ride reports) the deepest shaft sunk is only 50ft.,
and, as far as can be learned, the work has been
confined principally to James’ blocks, the immense
formation hardly ever being mentioned (50ft. isn’t
much to sink in nearly 18 months). According to esti-
mates furnished, the cost of carting and packing would
be about £7 10s. per ton to Cunningham. The Govt,
geologist, after examination of the field, wound up a long
report by saying that he did not consider that anything
so far discovered was of a payable nature, but further
prospecting, &c., &c. Since then the reports have mostly
been furnished by more or less interested parties. How-
ever, it is always possible that in such a vast belt of
mineral country some things will pav to work. In fact
half-way between Deddiek and Buchan there are several
lodes up to 15ft. or so in width composed of a pvricous
quartz that seems well worth prospecting, as both assays
and surface appearance are highly encouraging. I don’t
like the Deddiek lodes, although I may be altogether
wrong re their value; still I have met several men well
qualified to express an opinion, who have been there since
my visit and entirely agree with me on the subject
Miner.

A London syndicate offered .£130,600 spot cash
for the Great Southern Garden Gully, Bendigo, but Geo.
Lansell, the biggest holder, says no. There are only
33, 1 50 shares in the co. —which probably accounts for
some of its success. Anyway, more than a few' moderately
successful Bendigo mines would have collapsed long ago
if they had been burdened at the outset with the weight
of capital attached to the average Anglo-Australian co.
The Garden Gully offer came through H. S. Bussfc, the
local mining registrar, through whom the syndicate is
nibbling after one or tw'o other Bendigo mines.

SOCIETY
Although the English cricketers appeared
duly to appreciate the sentiment of the legend,
“ New England Welcomes Old England,” placed
over Glen Innes cricket-ground entrance, it is a
surprising fact (writes a Bulletin correspondent)
that at the ball given in the evening “ the dis-
tinguished visitors refused to take part in the
dancing, and declined to be introduced to any of
the ladies present. The Glen Innes people are
comparing this conduct with that of Gov. Hamp-
den, who, when he visited the district about a
year ago, not only attended with a large party the
ball given in his honor, but took part in the danc-
ing, and made himself agreeable in every possible
way. Stoddart, Ranji and co., however, remained
but a short time, and then retired to their respec-
tive hotels. The natural result of which was
that the departure of these great potentates was in
striking contrast to the reception, and presented a
clear case of no ‘bawl.’ It is the first time an
English cricketing-team has visited the locality,

The average Brisbane (Q.) citizen doesn’t
care a rap about the future of the Q.N. Bank or
the fate of its inferentially accused officials. What
troubles him is that Bung in the aggregate has
got a new style of long-sleever glass—as large as
before in outside measurement, but thick-eylin-
dered and thicker-bottomed. One small swallow
and—“ Where’s that beer ? ” It isn’t in the glass
and it certainly isn’t in the man !
♦ * * *
One of the results of the hot weather. One
day last week the writer saw a cab with a lady in
it draw up outside a hotel in Pitt-street, Sydney.
The driver got down, and brought out from the
pub. an immense threepenny “colonial beer”
with a bloated yellow head on it, and handed it
to his passenger. She blew the froth on to the
horse, and drank the beer down in two gulps, and
then the driver returned the glass, and drove off
with his refreshed fare.

the Argus leaders. The heavies have been laid

The successful sale of the Brennan torpedo
in England has given so great an impetus to Aus-
tralian invention in this line that five diffei'ent
new torpedoes—all Australian —have been sub-
mitted to the British naval authorities during the
last eleven months. An Admiralty friend of the
writer's says that the officials of the submarine
engineering department are so full up of Austra-
lian torpedo specifications and models that only
inventors who don’t value their lives should risk
interviewing the department as Australians, or
with anything that looks like a torpedo or specifi-
cation of one.
* * * *
Gov. Fowell Buxton inferentially denies
the statement that he intends an early with-
drawal from his 8. A. Governorship. Judging by
the outward and visible signs, H.E. is not living
beyond his official salary. Like most other
Governors, he considers the honor of his presence
quite sufficient payment for the seats occupied by
his partv at any public entertainment.
* * * *
Gov. Buxton has made himself popular in
Adelaide in one respect : he has unostentatiously
thrown open Govt.-House to all classes, instead of
following precedent and limiting his invitations to
the select circle of money-aristocrats who usually
haunt vice-regal residences.

The population of Lane’s Cosm6 colony is
steadily increasing, and as a hundred Scotch lassies
are said to be booked for the settlement, a further
and sudden increase is a dead certainty. By the
way, the single bushmen who went out from Aus-
tralia with the Argonauts have now abstained
from womanity for about four years—quire long
enough for Bill and Jim to serve for a Rachel
a-piece. And, if Lane’s braw beauties happen to
be mostly Leahs, neither Bill nor Jim will com-
plain exceedingly.

A story is told in connection with a well-
advertised divorce-suit, now going through various
preliminary motions at the court. A muscular
young professional man is named as a co-respon-
dent, and, when he heard that he was to be drawn
into the case, he decided to go round and take it
out of respondent’s hide, said respondent being a
very little chap, at one time a very fair amateur
jockey. He, however, “struck a snag’’ in the
diminutive respondent aforesaid, who made things
very “ willing,’’and, a little later, a bruised and
battered lawyer was bundled into a cab and sent
home.

Two men recently arrested in a N.S.W.
township on a charge of theft were brought before
the local J.P. at 10 next morning, but before the

watch. “Can’t hear any more evidence to-day.
It’s 11.15 now and I have to be at the yards at
11.30 to draft sheep.” Accused consequently
“ drafted ” back to limbo for another 24 hours.

A VANISHING QUANTITY.
FAIR DINER : “ I don't see any of that nice blue cheese to-day.”
WAITER : “ Well, miss, it was on the table not long ago—but it do become
so restless.”
Hev. Rentoul, of Melb., accuses the
Argus of persistently assailing all that Presby-
terians love and honor. This isn’t quite true.
The Presbyterian, as a rule (the exception should
be in a dime museum), loves money, and the
Argus is the organ of capital. Fat Man supports
the Argus, and Fat Man supports the kirk.
You’ll see more fine carriages outside the temples
of Calvin, on Sawbath mornings, than at any other
gospel shop. The Argus’s one standard of merit
is the bank-balance, and the kirk loves also the
elder of the bank-balance. For him it preaches,
expounds, saves and damns. It lives for him’
and the thing he symbolises. Wherefore the
Argus and the kirk row in the one boat, and
Rentoul is, so far, wrong. But, then, Rentoul is
Irish, and as illogical as a shillelagh.

The incredible gullibility disclosed by the
latest Sydney confidence-trick story makes it look
itself most like an attempt upon the gullibility
of the public. It was the old game of the casu il
stranger and the accidental friend philanthropis-
ing on behalf of the rich unc’e. But, in this case,
the victim, after being diddled out of his £soo|
actually accepted a loan of ,£5O of it and went
home to the bush—from which, however, at so ne
less verdant person’s instigation, he has now come
forth again to set the police hunting for his shekels
or the shakers thereof.

H. : “Story given as true by a Melb.
detective. A fat, white-waistcoated gentleman
was talking to a woman under the electric-light
at a street corner when he laid hands on her, and
said she had picked his vest-fob of a <£s-note.
Policeman came up and laid hold of her, too. He
said it would be a simple matter to search her at
the lock-up, because she could have thrown noth-
ing away. The female-searcher ransacked her,
but found nothing. A week afterwards she
admitted to the policeman she had stolen the r te,
and pleaded to be allowed to return it by thrust-
ing it under the cuff of his great-coat while he
looked the other way. He agreed. When she
had gone he could not find the note. She had
slipped it under his cuff the former night, and
now took it out.”

Received by a small Sydney tradesman,
the other day—a square envelope of thick, hand-
made paper, post-marked “Parliament House,”
and embossed in red, “Parliament N.S.W. L.A.”
with red royal crown on top ; inside, two leaves
of notepaper of same quality similarly embossed,
“Parliament House.” The tradesman opened,
read, and handed the political document around.
It read: —
Dear Sir,—Your milk account, as you will see, tots up
to 3}4d. more than it should be. Send me a corrected
account and I will forward postal-note for the amount.
Bystander’s comment: “ These are the men who
claim the right to frank their political corres-
pondence.”

A lately-issued rule for the guidance of
officials in Sydney G.P.O. reads :
Punishment will not be inflicted for the first late at-
tendance during the month, but for the second and each
subsequent occasion a fine will be imposed.
For the first after pay-day no fine ! Yet, Holy
Joe Cook is a teetotaller and holds no brief for
Bung and Co.
There is no provision in N.S.W. Postal
Act against the delivery of letters at post offices to
children of tender years. At a suburban P. 0.,
recently, an irate father with six letters in his
hand which he had found in the possession of his
13-vear-old daughter, who had been carrying on a
clandestine correspondence, severely abused the
postmaster for delivering them to her. Certainly
a matter for official attention.

A certain N.S.W. “ Majah ” wrote to the
General a few weeks ago recommending some of
the members of his regiment for promotion. In
the list was the following :—“ Major (him-
self, if you please !) I recommend that this officer
be promoted to Lieutenant-Colonel. ” Talk about
cast-iron cheek ! What a pity the sneerful General
Hutton is not at the “ War Office ” to “ promote”
the gallant “Majah ” on his own recommenda-
tion ! The Majah’s point was even “too strong”
for the peaceably-disposed Commandant French.

An English dyspepsia-specialist would ex-
clude from diet not only bread, potatoes and
puddings, but even fruit of all sorts. In fact, for
many people, he says, boiled sole and whisky-
and-water make the only proper sustenance—the
sole diet, as it were.

Southern widow has seen one son and two
daughters divorced from their respective spouses.

N.S.W. Colonial Secretary Brunker sagely
says he isn’t prepared to permit scientific examina-
tion of executed criminals’ brains unless upon the
recommendation of Govt, medical advisers. That
is to say, Brunker would heed not the District,
nor the Supreme Court, nor even the Privy
Council, until he had received the august decision
of Petty Sessions. Brunker won’t give the
world’s leading criminologists a show until his
little local oracles are favorable, which, of course,
will never be as long as they have sense enough to
come in out of the wet by making their own
criminological dictum final.

* » * *
Sleath, the heavy bore of N.S.W. As-
sembly, when the military estimates were on,
cracked hideous jokes at the expense of Majah
Neild and other amateur Bonapartes. The Majah
now wears a sort of undress military costume in
the House, and forms himself into a hollow square
in his dreams, and performs deep military strategy
with the loaf at breakfast, so he is regarded as
fair game. Sleath jokes more like an armoured
elephant than anything else, but the painful
writhings of his victim lent point to his observa-
tions :
They might as well enroll some of the stray dogs in
Centennial Park. . . He found George Dibbs, and
about nine of the National Guard, and a little chap about
3ft, high, and a drill-instructor performing some marvel-
lous evolutions in Macquarie-street. Fifty or sixty news-
paper boys were assembled and saying, “ Oh, mister, lift
the other foot first,” and people had their windows and
doors open, and it was a kind of a circus. George Dibbs
was calmly smoking a cigar on the pavement and swore
occasionally at the little fellow because he didn’t give the
word of command soon enough, or gave it too soon. . .
One fellow would trip over the kerb on the off-side. Then
“ right-wheel ” was given, and the chap on the other side
would fall over the kerb. . . The St. George’s Rifles.
The only man enrolled for some months was the hon.
gentleman (Neild) himself. . . He was created a major
by accident a night or two before he left for England, and
the whole of his regiment consisted of himself. . . We
ought not to dress up a few old men in ridiculous uni-
forms and send them out to be a laughing-stock. . .
He saw his hon. friend, Lieut.-Colonel Neild, at the
Agricultural Ground, the other day. His spurs were 1-sin.
longer than those of anybody else. He was stuck where
every trooper had to salute him about twice in five
minutes. . . Would he have been so cool and collected
with bullets whizzing about his ears?
The obvious answer to that was that the only
time Sleath was under fire he fought a disorderly
campaign with a chair and the other man with a
ruler, and Sleath, as a badly-defeated warrior,
wasn’t qualified to criticise anybody’s military
tactics. But Neild missed the point.
T.T. : “Public telephones should be
cleaned with frequency and thoroughness.
To-day I saw a wheezy consumptive fol-
lowed at a ’phone by a lad who almost
sickened at the other’s nauseous breath.
There can be no surer method of dis-
tributing infection yet many Sydney
instruments go without cleaning from year’s
end to year’s end.”
A Yic. church deacon, as the result of
certain slanderous reports, was recently “sus-
pected ” in connection with the financial manage-
ment of a local institution. The matter was
discussed, by the chapel authorities, with the re-
sult that it was decided to permit him to continue
carrying round the plate, which he accordingly
does. But his enemies in the congregation indi-
cate their opinion of the situation by enclosing
their “threepences” and sixpences in a sealed
envelope, so that, by the time the collection is
finished the plate is a pyramid of envelopes.

The Bulletin has so long suspected Car-
dinal Moran ot a gentle and unobtrusive turn for
irony that it isn’t able to feel quite sure that the
naming of the Foundling Hospital after St. An-
thony isn’t a caustic suggestion of the most
effectual way of obviating the necessity for any
such institution. The Man who Didn’t !
* * * *
Wasn’t there a virtual miscarriage of jus-
tice about that recent conviction of a woman at
Sydney C.C. Court for uttering a counterfeit coin ?
Doesn't the fact that she took the same florin
back to the publican who bad already refused it
raise a strong presumption that, instead of trying
to impose upon him a second time, she was en-
deavoring to induce him to accept a coin which
she still believed genuine ? What about the
benefit of the doubt ? in this case a very large
and obvious doubt, indeed.

Dear B.—The ways of peelers! A man was
in charge of a boot-shop in a big Yic. town. One
day certain goods were stolen from the shop, and
my friend informed the police. An hour or two
afterwards he was requested to accompany con-
stable to the lockup for purpose of identifying a
man who was believed to be the purloiner. My
friend saw at a glance this wasn’t the man, and
said so. Prisoner consequently demanded to be
set free. “ Liberate yez, is it ? ” responded the
guardian of the law, “ why, ye’re a dhrunkand dis-
orderly.”

Moody, M.P. (S.A.), as an argument
against placing the “age of consent” too high,
told the Assembly recently that he knows a
woman who was married at 12 and became a grand-
mother at 30.
In a certain N.S.W. town there flourished
a popular doctor, J.P., Mayor, &c., and, amidst
many other social qualifications, one of the biggest
liars ever heard of—a liar of the Munchausen kind,
nothing vicious or spiteful about him. One dark
night late the doctor was going homewards with a
friend, when they tripped over aman lying lament-
ing and groaning in the gutter. The doctor, his
professional spirit aroused, lit a match to see what
was the matter with him and who he was. The
man in the gutter was really only very drunk, but
the doctor persisted in lighting matches and
examining him. “ Wonder who he is ; voice
seems familiar.” “Oh ! I know you well ’nuff—
biggish liar’n Boomadong,” said the. gutter man.
“ Wonder who he is,” repeated the doctor, lighting
another match —“ seems to know me very well.”

fined in Melbourne for evading duty on stock.
Their goods were seized, and they have rendered
to seizer the goods that are Seized.

“FREE WILL?”
A man said, “ Fate is strong,
And life is drear and long,
And all things wrong.
“ I shall not bend nor bow
My life to Fate, I vow,
But end it now.”
And he lay dead : but, lo 1
Fate had ordained it so,
Long, long ago.
F.KENNA.
A Modern Buccaneer.
Mr. R. C. Burt writes The Bulletin from Melb.
on 24th Nov. :
While in New York, connected with the Sun, I did
some w'ork for the Book Buyer, a monthly literary maga-
zine published by Scribner’s. Among the pars. I wrote
was one about the Boldrewood-Becke matter as I under-
stood it. This par. was quoted in the Evening Sun with
a contradiction or correction said to be inspired by one
Wagner. The editor of the Book Buyer is anxious to
know which is right. I enclose the clipping from the
Evening Sun, and would be exceedingly obliged if you
could let me know whether Wagner’s or my version of the
affair is the correct one.
The following is the clipping from the Evening
Sun referred :
The Book Buyer of this month contains the following :
A year or two ago Rolf Boldrewood published a book
called “ A Bold Buccaneer.” It may not be known in
America—as it is now pretty well known in Australia—
that the material of the story really belonged to Louis
Becke, whose tales of the South Seas have since made
him famous. Becke was a guest at Boldrewood’s house
once or twice before he took to writing, and he supplied
the whole of the plot which Boldrewood used, without
acknowledgment at the time. The facts became known
in Australia, and since then Boldrewood has freely
admitted his indebtedness to Becke. The picture pre-
sented herewith is, we believe, the only portrait ever pub-
lished of Rolf Boldrewood.
[This statement is not correct. What happened was as
follows While Becke was staying at the house of
“ Boldrewood ” (T. A. Browne) the latter was engaged on
a story about the islands, and Becke sold to his host a
notebook containing a number of jottings for a fixed sum
of money, with the understanding that they were to be
used for purposes of reference. They were so utilised.
When the report that “ Boldrewood” had made a wrong
use of the materials was first started the whole matter
was fully explained to the satisfaction of everybody. The
best proof of this lies in the fact that Becke did not inter-
fere in the momentary controversy, for a very simple
reason. It had been a simple commercial transaction.
It is just as absurd to make this charge against “ Boldre-
wood ” as it would be to accuse the Hon. Andrew D.
White of impropriety in using in his historical writings
material which had been worked up by young men em-
ployed by him for the purpose. “ Rolf Boldrewood ”
enjoys with all who know him the reputation of an
honorable man, both as a magistrate and a writer.]
In response to Mr. Burt’s request, and as a matter
of Australian literary interest, The Bulletin
shortly repeats the facts as related in its issues of
Sept. 1 and 8, 1894. The Book Buyer’s para-
graph is essentially true ; the Sun’s statement is
essentially false. Becke didn’t sell “ a note-book
containing a number of jottings”; he sold the
actual book of “ A Modern Buccaneer”— i.e., all
that really makes “A Modern Buccaneer” worth
reading. Becke was unknown and hard-up, and
Mr. T. A. Browne bought the MS. of his story for
<£2s or £3O. “ A Modern Buccaneer ” was first
published in three volumes. Becke’s narrative,
with style and matter substantially unchanged,
occupies nearly the whole of the first two volumes
in the three-volume edition, and some 200 pages
out of 368 in the one-volume edition. What
“ Boldrewood ” chiefly did was to pad out Becke’s
story to conventional three-volume length by the
addition of incongruous matter in his dullest
style. Then Mr. Browne published the book as
his o vn—“ A Modern Buccaneer, by Rolf Boldre-
wood ’’—and when the fact of Becke’s author-
ship leaked Out —Becke’s “ Reef and Palm ” stories
were then appearing in The Bulletin —Mr.
Browne advertised in the Sydney papers of Aug.
15 and 16, 1894, this acknowledgment:—
TO THE EDITOR OF THE SYDNEY MORN-
ING HERALD.
Dear Sir, —I desire to acknowledge my indebtedness to
Mr. Louis Becke, author of “ By Reef arid Palm,” for the
South Sea Island portion of “ A Modern Buccaneer,” ex-
cepting the chapter headed “ Poisoned Arrows,” which
was founded on a diary of a whaling cruise by my late
father.
T. A. BROWNE.
Albury, 13th August, 1894.
And “the South Sea Island portion of ‘A Modern
Buccaneer ’ ” is all that makes the book worth
reading. Becke’s matter was not used, as the
Sun says, “ for purposes of reference.” His actual-
narrative was printed, with some editorial al-
teration, as “by Rolf Boldrewood.” Mr.
Browne, who is no authority on the South Seas,
was anyhow not competent to otherwise alter
it. The matter was never explained “to the
satisfaction of everybody ” —it left an ugly blot
on “ Rolf Boldrewood’s” literary reputation. And
the Andrew D. White case isn’t in the same
street. Andrew D. White didn’t take another
man’s gist-of-a novel and affix his name
to it while suppressing the other fellow’s—ap-
parently permitting the public to give him credit
for the other fellow’s brains and brain-sweat.
The transaction would have been perfectly
fair had Mr. Browne pointed out its nature on his
title-page, or in a preface, and given Becke his
proper credit. As he didn’t—well, the transaction
smelt—and it smells still. Mr. Browne’s “ acknow-
ledgment ” is forgotten in the files of a three-
year-old newspaper ; but “A Modern Buccaneer ”
is still sold as “ by Rolf Boldrewood,” without a
word of reference to Becke’s authorship; and
persons ignorant of the facts are thereby deceived.
Yet to paste-in an acknowledgment-slip would
have meant very little trouble and expense. Mr.
Browne’s conduct in allowing the book to be still
sold as his own sole and original work is, pre-
suming that he can prevent it, in the highest
degree unsatisfactory.

A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, November 30, 1897.
Mi Dear Mtee—
One of the liveliest accounts of the defuhct
Jubilee and some of the littlenesses hanging on
to that great occasion, is given by a Sydney ladv,
who with her family has lately returned from
’Ome. The lady chats amusingly on the weird
capers cut by some of the Australians at ’Oiiie,
and the magnificence of the Cecil inmates, who
were driven all around London by a hastily-
bired staff of menials run into royal livery for the
occasion. In crowded thoroughfares the scarlet
livery on the box of the carriages always attracted
attention, and in a traffic-block the window-
frames would be darkened with faces flattened
against the panes gazing at the presumed
“royalty” lurking within. When a mere “colo-
nial ” stepped forth from thfe gorgeous surround-
ings, it was as great a come-down as when a large
red crayfish drops out of a violin-case with which
a gorgeously-attired, damsel is tripping along the
streets to her “ lesson.” The Mansion House ball
was apparently the great night out for the visitors
-—“Colonials” they were all labelled. As each
batch of arrivals from oversea pranced in the
flunkey shouted their names and addresses, and
onlookers clapped vigorously. About the Fre-
ni ieresses there was whitening of hair among
Colonial Office folk. Most of ’em weren’t dazzling
enough. One of them went to a Duchess’s garden-
party in a green-cloth coat and skirt and a
rumpled blouse. Her Gracious at first turned
up her royal nose at Premiers’wives just as Queen
Bess ignored bishops’ spouses, and for some time
the ladies didn’t exist socially. Later, after much
official coaxing (for when H.M. puts her foot down
the whole household apparently sit back and hold
tight) a grudging invitation to Windsor was given,
and the Matrons of Greater Britain sallied down
to the Castle with vast tomes of addresses, which,
on the get-it-over-and-done-with-it principle, were
taken as read. One of the Premieresses, at a sub-
sequent dinner-party, loudly descanted on the
incident, and patted Queen Victoria affably on
the back. “ Poor dear,” she sighed, “it was such
a relief the addresses were taken as read. One has
so much of that sort of tiresome thing oneself,
opening bazaars and receiving bouquets and all
that.’’
An ’aughty titled Premieress when in Lon-
don delivered a severe snub to a person in the
shipping trade who was introduced to her at some
lordly snivoo. Although from the same province
she seemed as though she had never heard of the
big firm which bears his name. “Surely you
know Mr. Blank,” said the introducer, “hecomes
from the same city as yourself.” “Ah, indeed,”
she sniffed loftily; “what is he—beef or mutton?”
The ladies’ gallery up at the House has always
a fair sprinkling of occupants. Politics jumped
into popularity with Federation. Before the
Convention only suffragers and other women with
a little axe to grind were to be seen sitting aloft;
but now the fashionable and frivolous are gathered
there. The door-attendant’s face is pervaded with
smiles whenever he sees ladies coming. He seems
to take their arrival as a personal compliment, and
bustles round courteously to find room. In a
women’s Parliament he would be an ideal door-
keeper. Debates are generally dull, and the most
absorbing thing to do is to criticise hon. members’
tailors. Summed up sartorially, the House is a
collection of baggy pants and overworked coats
and shapeless hats, though the Premier has some
nice London clothes draping his figure to com-
parative slenderness. A mild joke brightened the
all-night sitting. A member who is an adver-
tising tailor poured forth a long and windy speech,
and then J. Haynes arose and said it was a pity
that the previous speaker’s speeches weren’t as well
shrunken as his goods were advertised to be.
The wedding of Miss Groth, daughter of Mr.
H. H. Groth, of Sydney, was an event in Croydon
last week. The bridegroom is Mr. Montague
Robinson, a son of Peter Robinson the Great, of
Regent-street, London, who met his bride while
on a visit here. Her wedding-gown was an Edition
de luxe of the orthodox white satin and orange-
blossom, be-diamonded by the bridegroom. The
knot was tied in St. James’s Church, Croydon,
and attracted a large audience. A train of five
bridesmaids filed after the bride, and the groom
had a contingent of three friends to support him
through the ordeal. A large party was afterwards
given by Mr. and Mrs. Groth in honor of the
occasion.
The Amateur Orchestral Concert in the Town
Hall last Wednesday evening went with a loud
and tuneful bang from beginning to end. It was
a “special-request ” programme, and the audience
was so enraptured at its own choice that it
wanted it twice over. Several things were en-
cored, and, after one item, conductor Hazon
bowed and smiled as if he were going to repeat it,
then leant over and gave the orchestra the tip for
the next number with a grin. He “ had ” the
people this way several times, but they were all
very enthusiastic, and gave him a laurel wreath.
Rivers-Allpress played exquisitely and to great ap-
plause—though he is only a local, not an imported,
fiddler. The dressing in the hall was a weird en-
semble of street and evening garb. From the
gallery the hats below looked, like the marked-
down stock of an insolvent draper, and the dresses
were dowdy to match.
At the Town Hall, on Thursday evening,
Florence Schmidt warbled her last to a Sydney
audience, before her departure for ’Ome and more
study. She sang more charmingly than ever.
For so young a vocalist, the Bananaland songstress
has had a record career. This time last year she
was absolutely unknown to the public, and now
she has come with a rush into the front rank.
She intends to tour in the North early next year,
and also proposes to sing to a Melb. audience.
Miss Schmidt has an idea of her own in platform-
gowning, which she carried out on Thursday—in
pale blue brocade cut in a fashion that adds to her
height. Miss Ruby Reynolds, a pupil of Mr.
Steffani, sang her best, clad in simple white. An-
other draw was Lees at the piano, who
rattled over the most appalling difficulties like
five-finger exercises. Two men balanced the gifted
girlishness of the programme: M. Stael, on the
violin, and Mr. Gooud, who was encored for his
second song.
Carnations as small as daisies, a few wilted
sweet peas, and some nondescript yellow flowers
are all the floral glories this early summer has for
show. Flower-sellers in desperation are thinking
of giving up from failure of supplies. Out of un-
E remising materials, however, some very swagger
askets and posies were contrived, and handed in
farewell to Austral passengers on Monday last.
One particular tribute, bright with pinks and in-
frequent roses, was given to Miss Hilda Hoskings,
one of Sydney’s pretty girls who is departing for
London and a high-toned wedding, as she is to be
married on arrival to Mr. “Bert” Macarthur,
brother of Mr. W. Macarthur. On Friday last,
on board the Austral, Mrs. D. Anderson (Miss
Hoskings’ sister) gave an afternoon party at which
many friends were present to say good-bye to the
bride-elect, who shook hands attached to a huge
bunch of pink and mauve flowers, and wore a
white and frilly frock.
Poor little Sadie Macdonald, the fascinating
American soubrette who died a year ago last
Monday, had the art of making friends. Her
anniversary was remembered. Florists in town
were kept busy making up bunches of such rare
white blossoms as the drought and heat have
allowed to grow, and the lonely little grave near
the sea was heaped up with flowers. Then the
camera-man came along and photoed the decora-
tions, and a full description of each flower-trophy,
with name of donor, appeared next morning in
the papers, and the poetry and sentiment fled
from the incident, which thereby shrivelled into
the usual hideous self-advt.
The handbill in the penny-dreadful style put
forth by the Salvation Army to emphasise the be-
ginning of the Slum Scouring by Mrs. Booth and
sister-workers, gives Sydney a worse character
than Melbourne. “Iliad heard,” it remarks in
large capitals, “ that the shady side of Sydney
was darker still in some places than the darkest
part of the slums of Melbourne, and, alas, I can
but say it is only too true.” Mrs. B. and Mrs.
Major Wassberg set out to explore “ darkest
Sydney ” thoroughly, and including the beer, and
bad smells, and the opium-dens, and all the other
heavy shadows the picture is not more sensation-
ally lurid an exposure than ordinary police-court
cases show. There are some bright lights in it,
too. In the slumming was discovered one young
woman “on the downward track,” who has
worked hard to keep other women from following
in her own steps, and even paid the board of one
young girl for a long time to keep her out of the
streets. Also, a sensational episode of a betrayed
young woman with “ the loveliest baby,” slaving
alone honestly to support herself and child in one
small hired room, presents chiefly a shining ex-
ample of moral courage more precious than rubies
in this Grundy-ridden community, where the
average mother of an unwonted infant ties it up
neatly in brown paper and drops the parcel over
the nearest fence. The handbill ends in a to-be-
continued-in-our-next fashion, promising to give in
another issue the spicy life-storv of “Nellie the
Cockroach,” now doing time on’Cockatoo Island,
and tacks on an epigrammatic tag-moral, “ The
way of transgressors is hard indeed, especially
when it leads to oakum-picking.”
Nicholson and Co. send a bundle of eight draw-
ing-room ballads, three of which, at least, climb
considerably out of the ruck of the mediocrity
usual to this class of publication. “ Golden
Lilies,” by Franco Leoni, already very popular
here; “The 'ihree Guides” (Behrendj, wedded
to charming verse by L. A. Lefevre ; and a new
setting of Tom Hood’s “I Love Thee,” by E.
Beningfield, are all most fascinating ditties. An
inevitable Chaminade, “April,” is a rippling little
lay which would take a “ bit of doing,” as Chirg-
win would say, on the singer’s part, for the vocal
gymnastics it contains are considerable. For the
piano there are four dainty little compositions.
“Moderato in C,” by Walter Maofarren, and
“ Elgin King’s March,” by G. Leese, are carefully
fingered and quite within the scope of the fingers
of the early-morning practiser. “ Air de Ballet ”
(Bachman) and “ In the Meadow ” (Sherwin Mar-
shall) have more pretensions to musical show-off,
but are taking little pieces, bound to become well-
known.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“An unmistakeable aroma of deputy-vice-regal
distinction clings to Chief Justice Madden and hia
comely womankind, in the columns of the daily
press. An Argus paragraph states that * Lady
Madden and Miss Madden, who have been visit-
ing England in connection with the Jubilee cele-
brations, return to Melbourne by the ’ &c., &o.
They will be accompanied by Colonel Campbell,
aide-de-camp to Sir John Madden when he was
Acting-Governor of Victoria.’ The sweet in-
genuity of which par. suggests that this some-
times Excellency wrote it himself. Mrs. Madden
and the girls have certainly been visiting England
at Jubilee time, but their connection with that
brief madness was ‘all on their little own.’ And
considering that the expedition had no official
character, and was nothing to make a song about,
it really doesn’t seem absolutely necessary to brag
in the fact that Colonel Campdell acted as accom-
panist.
“ Busy little Mrs. Freeman Thomas has
appointed herself president of the ‘Nagel Con-
cert Committee,’ so one may take it for granted
that Regina N. will be fired off to Europe with a
great big bang (and a satisfactory cheque) before
the year runs out. The nervous damsel is gifted
with a phenomenal voice, as everybody knows
who heard her sing * Ben Bolt ’ when she was
fully wound up. If she discovers the force of
character necessary to carry her through a course
of training in Paris or London, the world at large
will hear from Regina Nagel presently. The
future of the voice depends solely upon the deter-
mination of the singer. Some people are apt to
shrug their shoulders and hope for the best in
Miss Nagel’s case, but, anyway, she is a fit and
proper subject for a grand farewell testimonial
concert. The event is fixed for Dec. 8, Town
Hall.
“The Metropolitan Liedertafel put all possible
pathos into its 187th programme. Maybe the rival
society’s recent capture of conductor Marshall-
Hall came as a poke in the eye to Ernest Wood’s
nightingales. At all events, most of the choruses
were doleful as funeral hymns, the sandwiched
serenades sounding jiggery in comparison.
“ Gov. and Lady Brassey arrived rather earlier
than usual, as per contract, the latter wearing her
feather-ruched cape. Aides Neville and Shaftes-
bury came along, too. The first-named, excellent
creature that he is, always looks as if he loved
doing his duty, unlike his comrade in calamity.
This wretched young man. on the strength of
being a sweet singer, must dree his weird (who-
ever else gets off scot-free) every time Govt. House
commits itself to a concert. Son-in-law'Freeman-
Thomas, though less gifted in the matter of voice,
is also a musical enthusiast. But on Liedertafel
night he was at the Neglected Children’s Meeting,
warbling of embraces, eyes, and suchlike—so
appropriate to Miss Sutherland’s platform, wasn’t
it '!
“ The just-mentioned philanthropic spinster, by
the way, has lots to tell of London life, from her
point of view, and rescues the perishing with re-
newed relish. Meanwhile, the Kirk-society, from
which she seceded to start business on non-denomi-
national lines, goes on its child-saving way in
peace and quietness. At the present moment its
prize brands snatched from the burning are a
grey-haired girl of 12, who owes her silver threads
to anxiety and hardship, and a seven-year-old
who is learning the rudiments of kissing along
with cleanliness and catechism. Some authorities
consider that the said branch of a waif’s education
might just as well go on being neglected. In sup-
port of this view they point a warning finger at
the over-huggishness of ‘ Dr. Barnardo’s boy,’
named as co-respondent in a recent quashed
divorce suit. Who knows? P raps, if the curly-
headed affable sprat in question had been given
scope for his young affections in early life, he
wouldn’t have developed them so acutely at sweet
seventeen.
“ To return to the Liedertafel : Of the perfor-
mers, Maggie Sterling, in white brocade and
breast-knot of red carnations, held more than her
share both in looks and tunefulness. Debutante
Mabel Martin, in a jerk-tailed white costume,
took her first professional plunge with an air of
stern resolution, proving herself the lucky possessor
of a round, fresh, untrammelled young voice, with
room and to spare for vocal - polisher Armes
Beaumont to go on working his intelligent will
without danger of developing sharp edges. Then
there was tenor Walter Kirby, always more
effective in a smallish room, where you are near
enough to admire the color-blend of his brown
eyes and auburn hair instead of brooding over
those old defects of knee-and-toe deportment. A
girl in the audience was heard accusing Mr. Kirby
of a ‘ milk-oh voice,” but girls were critical that
evening. You see, there were such mobs of them.
Unbroken rows stretched right across the Town
Hall from wall to wall. An extra lucky line had
a male creature at each end. One of these was
frightfully old—somewhere near the ‘seventh-age,’
I guess ; the other a fresh young thing in the very
prime of his teens.
“In the stalls at ‘The Gay Parisienne,’ the
other night, sufferers girded at the wearer of a
fine specimen of the ‘ we-are-seven hat ’ —five
white plumes and two black ones.
“ At a public entertainment the other evening
much notice was lavished on an old lady with
phenomenally thin arms in scant black sleeves,
and curls a fa Christian Adventiste— i.e. , five
smooth oily ringlets meekly parted and snooded
with black velvet. So much for Joan ! Darby, a
well-set-up patriarch, was palpably proud as Punch
of his partner. Hard-by, a nice, comfortable old
thing, plump as a partridge, with a baldish patch
on top and no cap, received far less attention from
‘John Anderson, Aer Jo.’ These studies in human
nature destroyed my childlike faith in a recent
Bulletin paragraph, which quoted somebody or
other to the effect that ‘ no man ever unreservedly
loved a woman with thin arms.’
“ A blind Vic. country girl, at present staying
in Melbourne, goes everywhere, theatre, cricket-
matches, Block, &c., with as much pleasure, appar-
ently, as if she could see. On the Block, perhaps,
she doesn’t miss much by being blind, and pro-
bably at the theatre there has never yet been a big
hat in front of her.
“ One of the Women’s Hospital Committee
(Carlton, Vic.) objected to a candidate for cap and
pinny because she wore spectacles. ‘ They mig t
drop off at a critical moment,’ quoth the wise
woman. Have none of the ladies ever noticed
that Dr. Dunbar Hooper, one of their honorary
medicos, wears a pince-nez? Yet he and his eye-
glass were thought safe enough to attend Lady
Hopetoun on a certain interesting occasion. On
a par with this was the decree which compelled
the wearing of print gowns all the year through
by the last resident woman-doctor, while the men,
resident and honorary alike, wore serge or broad-
cloth at their own sweet will. A coat and trousers
could not possibly convey infection. Oh no!
“ Art is the correct cult of the moment among
the ‘smart set.’ Soulful maidens and youthful
matrons in pairs or trios set up studios, dabble
among oils, perpetrate highly-colored caricatures
of friends good-natured enough to sit to them, ask
all their acquaintances to come and see them and
enjoy themselves immensely on their ‘ at home ’
days, when the clatter of china and tongues and
laughter resounds through the building. Some
girl-artists have work-shop studios and really mean
business. Clever Miss Chapman is making way
as a portrait-pain ter, and has now tenor New-
bury’s counterfeit presentment smiling from the
canvas in a nearly-finished stage. On her walls
she has some well-treated seascapes, but finds
portraits better commission work.
“ Melb. sassiety is smiling on baritone Peachey,
and he has many cards for Naice tea-parties, etc.
Actors covet social notice for biz. reasons, as ‘the
Finn ’ recognise the value of the advt. if the query
about an imported artist ‘Do you meet him out?’
is answered in the aftiruiati ve.
“A male addition to a certain family in am
Australian city was recently the cause of much,
rejoicing, for the two previous children had beern
girls. The boy came a few days before the family
were to shift to another house, and on the last
morning under the old roof pa and ma went down,
to breakfast leaving him snugly asleep in the
nuptial couch. They returned in due course,
and on entering the room—! 1 !—the bed
had completely disappeared, and there wasn’t
a sign of the son-and-heirlet. Then a*
pale, wild-eyed, terror-stricken couple rushed!
frantically through the house, and finally
ran against two men who had been clearing
out some of the rooms, according to removal con-
tract, during the early morn. When father and
mother had gasped out their calamity, the two
men explained that, seeing the room empty, they
had supposed it to be ready for clearance, and had
begun by rolling the bedding, tying it up, and
depositing it in the van outside ! It didn't take
many minutes to recover and unroll that bedding,
and it was accomplished just in time. Then there
was again great rejoicing in that household, even
as at the birth of male twins.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.

The Bulletin’s Xmas Number (ready
December 9) will be as good as any previous
number, and better if possible. All the old favorite
Bulletin writers are represented by picked verse
and stories ; and some new ones this year come
to the front with a bang. Illustrations adorn:'
every page ; and from cover to cover the issue will!
be as original and characteristic and Australian as
it is possible to make it. The price will be six-
pence, as usual; post-free from Bulletin office,
7d. elsewhere than to Queensland, and B|d. to
Queensland. As the supply of past Xmas num-
bers has almost invariably run short, agents who
have not yet sent in their orders are desired to
apply at once in order to avoid disappointment.
The Bulletin purposes printing shortly
the opinions it has received from medical men and
others in regard to the notorious Suffield case.
So far, the essence of these opinions is an exclama-
tion at the astounding quality of the evidence
which in Botany Bay can earn a man ten years’
penal servitude. Meantime, Suffield spends a
merry Xinas behind his bars because—though
offered mercy—he will have justice.
Queensland Government has dropped the
Federal Enabling Bill once more. When Nelson
was last in Sydney he stated that for the purpose
of electing delegates to the next Convention,
Queensland would be divided into three consti-
tuencies. He introduced the Bill with Queensland
as one constituency, which meant, of course, that
the South would nominate all ten delegates, or
thereabouts. The Northern and Central members
demanded the three-electorate system, and Nel-
son, instead of adhering to his pledge, said they
might amend the Bill if they could, but he
wouldn’t support the amendment anyhow. Then
the northern and central crowd, reckoning it)
better to postpone Federation than to have their
share of the Constitution-making delegated
entirely to the Brisbane gang, joined forces with
the anti-Federationists, and the bill was thrown
out by 21 votes against 19. This makes the third
rejection, and the treachery of the Nelson Minis-
try was the cause each time.

In the face of the details so circum-
stantially set forth of the career of the celebrated
Margaret Catch pole (Mrs. Reibey) it is curious to
find her grandson asserting that, as she was
13 at the time of the horse-“ borrowing ” incident,
she was never dealt with by law at all, and that
instead of coming to Botany Bay as a convict she
was sent out by her relatives under care of a
clergyman. But this, be it remembered, was her
own version of the circumstances.
Suffield is still in his living grave, and the
N.S.W. Justice Department is jumping on him
with all its feet to make him fit that grave.
Suffield is a victim not only of Windeyerism, but
of Government by Contradiction. Both N.S.W.
political gangs, the ins and the outs, are interested
m justifying the actions of their respective Minis-
ters of Justice. A pamphlet fully dealing with
this monstrous wrong has just been published.

Ed. Bulletin, —Let me, as a Westralian,
protest against the term “groper” as applied in
your paper to the principals of the recent (Glen
Dhu) flogging-case. The two person? named were
imported a few years ago from Victoria, and so
are not Westralians. The W. A’s. are bad enough,
but please do not say that because a man kills a
couple of niggers in this district he must be a
groper. As you say, if the cliaroe is proved, the
accused ought to hang, but let them please hang
as Victorians and not as gropers.— Leg Iron.
Brisbane (Q.) Worker gives publicity to a
number of rumors that have long been bandied
about in whispers all over Queensland, in an article
headed —“ Is Drury dead ? ” The Bulletin here-
with gives an authentic anecdote, that has not
yet found its way into print :—The Brisbane
corpse-planter who buried Drury, being chaffed
by a friend on this ever-living scandal, said—
“ Why, I put him in his coffin myself. D’you
think I wouldn't know my own banker, sir ? Yes,
sir; Drury screwed a lot of people up, and I
screwed him down!"

When rumors first got out
about the silver-finds at Broken
Hill, a well-known Western squat-
ter, who thought of investing,
happened to mention the matter to
a Yankee stranger whom he was
entertaining for the night, where-
upon the other, announcing that
lih was a “silver-expert,” advised
him instead to pitch his sovereigns
into the adjoining lake. “Then
you’ll know where they are, at all
events, and perhaps even get some
of them back —which is more than
will happen if you put them in
Broken Hill ! ” And the squatter
allowed himself to be thus choked-
off becoming one of the original
shareholders in the Proprietary
mine at <£l6o a share. One of
these original shares fell to the lot
of a young Englishman who had
become engaged to a girl with
.£lO,OOO, and had come out to
Australia to try to make an
equivalent amount before fulfilling
the engagement. After trying all
sorts of things, he took to book-
making, and, when on his way to
Silverton races, met a man who
wanted to sell the share in ques-
tion. The Englishman proposed
to decide by a game of cards
whether he should give £l6O for
the share or £l2O. He won, and
afterwards sold the share for
£60,000 to a person who is now a
millionaire.
