Recent mention of Dr. Spiridion Can-
diottis —“ Candiottis the Greek ” —recalls a Rock-
hampton incident of 30 years ago. A symposium
of the “card-push” at Johnny Ward’s “Criterion”
ended in a free fight, in the course of which
“ Candy,” having floored his particular
antagonist with a stick, put the bottom
rung of a chair across the man’s neck
and sat on the chair—lCst. of him
till the poor devil was apparently
choked to death. They had hard
work to bring him round, and
when the doctor was afterwards
fined <£■) over the affair he said :
“ When a man has to fork out
a fiver for defending his life,
it’s time he cleared out of
Rockhampton ! ” And he
cleared accordingly.
Saul Samuel, when he
first became a N.S.W.
Minister of the Crown,
rehearsed an important
speech with great care,
and under the criticism
of a gentleman who
whispered the fact abroad.
Samuel, it appeared, in
order to perfect his pos-
tures and gestures, spoke
his speech in front of a
large cheval glass. When
he came to deliver the
oration, he got on very
well for a time ; then he
halted and blundered.
“Bring in the looking-
glass ! ” snuffled Jack Robertson, “ the honor-
able member wants to brush up his memory.” The
jibe, instead of baulking Samuel, pulled him
together, and he went on to the end without
further pause or confusion.

John Williamson, Superintendent of the
province of Auckland in 1859, had a weakness for
good liquor, and once at a dinner-party at Govern-
ment House—in Gov. Gore Browne’s time —when
the ladies arose to leave the gentlemen to their
wine, Mrs. Williamson kept her seat till she had
to be reminded that Mrs. Browne had retired.
“Aweel!” she said, “may be sae. Her man
diesna need her. But ma Jock diesna sit here
wi’oot me tae keep tally o’ his stoups. Hoot! he
wad be that fou in twa minutes, he wadna be able
tae tell the Governor frae the Superintendent—
and that’s himsel’. Gang awa’, ladies. I’ll joost
keep ma een on Wulliamson.” And she did.
When the benevolent Capt-. Machonochie
ruled at Norfolk Island, the convicts took ad-
vantage of his leniency, and, among other things,
they stole his choicest ducks, which were under
the care of a half-witted fellow known as “ Stut-
tering Billy.” Every other day or so, Billy came
back with a diminished charge, till the captain at
last got “ mad,” and gave Billy half-a-dozen, and
Billy asserted that they “ floo-o-o-ed away.” He
was tied up again, but still the ducks went till
none remained. “The blanky d-u-cks, sir,” re-
ported Billy, “fell in-in-ter th’ cr cr-eek, and wa-s
dr-dr tnvnd-ed.” At which the captain was so
tickled that he let Billy off his usual allowance.
Billy lived to be “ respectable ” in Hobart, and to
be known as “ the duck drowner.”
Capt. Beckham, police magistrate at Auck-
land (M.L.), before the last war, had his court at
the upper end of Queen-street, and, to get there,
had to pass the shop of a butcher, whose taste
for dog-fights and cock-fights had frequently
brought him before the P.M., with unpleasant
results. The butcher cast about for revenge
until he hit upon the idea of calling a bull-
pup “ Beckham,” and trained it to go to the
other side of the road and sit there, so that,
when Beckham came along, the butcher would
open out: “ Beckham ! Oh, you son of a beast !
There you are, loafin’ around as usual. Come
here, you ugly, stoopid, hignorant himposter ! ”
And, when the P.M. had passed along, boiling
over, the butcher would whistle up his pup and
shout half-a-pound of steak for him. But, the
next time the butcher was run-in, he got six
months ; and when he came out, he found that his
wife had run away with a smallgoods-man, and
the shop was shut up. He enlisted, and was shot
at Waikau. A novel in a nutshell.
The recent death of Dr. Newfield Lucas in
Adelaide revives the old tragedy of the St. Kilda
Road Barracks (Melb.) of 1857. The 40th regt.
was stationed there at the time, and among the
young officers were Lucas, then a lieutenant, Sur-
geon Macauley, Ensign Veith and another ensign
—a wild Irishman named Pennyfather. Penny-
father had boasted of his immunity from certain
trouble that most young men fall into, so, when
he himself did join the great majority, he was
subjected to much chaff. One afternoon, the re-
marks of his fellow officers turned him into a
raging lunatic, and, drawing a revolver, he shot
Macauley dead, sent a bullet through Veith’s
cheeks, lodged a third in Lucas’s jaw and a fourth
in his own brain. At the inquest a verdict of
suicide whilst of unsound mind was returned, and
both murdered and murderer were buried in the
present Melb. cemetery, where a publicly-sub-
scribed monument stands over them. Veith
returned to England, and, after years, Lucas
recovered (the bullet still remained somewhere
beneath his tongue) and joined the military forces
in Adelaide. Some years ago, the bullet came
out of his jaw, but a cancer formed and killed him
—4O years after the injury was inflicted.

The squatter king, M'Caughey, holds
3,000,000 acres. One trifle of 45,000 acres in
Riverina he calls a stud-farm. He has a million
acres near Hughenden, Q., and two huge stations on
the Darling, Tooralie and Dunlop. During last
drought he is said to have lost 1-00,000 sheep and
lambs. Shears a million sheep, the world’s re-
cord. Truly a mammoth among mutton-raisers.
A most important event is when M‘Caughey “gets
his wool off.”
Melb. : “ Says Soft goods Beath to Soft-
goods Sargood about half a year ago : ‘ I hope you
won’t be away enjoying yourself « hen your fire
comes off.’ The Sargood business was founded
by the military and millinery Sargood’s father,
but the Sargood of to-day wrought it up,
while the title was Sargood and Son. It was
the first Melbourne house in any line to go
for Dunedin (M.L.) in a big way. Sargood
capers about in such an absurd fashion as a
military General Bombastes, and is such a
thoroughly commonplace politician, that his
genuine ability is overlooked though, indeed,
there is no occasion why such an eminent business
man and money-maker should care to be recog-
nised.”
T.D. : “7fe the contention that Supreme
Court Judges are badly paid in comparison with
other successful barristers. At the very most only
three Sydney barristers would top Chief Justice
Darley’s screw (.£350 ) —and Salomons, who pro-
bably leads the lot, was ready enough to take the
Chief - Justiceship when chance offered. The
ordinary Judge sits (say) six hours a day, and has
pretty well done with it; the busy Q.C. plods
and bustles double those hours, and to keep it up
must take his annua jaunt to Eurojie—or Japan.
When a Judge retires, he has a thumping pension
at hand, but fees cease when a barrister throws
up the sponge. The Judge pays no office rent,
keeps no clerks—has every cent, for his own
pocket.”

despairing persuader promised to refund the money paid
for the ticket if he did not laugh or cry during the lec-
ture. On the platiorm the lecturer came, pale but deter-
mined. There the young man sat, cold as a frog, close as
an oyster. The lecturer began, proceeded, told one of his
best stories. No sign. Told a better story. The corners
of the young man’s mouth twitched. Then he (Smith)
said, “ Up Guards and at them,” and gave his most
humorous sketch. The enemy wavered, broke, fled, and
Waterloo was won. Then he grew pathetic, tender.
Hist! What was that? Plash, plash! tinkle, tinkle!
Tub tub—tu-hoo -boo—boo ; that callous, cold-hearted
young man wept. Wept ? And it sounded like “ Plash,
plash ! tinkle, tinkle ! tub, tub, tub ; hoo boo hoo.”
Heavens—what an ear Smith must have.' Ar.d
what a ghastly old chestnut !
The recent Wentworth versus Single, Emu
Plains land case, tried before N.S.W. Chief Jus-
tice Darley, sent the jury back to 1838. Deeds of
land were then favorite gifts of those in power.
But Wentworth met one Governor—Gipps—who
inflicted a knock-out on his land-acquisition
ambition, or “ the great Tory,” as Parkes used to
call him, would have died proprietor of most of
Australia. Wentworth negotiated with the Maori
chiefs of his day for the whole Middle Island and
a great slice of the North Island, and succeeded in
having the properties conveyed to himself and his
associates in due legal form. But Gipps declared
the transaction void, on the ground that the
Caucasian had simply wheedled the brown man.
Wentworth, who was a fighter and had duly-exe-
cuted deeds at his back, was eventually beaten by
the sheer exercise of authority on the part of
Gipps—one illustration of the occasional useful-
ness of a benevolent despot.

Re the great Melbourne Flinders-lane fire,
where £1,000,000 worth of property went off with a pop
and a “whoosh,” a local official of the North British
and Mercantile remarks loftily that he “hopes it will put
an end to all talk about State or municipal fire insurance.”
Then he says: “Suppose a municipal insurance office
were in existence in Melbourne, with a monopoly of busi-
ness, how could it meet claims amounting to over half-a-
million sterling ? Even if business was done by the State,
the losses could only be met by means of a fresh loan."
By way of a wildly original observation, this paper sug-
gests that probably the State Fire Insurance Department
would meet its losses out of premiums just as the North
British and Mercantile does. At least, the M.L. State Life
Department does that—just the way the N.B. and M.
Life Department does.
• * * •
Two competing Brunswick (Vic.) grocers started
cutting rates on potatoes recently, and the prices soon
came down almost to zero. Still the fighters kept at it,
chuckling to themselves that the other man couldn’t hold
out much longer—until they both found that every other
grocer in the district was buying his tubers anonymously
from one or other of the contestants. Which recalls how
Jay Gould beat the opposition railroad line that tried to
knock him out by offering to carry cattle 300 miles at 3d.
a head. Jay didn’t trouble about railroad business after
that offer. He went in for cattle-dealing, and ran several
hundred thousand beasts over the other line before its
bosses “tumbled.”

* * * •
An awful slump has fallen on most of the Maori-
land mines passed on to Bull and co. during the last two
years. Already several sales per sheriff have occurred.
Representatives from London have arrived and were so
appalled at the prospect that immediate orders to shut
down were given. The salter and lying expert put in
some good work ; and, amid the number of shows floated
and for which ample working capital was furnished, it is
declared that there are only two or three displaying any
prospect of returning the money spent on them.
The gentlemen recently mulct in damages
for improperly investing the funds in the estate
for which they were trustees, lost no time in tak-
ing it out (with a vengeance) of the attorney who
engineered the action against them. _ By the way,
few gents one down south are sufficiently solvent
to dare risk the result to themselves of a success-
ful action of this nature.
• * • *
Only two of the many Suburban Melb.
Tram Co.s floated privately since the boom are
earning expenses. One has been abandoned for
five years, another was taken over and stopped by
its bank a month ago, and still another is on the
point of haying the brakes applied by its “ financial
supporters.”

Hess, in the South African Critic, on
Labouchere : “ I have branded and now again brand
Mr. Labouchere, M.P., as a heartless contemptible
scoundrel, who, for his own personal pecuniary
gain, was, during the years 1874-6-0, engaged (1)
in a deliberate fraud on the pockets of the public ;
(2) in a systematic attempt to plunder the in-
vestor and shareholder; (3) in a fraudulent con-
spiracy to swindle them; and (4) in persistent
attacks which resulted in their ruin upon four men—
now deceased —each of whom was, compared to
him, the very soul of honor and integrity.” And
now it is cabled that Labouchere has injuncted Hess
from publishing certain letters written by him (L.)
to the late George Augustus Sala.
* • • *
Re Lucknow strike. There is no doubt
that gold-stealing on a large scale has gone on in
the Luoknow mines for many years (writes a Bul-
lktin correspondent). Twenty or thirty years ago
the miners used to invent all sorts of methods for
concealing the gold, so that they could get out of
the mine without being caught. One long-suc-
cessful dodge was to bore an auger-hole 3 or 4in.
deep in the end of a pick-handle and fill it up with
mud. Then, in the mine, when the miner had got
what gold he could “shake,” he had only to knock
out the mud, shove the gold in, and fill it up with
mud again. The thieves used to have trouble in
getting rid of the gold, the only buyers being a
few little local storekeepers, who, knowing that it
was stolen, would only give ridiculously small
prices—often as low as 6s. an oz. —for the purest
gold in Australia. The buyers abovementioned,
when sending the stolen gold away for sale else-
where, used to conceal it in different ways. One was
by putting a good-sized package of it in a barrel of
tallow.

Hermann Aron, German Imperial Council-
lor of State, who describes himself as probably the
largest individual holder of Westralian mining stocks,
writing to Modest Maryanski, saying that all German
investors have to thank M. M. for arresting the panio
that had set in by giving them a true conception of the
future of Kalgoorlie :—“We Germans know well the true
state of affairs. We remember only too well the lying
prospectuses issued by dishonest boomsters, filled with
glowing reports from so-called mining engineers. Nothing
can prevent these men, who are well-known, from being
roasted in the fire of hell—of this world’s contempt.
The non-discerning publio steams these in one compre-
hensive kettle, but the few of us who have better oppor-
tunities of judging know that there are still a few
mining engineers capable and honest. The time of lies
and deception is past. We now go carefully through
ever}' prospectus, report, &o. If such names as yours
and others like yours are appended the shares will be
subscribed, but otherwise German investors will pitoh
each prospectus into the waste-paper basket.”
• * * *
“ The only way to rely on making good miners,
and therefore safe miners, is to catch them young, and
train them.” Late statistics from the United States sup-
port this view. Colorado figures for ’96 show 6'96 fatal
accidents per 1000 men employed underground ; for the
same year, the United Kingdom figures showed 171 per
thousand. In the States, as in Australia, a pretty large
percentage of miners, especially in the metal-mines, are
comparative new-chums at the game ; while in the U.K.
the son is a miner because the father was. Still, the
average N.S.W. rate is under 3 per 1000.
* • * *
A Melb. M.P. tells of sending a party of two
superior “ experienced ” miners out to work a small show
he had in a tunnel in the Bruthen district, and, going to
see them a fortnight later, found they had thrown all the
quartz down the tip and were saving the yellow reef for
treatment. Furthermore, they had transformed the
tunnel into a sort of quarry by the reckless use of dyna-
mite.

* * * *
The Vic. Mines Amendment Act is in opera-
tion, and Bendigo is feeling it pinch, as companies all
round are refusing to let tributes under it. The co.’3
bugbear is the sustenance or, as it is locally called, the
half-wages clause, while there is some grumbling on both
sides because each crushing is to stand on its own bottom.
It is not the little mines only that have placed much reli-
ance on tributors ; the New Chum Consol, Specimen Hill,
and Hustler’s Reef, for instance, have been largely sup-
ported hitherto by them. And on the whole the tributors
have been the most experienced workmen, too. How-
ever, local opinion seems to be that when the conditions
are better understood things will go on much as usual.
* * * *
_ Stead, in his Klondyke article, and others, men-
tion that the Alaskan gold discoveries aren’t unexpected,
seeing that the Russian Govt, has long successfully worked
gold-mines in Siberia. Worked them? Rather. Cut, as
the writers say, they don’t boom them. Russia gets out
something like five millions sterling of gold annually, of
which three and a-half millions are got from Siberian and
other Asiatic territory. The increase in this production,
and the apparent certainty of continuing it, as a result of
the Trans-Siberian railway and otherwise, had a great deal
to do in leading Russia to lately throw over silver in favor
of a gold currency. French scientific papers have a good
deal to say about Russian gold-mines; English papers,
practically nothing.
* * » *
“ There is no part of Australia where I would
sooner go to look for ‘a turn-up’ than the M‘Donnell
Ranges,” writes a Bullktin correspondent. “There I
saw some beautiful slate country (albeit somewhat dis-
turbed, but then, the richest pockets of many minerals
occur in ‘ disturbed ’ country), and there are innumerable
quartz and ironstone outcrops that have never been
tapped. The want of water, as usual, would be a draw-
back, but it could be overcome by making huge dams.
And thatcountry, unlike W .A.,would hold water. What is
chiefly prospected for there is mica, which occurs in an
outcrop of white rock, ‘blown out’ of ordinary granite ;
best claims found outcropping in garnetiferous granite.
The mica formation is, no doubt, merely an abnormal
granite (mica, felspar, and quartz in small particles, being
granite’s component parts). It is unsatisfactory stuff to
work. You’ve just to mine away everything ; and, after
quarrying out hundreds of tons of rock, very often dis-
cover no mica. There are no known indications by which
you can judge the likely or any quantity an outcrop holds.
The slabs of mica that go to make a solid block cross and
run into one another, and, as it is necessary to
out the marketable sheets without a flaw, the loss
is about 90 per cent. Clear and spotted mica of
three colours (preen, brown, and white) is found. It
is split to the thickness of Jin. and cut square. The
size varies from 3*3 inches to 20x20, and mica is worth
from 2s. to 265. per lb. A couple of years ago the world’s
output was less than 50 tons. It is a non-conductor of
heat and electricity. The M'Donnell Ranges have never
been prospected to any extent. A few dippers amonp
them raise a couple of hundred ounces cold every year.
They live from hand-to-mouth, and are a tough lot of
pioneers. It is hard country to travel about in. There is
known to be gold, galena, stream tin, mica, cinnabar, and
tons of garnets there. It was thought the garnets were
rubies till an opinion from Streeter, the London expert,
squashed that idea. Some of the garnets cut into pretty
gems. You can see any amount in the windows of Svd -
ney jewellers, generally set with Queensland opals, the
Burmese ruby is a bright scarlet color, those found in
“ The Territory ” are claret-colored. They withstand any
test for hardness, but being " off color ” damns them.
• * * V
Mr. Rickard, the State Geologist of Colo-
rado, does not agree with Maryanski in compar-
ing Kalgoorlie with Cripple Creek. He says no two
regions could be more dissimilar in their geological
structure. Kalgoorlie reminded him more of certain parts
of N. Carolina and W. Ontario. The ore of the deeper
levels of the Kalgoorlie mines would probably he more
bunchy in character, the gold occurrence more sporadic,
making in short shoots and pockets of very rich ore
rather than evenly distributed along the strike of the
lode. The cost of exploration will he much greater in the
hard rock which lies below the zone of oxidation.
Colorado is the only part of the world where telluride ores
have been handled in a large way, and there they have
been usually accepted as ari incidental portion of smelter-
treatment — i.e., they have been absorbed by the smelters
of Denver, Pueblo, and Leadville as part of a large lead
and copper-smelting industry. The Cripple Creek mills
use both oyanidation and chlorination. Owing to the
presence of a high percentage of carbonate of iime and
magnesia in Kalgoorlie pres, chlorination was impractic-
able. Tests at Denver with ore from the Boulder Main
reef demonstrated the entire success of cyanidation after
the ore had been roasted. Dry-crushing had been found
to deliver the ore in a condition better suited for leaching
than wet-pulverisation. There, where the water difficulty
threatens to become aggravated, there should be no hesi-
tation in crushing the ores dry.

Kalgoorlie and Boulder City are perpetually em-
broiled. The latter—the younger'rival hasn’t up to date
got the ear of the Govt., and has practically no public
conveniences beyond post and telegraph offices has not,
even a savings-bank or M.0.0.-office. Moreover, the
banks are afraid to move, for fear of offending Kalgoorlie
customers, and so, for their own protection, have agreed
not to open at the Boulder. Wherefore the Boulder man
spends much of his time cursing everybody but himself;
and “ Smiler ” Hales daily screeches against Creation and
John Forrest.
Five years work of the Miners’ Association in
Vic. Accident pay, £32,972; death allowance (to
widows, &c.) £9404 ; Watson sustentation fund (for worn-
out miners, and confined to Bendigo), £5136; and other
“ benefits ” brings the total to £55,530.

The chief business of every syndicate or co.
working a copper-show in N.S.W. seems to consist of
shrieking for a water-jacket. “If we only had a water-
jacket!” A start was made with a little one recently.
When in full blast the furnace was tapped, and men stood
around with balls of clay. The moment the slag-pot was
full these were thrown at the tap-hole. Too often they
were fired wide, and slag and matter escaped. At last a
man who had seen a blast furnace turned up, and sug-
gested that if these clay balls were placed at the end of
an iron bar, the holes could be stopped without trouble.
Thus, in that small way, he has made a reputation which
in that district, time will not efface.
MY LADY NICOTINE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
she was born (so runs the rumour)
In the dusky isles of balm,
In the Southland she was cradled,
And she grew beside the palm ;
She’s enwrapt in soft aromas,
And a pair of blackest een
Dance beneath her curls of ebon—
Dainty Lady Nicotine!
She’s the dearest, deft beguiler
(Though her voice is wondrous low),
And for him who woos her duly
She can softly blush and glow ;
And her breath hath workings potent ;
For a wily witch (I ween )
Lurks within the sable vesture
Of My Lady Nicotine.
And for him who seeks a goddess
She hath altar—she hath shrine
Where her vot’ries ever tend her
With the sacred fire divine;
And for him who seeks a mistress
She's a kindly lissome Queen
For she loves the lips full-hewrded,
Does My Lady Nicotine.
And for him who seeketh Wisdom
She hath many a musing sage,
And a crown of calm that passeth
All the peace of wrinkled Age;
And for him who lives a-drearning
She hath many a Deep serene—
Many a Deep o’ Visions teeming
Hath My Lady Nicotine.
And when Lights o’ Love have left us,
When their siren song is sung,
Still she lingers softly near us
Though the knell of Youth is rung;
Still her charms have power to please us
Though our eyes have lost their sheen,
And her kindly kisses soothe us—
Gentle Lady Nicotine!
Brisbane.
P.LUFTIN.


The varieties of pastoral by-prod acts are
not known to everyone. Fleshings, glue pieces
[hide remnants], sinews, weazands, blood, horse
and cow hair, hoofs, horns, and bones of all kinds
are included. It is interesting to see the diversi-
fied lots of these, along with the better-known
pastoral and other produce, weekly put through
by Pitt, Son and Badgery, Ltd., who are very
successful in this commerce.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

W. J. G. ELLIOTT, a veteran Australian cyclist.
Photo., Newman.
The Bulletin this week prints a portrait of
W. J. O. Elliott, a unique figure in Australasian—if not in
the world’s—cycling. As co-manager in the largest of
Australasian cycling firms, he works very often from 8
a.m. until II p.m.; yet, as a professional cyclist upon the
track, he is one of the most prominent racing men of the
day. Though he has been training for 20 years, and is
now married and labors under the handicap of a big
salary, he seems to suffer from a chronic ebullition of
energy. He is never content unless working in his office
with his coat off, or whizsing round the track at a 1.50
gait, or dancing at Govt. House, or pushing a fat
woman and her wheel up-hill (and likewise assist-
ing the daughter), or doing some such- thing calcu-
lated to break a man hopelessly down. His elder
brother did, in fact, kill himself by over-exertion
on the track, and W. J. 0. Elliott himself was warned, by
a Melb. doctor, years ago, to quit the excessive strain of
athletics. But Elliott has this rare gift which keeps him
together-he can lie down at any minute of the day or
night, on bed or board or solid stone, and instantly fall
asleep. On the day he won the last Sydney Wheel Race,
with its prize of £2OO-the largest ever yet won by a
cyclist in Sydney—he slept for two hours just prior to the
race. Though the athlete is in his blood, bones and brain,
he has none of the fads and foibles so popular with
athletes. He isn’t afraid of catching 1 cold, he eat* and
drinks what he likes, but he won’t touch alcohol or
tobacco. It is remarkable that this iron-nerved man
can be made absolutely groggy with two or three
whiffs of a cigar. Elliott is not a big man nor
an over-muscular one ; he succeeds in business and on the
'track by his cool temperament, his judgment, and hie
■adaptability. Every time he races he insures himself for
.'£looo, with so much per week in case of incapacitation by
■specific injury. Probably, he will arrange his own f’une-
iral when the time arrives, unless the subject bores him,
in which case, doubtless, he will drop off to sleep before-
hand, to avoid worry. He won the first Austral, the
•Melb. Oup of cycling ; has been favorite for the last two
Australs, and will compete again this time with a 60yds.
handicap. He has had all athletics bad—has been nearly
killed at swimming, boxing, shooting, football, and
•cycling. And it is rather a pity for him that his surplus
energy cannot be bottled up, and distributed among his
iflrm’s branches.
Is it True?
We are used to “tall yarns” from “Yankee-
Iland,” and, in fact, can hardly pick up an Ameri-
can newspaper without seeing an article referring
to some new improbable invention ; a recent dis-
covery ; or some wonderful feat, which, if true,
would command our wonder, astonishment, and
praise. While we would not reflect upon the
veracity of our American cousins, we must in all
■conscience, and in justice to ourselves and readers,
accept many of these statements with a grain or
two of allowance, even if we credit their incon-
■gruity to inaccuracy of cables or typographical
■errors. When we receive a startling statement,
however, of facts occurring, or a discovery made,
in Australia, verified by one of our own country-
men of professional and social standing, we have
no hesitancy in accepting it as “ true.” The
latest and one of the most interesting letters from
a prominent and well-known Australian, Mr. J.
M‘Donald, the well-known artist and sketched
we know represents the truth, and is of personal
interest to all. He writes from Universal Cham-
bers, 326 Collins St., Melbourne, 19/7/97 i
“ About fourteen years ago, when on a visit bo
nay father’s home in Otago, N.Z., I learned that
;a neighbouring farmer’s wife was seriously ill with
Bright’s disease, and that there was little hope of
her recovery. I informed my mother of a remark
made to a friend by a London doctor to the effect
that Warner’s Safe Cure was one of the best
patent medicines for Bright’s disease then in the
market. The information was conveyed to the
patient, who at once commenced taking the Safe
Cure. The result was most gratifying, and three
years ago when again in New Zealand I had the
pleasure of meeting this lady who was then ap-
parently in excellent health. The following is an
experience of my own. A few years ago I suf-
fered severely from ulcerated mouth and throat
occasioned by carelessly moistening my finger on
my lips when counting bank notes. After using
a mixture made up by a chemist, which proved
useless, Warner’s Safe Cure obtained speedy .re-
lief, and ultimately got rid of the trouble.”

UP TO HIS NECK IN IT.


PUT TO THE TEST
A Well-Known Sydney Journalist Makes Inquiries,
AND TELLS THE STORY TO “ BULLETIN ” READERS.
u npHOUSANDS of weary feet! We see them daily, dragging along, to and fro, pathetic
J- proof of the worn-out feeling in the bodies that possess them. Thousands of
weary feet, and thousands of correspondingly weary hearts, they go on daily, victims of
one or other of the many forms of General Debility.” . , .
It was the brief introduction to an article in one of the current magazines, and it set a
press-representative thinking. He had on his office-table, among other things, an adver-
tisement setting out the wonderful restorative properties of a well-known medicine as testi-
fied to by Mr. W. H. Cole, late headmaster of Petersham 0. of E. School, and it set him
thinking : “ If this preparation for restoring wasted energy be all that they claim tor it,
how is it that these weary feet go on in their downward course 1 How is it that they do
not seize the chance provided to recover their lost elasticity, and so grow young again . I
was a very natural question for the pressman to put to himself, and it was an equa y
natural answer that immediately suggested itself : “ Because they do not know, because
they do not believe, because they have been deceived so often they hardly care to exercise
their discrimination further.” Suddenly the idea presented itself : How if I should tes
the genuineness or otherwise of this testimony ; how if I should investigate it, and, its
honesty being established, give it the impress of my newspaper, and thereby prove a bene-
faCtC Inspired thus with this most praiseworthy desire, the reporter called the other day at
Roslyn, Frazer-road, Lewisham, where Mr. Cole lives, and, being accorded the privilege o
an interview, delicately introduced the object of his visit, and requested to be told 1 a
that had been said of that gentleman’s wonderful restoration to health and strength were
“ Perfectly so,” said Mr. Cole, a fine, scholarly-looking man, anywhere in the fifties.
“ Absolutely true. I tell you I was run down—completely done up, in fact and was qui e
unfitted to go about my customary duties.”
“ But may not that have been but a passing fit of ‘ out of sorts ?
“ Don’t interrupt, sir. Not at all. I tell you I was run down to a condition of the
most extreme weakness. I slept badly, I ate badly ; I was exhausted when I retired at
night, and utterly prostrated within a few hours of my in the morning.
“ You evidently had got into a bad state of health, then 1 ” . .
“ I had ; and everybody noticed it. I was as weak as could be—nervous and fretful to
a degree—and, what is more, I got worse with each succeeding day.”
“ Why didn’t you see a doctor, Mr. Cole ? ”
“ I’ll tell you what I did, sir. I got so bad at last I started taking patent medicines-
pills and tonics and things—and perhaps in that you would think I was very foolish.”
The reporter smiled.
“ Well, I was both foolish and wise. Foolish, because I tried nearly every tonic on
the market without getting the slightest relief ; and wise, because I didn’t give up my search
for a cure even when disgusted.”
“ Wise, indeed, if you found one, sir.”
“ You shall see. I repeat I had tried almost every tonic on the market, and only to be
disappinted ; but there was one I hadn’t tried, and when at last, acting on (he advice of a
friend, I turned to that, I assure you I had almost lost hope.
“ And that last one cured you 1 ”
“It did, completely ; and never shall I fail to acknowledge it—it saved my going to
pieces.
“ You certainly look the picture of health now, Mr. Cole.”
“ J am 10 years younger as the result of four bottles,” said the relator, stretching his
arms out. “My appetite is good, my nerves are strong, and, I believe,” smilingly, “ that
I would not come off second best even in a tussle with you, who look as ruddy as anyone
could desire.”
“ The weary thousands, then, have only to apply to you, sir, to be told the way to health
and happiness ? ” .
Mr. Cole became serious. “ I would be ungrateful were I not to do whatever lies in
my power to help others.”
“And the name of this wonderful restorative ? ”
“Is Minerva Tonic, sir no other M~I —N E —R — V~A.’’
Spelling out which classical name this most courteous gentleman extended his hand to
wish the reporter good-bye ; and the latter, returning the parting salutation, left in the
satisfaction that he had gained such information, as, being printed, would act upon
thousands, “ Weary, care-oppressed hearts, weary, care-furrowed faces, a service might
be done to sufferers that should not prove the least of a journalist’s claim to a future
reward.”
And with that object this article is printed.
Minerva Tonic is on sale everywhere, and everyone should insist upon getting it.

can he Inspected at the Sydney Depot.

POLITICAL POINTS.
If Premier Reid gets his way, the only un-
stamped thing in N.S. Wales will be the mailed
N.S.W. newspaper.
“A noo Guv’ment within a month !” is
what some of the Brisbane (Q.) political prophets
are tearing around to impart.
Rumoured that Reid’s long - forgotten
N.S.W. State Bank scheme may be heard of next
session—just in time to let the Opposition put
both feet on it before the general election.
Native Indian discontent at the cost of
frontier wars, in which the people have no direct
interest, is one of the pressing anxieties of the
Viceroy’s Government. The Indian debt exceeds
JE200,000,000, and the present war will add heavily
to it.
Nelson Govt. (Q.) has dropped M‘II wraith
like a hot potato. Admitted that he was torrid
to hold, would M‘llwraith have dropped a mate
under similar circumstances? Not much. He’d
have fought on his behalf like a cornered rat till
be was “ killed dead.”
Henniker Heaton ridicules the idea of
seven distinct Agents-General for Australasia.
One general representative, he thinks, would quite
fill the bill. “ The people at present occupying
these positions are for the most part simply titles
hunters.” Et tu, Brute?
Premier Reid boasts that he takes his
politics from “ people he meets, and not from
books.” Hence such unconscious theatrical con-
tradictions as a probate-tax, graduated and with-
out exceptions, and his professed admiration of a
land-tax with exemptions and without gradua-
tion.
When Q. Parliament has pensioned off the
ancient Bully Palmer, and Premier Nelson, P.C.,
&c., has taken Palmer’s chair as President of the
Leg. Council, then look mi for a thermal geyser
troubled by deep party -quakes. It looks as if any
week might see an election in Queensland. If
Nelson is party-wise, he will postpone pen sioning
Palmer till next year.
Premier Reid is trying an ingeni-
ous method to keep out the British nigger
and Chinaman, the Imperial Govern-
ment having refused to permit his ex-
clusion simply because he is a nigger or a
Chinaman. The coffee-colored person is to
personally write out and sign, in some European
language, an application to be exempted from the
Operation of the Act. If he can’t do that, clause
111. of the Act will eject him on his ear. The
same Act also shuts out paupers, idiots, criminals,
Persons suffering from certain diseases, and
'lossies, so it looks sufficiently comprehen-
sive. As it doesn’t openly tread on the corn
of the British nigger, but gets there by a
circuitous route, it seems to be understood
that the Imperial Government will let it
go through in a moment of abstraction.
According to Premier Nelson,
M‘llwraith “came to Queensland with
.£60,000 or £70,000. He has now been
down on a bed of sickness for four
years, and is penniless, bed-ridden, and
unable to write.” Then Nelson ap-
pealed to the Labor-party to stop kick-
ing the old man who is down. Yet re-
cent reports have it that MTlwra.lh
is looking rather well and living in com-
fort and apparent affluence ; and even a
penniless man has no right to get away
with £300,000 or so belonging to a set
of shareholders who are now, on the
whole, a great deal more down
than he is, and some of whom are
left without a bed to be bed-
ridden upon. And to people
who remember the masterful
roaring lion that MTI wraith
was when he was up, this
wail for pity sounds in-
congruous.
N. : ‘‘ The boss fools of earth are the
soldiers and the politicians. Graham Berry can-
not get a miserable pension of <£3oo a year, but I
Can point ton. Vic. civil servant, under 50, turned
loose with a pension of <£4oo a year. No wonder
M’sP. envy the Parliament-house messengers, who
heartily despise the members. Berry must be
referred to the Charities Commission or Charity
Organisation Society. I pointed out in these
Columns, months ago, how the Tories were sup-
t>orting his pension and the Liberals opposing.”
Humors of Maoriland Parliament. The
ancient Saunders, member for Selwyn, once char-
acterised a Bill introduced by Reeves as a resur-
rection. “Evidently, it must be,” retorted
Reeves; “it’s brought you up.” Seddon to
Rirani (4ft nothing): “Thehon. member’s imagi-
nation is out of proportion to his size.” Pirani to
Seddon (18st.): “The Premier’s imagination is
not out of proportion to his size.”
That Canadian “loyalist ” Wilfrid Laurier,
whom the Imperial press took so eagerly to its
flat, dry paper bosom, turns out to. be not nearly
so loyal as he looked. One of his most recent
public utterances : “ Canada has passed the period
of pupilage, and has reached that stage when
nationhood is desirable.”
Gregory, M.L.C., is mortally affrighted
that the latest Q. Elections Bill, in shortening the
residence qualification to three months, will
enable a merely residential elector to get his
name on more than one roll just about election
time. But the old man wouldn’t object if Pro-
perty got its name on 40 rolls.
In a return of expenses laid on the tables
of the S. A. Parliament recently, there was a
charge of 13s. for the hire of a cab in which certain
Ministers went to the funeral of Mrs. Catt (wife
of the Chairman of Committees) and 10s. for
another cab which took them to the funeral of the
late Commissioner of Police.
Any Government which employs the Public
Works Committee, at huge expense, to investi-
gate and report on such a proposed work as, say,
Sydney City Railway Extension, and then hangs
the report up unceremoniously, is guilty of such
utterly reckless waste of public money that a vote
of censure could be properly tabled against it.
Australian Federation will much reduce
the number of Imperial decorations given to Aus-
tralian politicians. Canadians visiting this coun-
try are always astonished at the thickness with
which knighthoods are scattered round in the
separate provinces. The reason, of course, is that
at present each is a complete self-governing entity.
The Westralian Leg. Assembly, by a
majority of nearly 2 to 1, pronounces in favor of
payment of members of both Houses. This is as it
should be. The Democracy which pays one
branch of a Legislature and treats the other as an
honorary institution spells self-confusion. Also,
to pay the Upper House would effectually de-
mocratise it.
A provision of the new Yic. Probate Bill
is that banks are to furnish periodical lists of all
deposits and balances which have been unclaimed
for seven years, so that the executors of dead
depositors may have a chance to hear about them.
But why seven years, in the name of all that is
wonderful '! If'Smith dies in 1897 leaving £lOOO
in the bank, and there is no record of it among
his papers, and his heirs have no idea of its exis-
tence,.they simply deposit Smith in the pleasant
bone-yard, and the bank says nothing but keeps
the money. That is the old way. The proposed
new way is that the bank will have to reluctantly
mention the matter in 1904, when Smith’s heirs
and executors have probably vanished to the far
ends of the earth—and, after that, it will presum-
ably keep the money in the old way. Why
shouldn’t the State annex unclaimed balances?

Maoriland’s fresh issue of postage-stamps
runs away from the eternal Queen’s head ; and
South Australia, too, in the new halfpenny will
adopt a local design.
Labor-leader Glassey, with tears in both
eyes, and sobbing determination in his voice, told
Q. Assembly that he could not hold guiltless of a
knowledge of nefarious Q-N. Bank transactions
Chief Justice Griffith-his old friend his more
than ordinary friend, for whom he a posn
tive affection. (First time this paper has heard

NO CHARGE FOR THIS SUGGESTION.
We have a dull Parliamentary session—a very hungry Treasury
and a funny Premier ; ivhy not the above ?
The Richmond and Tweed are making
signs of want-of-railway distress to Brisbane (Q.),
their natural geographical seaport. The Brisbane-
Southport line will be carried on to the L weed
Heads “as sure as eggs is eggs.” With their awe-
some river-bars, the Tweed and Richmond will
link on to Brisbane, no matter what N.S.W. does
in the way of counteraction.
Land-hunger in Yic. is acute. Whenever
a mallee block is thrown open there is a rush of
applicants. Farfns are changing owners every
week at from <£lo to £6O an acre. At Keilambete
estate sale, near Terang, the other day, 9000 acres
were cut up into 20, 40, 50, 80 acre blocks, and
amidst intense excitement every block was sold
at prices up to <£so an acre. Second-rate country
fetched £l2 and £l4. Most of the buyers were
the local farmers, who knew exactly what they
were doing. The very poorest land found eager
purchasers.
The proposal of Gray, M.L.A., to reduce
to £lOOO the salary of Bard Fitzgibbon, chairman
of Melbourne Board of Works, made Godfrey
Carter’s head swell to nearly twice its natural size
with indignation. “There are some men,” he
said, “ who would not pay the Angel Gabriel
£l5OO a year.” So there are. Carter, himself,
wouldn’t pay Gabriel £l5OO a year, or any other
sum. Neither would any other man. The fate of
the man who even offered to “ grease ” an arch-
angel would probably be too sudden and awful for
description.
Premier Nelson on M‘llwraith :
If he had a chance of an overdraft, he would be a fool if
he didn’t take it. ... A gentleman for whom they all
had the highest respect. ... On a sick-bed, penniless,
bed-ridden. . . . Could not write a line or sign his own
name . . . or even walk. . . . The noble occupation of
the Opposition was to give the sick lion a kick.
This was just after Drury had been sharply
handled by Nelson. But, then, Drury wasn’t sick
or bed-ridden. He was only dead. Thus, again,
the Courier :
It must be admitted that events have proved the Labor-
party was in the right with regard to the Q.N.B.
What an admission from such a quarter !
Recent census of N.S.W. aboriginals gives
3422 full-bloods and 3663 half-castes, showing,
since last year, decrease of 81 in f.b’s. as against
increase of 182 in h.c’s. Amongst f.b’s. have been
137 deaths to 77 births ; amongst h.c’s., 151 births
to 65 deaths. Twelve years ago, f.b’s. numbered
more than twice h.c’s., since when the average
yearly decrease of f.b’s. has been 160, and the
average yearly increase of h.c’s., 86. The black
race, on the whole, is evidently not so much
dying-out as passing into white, and thus is
becoming fulfilled the traditional black man’s
belief “go down blackfellow, jump up white-
fellow.”
Never was the painful inability of the Q.
Labor-party so apparent as during the recent
Q.N.B. debate. Glassey’s attack was rambling,
tedious, fertile of platitudinous invective, barren
of wit, and wanting in point. His lieutenants
followed in the same strain —the plucky ones
wanting the brains and the brainy ones lacking
the pluck. Tozer was superb in his way. He was
by turns horrified, amazed, astounded at the
audacity of people who didn’t believe Nelson and
Barlow pure as the driven snow—and he there and
then publicly and solemnly washed his hands of
the accursed Knight of Auchenflower and all his
works. Then Mat. Battersby grew eloquent of
interjection, was suspended by the Speaker for a
fortnight, and left the House exclaiming: “ Thank
God—l’ll have a rest! ” Even his intellect was
giving way.

The new N.S.W. Aliens
Restriction Act narrowly es-
caped being’ a measure to facili-
tate the introduction of Asiatics
rather than to keep them out.
it provided that all other
co ored races, not being British
subjects, should be restricted
ni the same fashion as the
Chinese, which would shutout
Africans, Polynesians, Turks,
Persians, Japanese, and the
miscellaneous Malayan tribes,
i hen it added that both among
these tribes and among the
Chinese, ministers,missionaries,
native teachers, tourists, mer-
chants, men of science and
students, and their wives,
families, and servants should
be exempt. Pretty nearly the
whole Syrian crowd, and many
of the Chinese and Japanese,
are merchants, and those tha
t didn’t get in under one or
other of the above headings
would probably arrive as the
14 descendants of some ex-
empted person. But the As-
sembly kicked at the exemp-
tion clause, and it was with-
drawn bodily.
What may be called the immigration clause
of the latest Q. Agricultural Lands Purchase Bill
is rough on Bananalanders. One-fourth of the
land open to selection—all picked, re-purchased
land—is reserved to Cornstalks, Gumsuckers,
Croweaters, &e. After which these applicants
can compete on equal terms with Bananalanders
for the other three-fourths. A great many of
these enquiring “ foreigners(in the provincial
sense) have been grunting that they're not game
to go along to Q. because of the “dasher ” appli-
cations ; because a man who really wants land
gets his sisters and his cousins and his aunts to
dip into the ballot in his interests. The one-fourth
reservation scheme is one of Lawyer Thynne’s
fads.
The official and other mysteries re the
D’Arcy Wentworth (Lucknow, N.S.W.) mine-
lease show that departments mystify Ministers,
Ministers mystify Parliament, Parliament the
press, and the press the public, with the conse-
quence that when a dispute arises days are spent
and reams of paper wasted over wrong issues.
The chief of one department knew lately that the
Minister misunderstood his official information,
but he allowed thelwrong version to go to Parlia-
ment and the country without making the
slightest effort at correction. Another chief
officer, when reproached on a similar count, ob-
served, “ How am I to know that a Minister does
not intentionally misunderstand me? ” But this
last was a case-hardened barnacle.
A well-informed Brisbane correspondent
to The Bulletin
Q. Labor-leader Glasse.v mismanaged the best ci.ance of
his political life by his method of handlng the recent
want of confidence motion in the Q. Assembly. Briefly
stated, his resolution bracketed MTlwraith (“ the gentle-
man not now in Australia ”) with Premier Nelson and un-
portfolio’d Barlow, M.L.C. Armstrong, M.P., moved to
acquit Nelson and Barlow, and took a majority with
him. Then the House—Ministry, their followers and the
Opposition - blackballed MTlwraith unanimously and
even cheerfully. This saved the Government by giving
old Nevermind Nelson time to have advice drilled into his
stubborn old noddle. Had Glassev’s original motion been
confined to kicking out MTlwraith, the chances
are that, in wrath and pseudo-chivalry to a
mate, Nelson would have backed “ the gentleman
not now in Australia ” for all he was worth, would have
made the motion a party' one and would have gone down
badly. As things were, Nelson took up the role of a man
wrongfully accused and who had the utmost contempt for
his accusers. It is well known that his close advisers had
the greatest difficulty to make hi in get on his legs at all
to contribute to the debate. It is said in inner political
circles that whip Hamilton only succeeded “at the
eleventh hour” in getting “ Nevermind ” to see that his
own supporters wanted him to speak. “ What have I got
to say?” retorted Nelson ; “but there, never mind,! I’ll
say something.” And he gave the Opposition banter of the
most humiliating kind. He was a pedagogue teachingdull
scholars. “ I would like you to remember that there are
20 shillings in a£. . . . I have often tried to educate
you, and I still hope to succeed some day.” When inter-
jections were mumbled: “Why don’t you speak up so
that I can hear vou ? What ? Must I go over it all
again ? Well, for the seventh time, then ——” and so on.
It was a remarkable piece of contemptuous irony', and, in
its method of delivery, masterful, since it seemed to
frighten the Opposition into subdued silence. Pitiful 1
Glassev s excuse, and that of his press supporters, was that
Nelson and Barlow were rather more blameworthy than
MTlwraith, and that it would have been cowardly to have
confined the attack to “ the Gentleman Who Is Away-.”
Well, it may have been magnificent, but it was not war. So
soon as the Govt., per medium of Tozer, announced that
MTlwraith was to he dropped (a move had been made by
the Cabinet to get Mac s resignation a day or two before)*
the teeth of the Opposition were drawr But, had
Glassey confined himself to MTlwraith, he would have
raised “ Nevermind’s ” slumbrous mateship, Nelson would
have jumped up to reply, would have spoken “prave
’orts” that would have bound him to stick to MTlwraith,
and the Govt, would have been defeated. When accused
himself, Nelson's main attitude was “ Nevermind.”

as to the tyres not fitting the rims properly.

as the standard rim for all Dunlop Tyres, as,
owing to our making them, we are in a posi-
tion to see that the rims are made to the
correct size, and will guarantee every Dunlop
Tyre to fit its respective sized Dunlop- Welch
Rim.

In their desire to gather the spoil, Grafton
(N.S. W.) Anglicans went so far as to sell rosaries
at their recent church-bazaar, which, after all,
only panned-out <£7o, as against £650 raked in at
the local R.C. show.
Dibbs gets home on Reid’s solar plexus
with a loud thump. The Dibbs’ deficit—that
part of it which was really Dibbs’ property—
arose mainly through loss of land revenue,
caused by the abandonment of the practice of
auctioning the public estate wholesale in large and
small lots, to whoever would buy. The new land
policy was approved by the whole Ministry, and
Reid was a member of it. And, as Dibbs alleges,
it is a mean thing for his bygone colleague “to
round upon his old comrade in a good cause.”
The flat refusal of Premier Turner to put
up a penny of public money as a grant to poor old
Graham Berry looks bitterly hard, and Turner
undoubtedly is a hard man. But he is wrestling
with a hard task—that of putting the boom-
finances straight; and the proposed .£5OOO grant
would have made the next surplus totter on its
foundations, and not having the easy gift of filling
the gap with loan funds Turner has to be hard if
he is to be successful. It was finally decided,
with great difficulty and not at all unanimously,
that members and Ministers should put up a suffi-
cient percentage out of their salaries to make up
£l5OO a year for the ancient Tribune. If the
money can be collected there is a lot more real
generosity in this idea than in pensioning Berry
at the taxpayers’ expense. Yet the spectacle of
the dilapidated hat going round periodically, and
the subscription growing smaller and harder to
collect each time, is very melancholy. Five years
hence the Berry collection-hat, when it returns
from its round, will probably have nothing in it
save the lining and somebody’s objurgation.
Two curiously-similar stories of bravery
come from Westralia—one from White Feather
of all places) the other from Mt. Malcolm. At
the Feather, Alfred Hole, losing his grip of the
rope, fell down a 40ft. shaft at the bottom of
which he had just lighted two fuses; and that is
about as tight a place as a stunned man is ever
likely to get out of alive. Charles Hicks, his
mate, instantly made up his mind that Hole
should have all the chances there were, and so
swung himself down the rope ; but, ere he could
pull away the fuses, one shot exploded. Hicks
was dazed and stupefied ; but he hung on to the
rope, and so was hauled to the surface. As it
happened, the second shot was a “ miss ; ” Hole’s
fall had displaced the fuse. But it had also broken
the poor fellow’s neck ; while, as for Hicks, he was
battered and scorched. Of course, the gold-fields
men, swift always to recognise unselfishness, sub-
stantially recognised the heroism of the plucky
mate. In the Mt. Malcolm case there was a
corpse, too —but that is farther out, where family
names don’t count for much ; and so his name is
only “Paddy.” He and his mate Wright were
sinking a shaft and had got six feet down, when
“ Paddy ” was left to fire two holes. One fuse
was stubborn ; the other, which had been lighted,
“jumped,” it is supposed, and before “Paddy”
could get out there was an explosion. Wright
reckoned that, even if his mate was not already
dead the second charge would surely kill him ;
and he also resolved to give him what chances
there were. He was down the shaft and his help-
less mate was thrown out in a few seconds : and
the second charge exploded as he was pulling him-
self out. “Paddy” died; but there wasn’t any
inquiry—a pity ; because fuse that has the habit
of “ jumping ” a few feet wants inquiring into
badly.

are for the better class of Cyclists, those who know the value of a good Machine.
While many firms have devoted their attention to belittling our cycles with the help of
hired barrackers and commission men, we have been working along in our quiet way, and made
friends, and these friends have stuck. As a result, although we are landing 1000 Bicycles per
month, we cannot meet the demand.
This is the result of honest quality and methods recognised by the knowing riders.
We beg to advise those of our patrons whose orders we cannot fill at present that our
November and December SHIPMENTS will contain 2500 Machines, including the famous
RED BIRD SPECIAL. (This machine is in a class by itself).
We trust our friends will bear with us a few days longer, as many months of added
pleasure, the result of being mounted on the best wheel on earth, will more than compensate for
the slight delay.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Ikey Mo (bitterly writes “ Backer ”) is born with
abnormal cheek, and’, as he grows, it grows better and
stronger than any other faculty he has. By the time
he is fully developed he carries around with him a front
that needs a special hoarding to exhibit it. In the case of
bookmakers, the excessive exercise which their leading
characteristic receives gives it still further strength ;
until, at last, it becomes a force that overbears all ob-
stacles. But sometimes this wonderful possession gets
the better of the man, because constant reliance upon
sheer audacity is apt to destroy its possessor’s sense of
proportion in its exercise. And that is how those two
bowled-out Victorian ringmen come to be out in the cold
to-day. To be sure, games as glaring have been carried
off successfully, and nothing was said or done in the
matter. But there was always the danger that the
pitcher might go once too often to the well. Now it’s
smashed at last, and Ikey weeps fruitlessly over the
wreck.
• • • •
The ex-Queensland champion Battalion put up
a really great performance last week, at Bendigo, where,
in the Sandhurst Cup, he carried the crusher of 10st. 21b.
into second place, giving the winner, Tecoma, no less
than 501 b., and defeating 12 other well - performed
animals.
• • • •
It is printed that the Sydney man who drew
Tecoma in Tatt’s. sweep on the Sandhurst Cup wired to
the owner of the horse offering to lay a third of the sweep,
for each place, to nothing. The manager of the Colonial
Bank at Winchelsea was then called upon to “ make ar-
rangements with the sweep-promoter, who telegraphed
that it was all right.” How comforting for the owner 1
m * * *
Arnfield and Allen, of Parthen<>paeus fame, are
reputedly worth £40,000 and £20,000 respectively, and
each of them would probably give a big lump of his little
heap for a rescission of his sentence. Sammy used to be
a big backer in the prize-ring, and a great believer in long
Jim Hall. It would be interesting to take the jolly and
fat Sammy’s opinion on the sporting aphorism, “ Never
trust anything that talks.” From all accounts, if the
pair had acted—call it diplomatically—by the jockey,
whose living was taken away in landing their little coup,
the job would never have been sheeted home to them.
* * * *
The trotting mare Calista’s mile, at Richmond
(Vic.), on a half-mile track, in 2min. 19Jsee., was a great
performance, and her owner uovv talks of a match with
Fritz. The go was a rattling good one, but would be no
use to Fritz. At Bathurst Show, on a half mile track,
Fritz, untrained, trotted a mile in 2.18, the following day
in 2.16, and the next day in 2.16. Air. Buckland drove
the wonder, holding the reins with one hand, and slap-
ping him on the rump with the other.
* • * *
N.S.W.-bred trotters did well at Canterbury
(M.L.) Carnival week meet. The Childe Harold stallion
Hazeldean, for some time driven about Sydney as a buggy
horse by Mr. M’Kechnie, of Macdonaldtown, won a mile
harness race in 2.47. Beware, a Van Cleve mare, which
ran unsuccessfully at Lillie Bridge, Sydney, a couple of
years ago, trotted two miles in saddle in smin. 21sec. The
little mare .Rita, sometime owned by Mr. Frank Palfrey,
of Sydney, who, to win, would have had to trot better
than 2.30, was only beaten by a length and a-half. The
uncertain pacing mare Hattie S., winner of the champion
prize for trotting mares at last Sydney Agricultural Show,
ran unplaced. Little Millie, by Blackwood from Abdallah,
trotted a mile in sulky in 2.26)4.
*'* « •
Barring “classic” meetings, the racing public
does not seem to “ cotton ” to Randwick. Saturday
afternoon meet proved no exception to the rule. “ Re-
versals of form ” do not agree with the betting crowd,
and it must be confessed that of these they get full
measure at Randwiek. In the Nursery Stakes, Essex,
who carried 101 b. penalty, started an equal favorite with
Vigorous, and looked all over a winner at the distance,
but Lucky Dog, who, on his running at Moorefield on the
previous Saturday, did not seem to have a “possible,’*
cut down the sterling little Essex almost in the last few
strides. Mr. Sam Hordern’s Mirella started a 6 to 4
against favorite for the Club Handicap, and won from
Bona-fide; but, as regards the other races, the prices
speak for themselves : Aboriginal, 6to 1; Town Clock, 8
to 1; Lucky Dog, 12 to 1; and Annadour Bto 1.
« * * *
A certain Sydney publican, who reckoned he
had drawn Gaulus in Tattersall’s sweep, in the generosity
of his heart made his house free on Melbourne Cup night.
During the evening a friend dropped in to congratulate
him, and asked to see the lucky ticket. The winning
number was there right enough, if the ticket bad been for
the No. 1 drawing. It was for the No. 2, and Mr. Public-
’ouse had the wrong result-list! The bar was speedily
cleared, ’mid much sulphurous language on the part of
the outraged landlord. Just imagine the feelings of a
man who was morally certain for six hours that he had
£6OOO in his grasp, and then found all his dreams shat-
tered in one act, and the old life of giving two tugs to a
beer-engine for 3d. stretching its loathsome vista in front
of him just the same as ever 1
* • * •
Adelaide bookmakers are up m arms against
Kingston’s Gambling Bill, which will result in the wiping-
out of the tote-comn>issir>n agent. Cohen and co. argue
that they fill a public want as regards the small investors,
who cannot afford to bet directly through the machine in
half-sovs., but the obvious answer to this is a ss. or half-
crown tote.

“The 8 to 1 Acmena, in the mile-andhalf Gat-
wick Handicap, was not thought to have a ‘possible,’
carrying Bst. 131 b. in such a field,” writes Tiik Bullktis’s
London correspondent. “ The 3 to 1 favorite, Carlton
Grange, was only giving her lib., and that settled the
Australian’s chances in the tips of the touts. Yet
Osborne’s mare took the lead a quarter of a mile from
home and finished, in the heavy going of recent rains,
before all the top-weights, but only fourth to one seven-
stoner and two six-stoners. I don’t think he could have
beaten Lord Beresford’s Nonsuch, 7st. 61b., but his per-
formance and those of a few others are fast educating the
English turfites to an unpleasant respect for the antipo-
dean equine.”

For the quarter ending Oct. 31, the list of
winning sires is headed by Carbine (£5900), with Lochiel
(£6300) a close second, and Abercorn (£2001) and Trenton
(£1800) next best. However, the current quarter will
show considerable alterations, Gaulus and Grafter having
given Gozo a great lift.
• » • *
About time Stoddart realised that the country
public plank down their shillings to see scientific cricket.
As it was in the past so it is now. At Newcastle, N.S.W.,
after the local score had been passed, Stoddart confabbed
with Hirst, and then both went in for pure slogging. The
local fielding was execrable, and the bowling ta.me, so
they soon ran into the hundreds. It was magnificent,
but it wasn't cricket. Later on, instead of giving the
public a taste of their bowling strength and trjing to get
the 11 out in the 2) hours remaining, the amateurs all had
a turn with the ball —excepting Stoddart himself. Had
some of the batsmen met the same bowling in club
cricket they’d have burst the ball. As it was, they played
against their opponents’ reputation, and for half-an-hour
at a time all except the batsmen, bowler and the fieldsman
at mid-off might as well have gone for a drink. It was
cricket, but poor sport.
The Giffen controversy lias assumed such dif-
ferent phases that it is difficult to say what is the exact
position just now. The writer has carefully studied all
the newspapers, and he finds that Giffen has a bad knee,
but, under certain circs., the knee can rapidly recover.
Giffen has certainly had a difference with Ben. Wardill,
but, although the’two are considerably estranged just
now, the great George still regards dear old Ben as his
best-loved brother, and is prepareil to weep on his neck
at a week’s notice fcheque enclosed). There is truth in
the rumor that George sticks out for an extra £5O, but it
isn’t a money matter ; George will gladly play, and pay
his own expenses, if necessary, provided the team elects
him captain and allows him to bowl unchanged for three
years—and he’d like to hat for a similar period without
being given out. And yet people talk learnedly about
“ the Giffen difficulty.”
• * • *
There is a growing impression that Geo. Giffen
only did what all the other professional-amateurs should
have done as regards matches with the English Eleven —
refuse to play for a grab-all management. Giff., viewed
in this light, begins to look like St. George fighting the
capitalistic dragon.
* • * *
Darling has so far been quite out of batting
form, and has failed in electorate as well as big matches.
One result has been that he has lost confidence. S.A.
selectors, by the way, learnt one lesson in the match
against Victoria. In the past, players have been dropped
on one failure. Evans, a young player, has been perse-
vered with, and came out with 5 for 35 in the second
innings, when the S.A. bowling looked like being collared.
* • * *
In the four Vio. Cricket Association pennant-
matches, began last Saturday afternoon, the totals, after
about four hours’ play, were : Melb., 293 for six wickets,
against S. Melb. ; E. Melb., 400 for three wickets, against
Carlton ; N. Melb., 346 for nine wickets, against Hawks-
bnrn ; St. Hilda, 317, all out, against Richmond. A grand
total of 1356 runs for 28 wickets. For E. Melb., Harry
Stuckey is 206 not out; and for N. Melb., his brother
George, 72 n.o. Charlie M’Leod made 136 for M.C.C.
Other interprovincial and international men who scored
heavily were : M’Michael 89, Harry 63, Bruce 62, Trurn-
ble 50.
• * • *
“ Long Tom ” Rockemin Richardson may yet
be the founder of a school of bowlers in Australia. At a
club pennant match in Melbourne, the other day, it was
noticeable that two bowlers on one side had developed
the long Richardson gallop up to the wicket, and the
Richardson delivery. But they managed to keep a more
consistent “ length” than Thomas.
• • * •
The 400 for three wickets made by East Melb.
v. Carlton last Saturday took exactly four hours, and
each hundred was hoisted at the end of each hour to the
minute.
* • • *
The Mayor of Brisbane, welcoming the English
Eleven, expressed a hope that it wasn't a Queenslander
who sneaked Sloddart’s watch on the previous night.
Stoddy seemed rather indifferent as to the birthplace of
the marauder.
• • * •
The match between the Englishmen and com-
bined team, Queensland and N.S.W., resulted in a draw
on Monday last, the game standing ; England, 636 ; com-
bined 13, 8 wickets for 316. The latter team was more
N.S.W. than locr.l, no less than seven of the men who
batted hailing from the mother province. The Cornstalks
Included two such old time warriors as “Sammy” Jones
and “ Charley ” Turner. There was a first-class wicket,
on which M’Laren rolled up 181 and Druce 126. Young
Pye’s effort was watched with interest, and the colt came
out of the ordeal in brilliant style with 55, which, on. top
of his 166 against Queensland last year, cannot allow of
his being overlooked for selection when next N.S.W.
needs representation in first-class cricket.
• * * •
Maclarcn, who holds the world’s record for
highest score in first-class cricket—424 against Somerset-
shire —seenfs to have about the best show of securing the
average of the tour. It will be a terrible blow to Ranji.’s
bnrrackers if a mere white plebeian beats their Injun
Prince.
• • * *
“ Cricket Critic ”; It is very evident that, with
a fair share of luck, the Englishmen will win the
majority of the test-matches, owing to their undoubted
superiority in catching. Just look at the matches already
played—Adelaide, Melbourne, Sydney, and now at Bris-
bane. While the home teams consistently miss catches,
the visitors snap up nearly every possible catch offered by
their opponents; in fact, in the Sydney match I don’t
believe the Englishmen dropped one “ possible ” during
the two innings ! It won’t do to give men like Ranji or
M’Laren two or three “ lives.” The innings of the former
at Adelaide and the latter at Brisbane are object-lessons.
Both were missed before they’d scored ! Result, over 180
a-piece at the finish. By the way, Ranji, so far, is the
only Englishman who has made 100 runs or more without
a chance—viz., in the second innings against N.S.W.
• * * *
Even bad and vacillating judging, ignorant
refereeing, and all-round execrable otfieialing of the
L.V.W. sports, last Saturday, failed to spoil the meeting,
and the racing was perhaps the best yet seen in Mel-
bourne. The big scratch Cyclone Plate consisted of three
events—one mile, three miles, and five miles—all of which
distances fell to Green, with “ Newhaven ” Jackson and
Walne second and third respectively. The judges cer-
tainly said that Jackson won the second event, but they
were*really all out. Each race was won in the same way.
Green, who rode magnificently in the mile, after being
blocked at the bell, scooped round on the outside, while
Jackson and Walne battled all they knew for second
place. Barden, the open-mouthed Britisher, finished last
in his preliminary of the mile, and fourth in the three
miles, and was altogether sadly disappointing.
The second-class two miles pave a splendid
finish, A. J. Berry, from 10yds., winning- in great style,
with a dozen on his wheel. Judges gave Hickox second
and L. M. Jackson third, but after a brief argument with
March evidenced their backbone by substituting his name
for that of Hickox. The third-class fell to H. Q. Hill, off
10yds., and was again a fine finish. Owing to the an-
nouncement that no winners would be penalised in the
Austral events, nearly all were triers, though Kellow
stood down for the rest of the afternoon for inconsistent
riding in his heat of the Cyclone Mile. This decision, or
even something more, could have been understood on
Kellow’s riding the previous Saturday, but that he did
wrong in the Cyclone Plate was apparent to nobody but
the ref'-ree.
On Saturday’s riding, none of the scratch men
should have a ghost of a show against W. C. Jackson in
the Austral. The Avoca man starts from 65yds.,and that
he can make his pace is amply shown by his riding in last
year’s Austral. “ Newhaven” will be a warm member for
the big race.
• • * •
Bilmartin still sticks to his training, and it is
whisperer! that the gentle William intends shortly to try
and be reinstated as an amateur. The spectacle of Bill
bursting himself for a J 2 trophy negotiable for about
Is. 6d. would be the most comic incident the Australian
track has ever seen.
• • * •
Bilmartin has started operations as a cycling
punter, and, at Melb. Friendly Societies track, on Satur-
day raked in many dollars. With a camera over his
shoulder, Bill collected £3o—he won on Green-and
walked along the front of the stand shovelling it out of
his hat in handfuls.
* * * *
Occasionally Nemesis gets home on the cycling-
record fiend, and the hearts of the godly rejoice. One
Tipper left Sydney G.1’.0. t’other day in a violent hurry
armed with a certificate given by somebody, to attempt
the futile asininit.v of riding a bicycle to Melbourne
quicker than any other cycle crank —whereupon sensible
people at once went to their news-agents and cut off
their daily papers for a week to avoid the progress tele-
grams. But when, next day, it was recorded that Tipper
was tipped into the Bargo River (good old Bargo !), all
Sydney shook hands with itself and went out and had a
drink. But Tipper wouldn't take the hint. lie started
again next day from Sydney, and to the great delight of
the unprincipled rode into Yass a mass of cuts and
bruises, and resolved to rest a week in the hospital. The
writer doesn’t know Tipper from a crow, and doesn't
want to, and in his capacity as Tipper wishes him no
harm, but as a cycling record-breaker may he always
meet with a Bargo, and find a cot in the Yass hospital 1
. * • • •
Australians who know anything of the game
.did not, expect Joe Goddard to stand much show against
Sharkey. Goddard ever depended upon his power of
taking punishment and his stamina to pull him through,
and a man who fights in that fashion is no good after the
first blush of yout'i and strength. Mick Dooley and Joe
Choynski both got their rights on to Goddard, bringing
him down ; but he pulled round and beat them by main
strength. As Sharkey has all Goddard’s old-time devil,
with science added, the sailor-man’s victory over the
worn-out slogger was a foregone conclusion.
* * * *
The League of Vic. Wheelmen has taken posses-
sion of, and made luxurious, the building in Kussell-street,
Melb., formerly occupied by the Young Men’s Christian
Association, run by Goldbug and Co.
Their tents “ those Christians” had to strike,
For “ holy ” bankers all went bung;
A potent lesson tor the young—
Where once the Bible, now the Bike !
• * * •
The. Johnston’s Bay (Sydney) Sailing Club’s
22ft. race to decide upon the boat to represent the club
at Brisbane, Q., proved a big set-to between the Vigilant,
sailed by VV. Read, and the Effie, sailed by Nick Johnson.
First one boat and then the other took pride of place,
Vigilant ultimately winning by the small margin of 20sec.
By her win her owner takes £lB, a trophy, and £l5 sub-
sidy if she goes North.
* • * •
Sydney boat-owners very properly protested
against the 22ft. and 18ft. races being sailed on the same
day at Brisbane (Q.) Carnival, as it was not to be expected
that separate crews would be sent up with each boat.

should be fired by the time this meets the eyes of readers
hereof.

• * # *
An Austral dream. A well-known member of
the Melbourne bicycle trade had a dream as to the re-
sults of the Austral Wheel Race heats and final. The
dreamer “distinctly saw’’the following men placed in
their respective heats Beauchamp (Tasmania), Walne,
Green, D. J. Walker, A. Turner, K. Lewis, H. Reynolds,
H. James, W. Elliott, Hourigan, and C. Kellow; the
remainder of the placed men in the heats he is not sure
of. In the final the result was Don Walker, Walne, A.
Turner and 11. James in a bunch ; then some few yards
off came K. Lewis, Kellow, Elliott, Green, and Reynolds.
The details of the whole race were given with such clear-
ness that it will be interesting to watch whether the race
and dream agree in any particular.
THE REASON OF IT.
In the science of cutting and driving
And banging the bowling about,
Or by marvellous field-work contriving
To bustle big run-getters out,
He is great, I admit, but the others
Are likewise —now what is the cause—
Him with praise while the crowd fairly smothers,
The rest gain but seamy applause?
I can solve you your riddle ; the reason
Is as plain as the sun in the sky ;
Its drift you'll immediately seize on
When 1 cite you the how and the why.
Those who cheer him so wildly eestatio
And such grovelling homage evince,
Are the holders of views democratic,
And Ranji—well, Ranji's a Prince.
F.L.P.


Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cure's instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post i/4, 2/G,
3/8. Senior, chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
> A carious thing in Melbourne’s commer-
cial social life is the inevitable rally of the same
old, free-drinking fraternity at every dispensation
of free drinks. The other week there was a
fathering of the dan at Menzies’ Hotel to sample
‘ol Roger champagne. The invitations were sup-
posed to have been issued to press paragraphists
and “representative” persons likely to advance,
by some means or other, the interests of the new
fizz, yet tlie crowd where the corks were flying
thickest included all the familiar faces of men
who are “representative” only of the thirst that
slakes itself gratis on such occasions. The men
who smell champagne from afar, as vultures scent
carrion, and are not even grateful for the liquor
they soak themselves with. Pol Roger is a light,
dry, palatable wine, of the kind that leaves no
headachey after-effeot, therefore the “push,” as a
rule, raised the negative objection that it didn’t
make them sufficiently unsober. These hardened
experts believe in getting drunk on the premises,
and as critics they should confine themselves
strictly to beer.*

Let every good citizen read the Suffield
case pamphlet—post free from Bulletin Office, 7d.
The Bulletin will be glad to receive, for
publication, from all quarters, tersely-expressed
comments on the Suffield case —which it regards
as involving one of the most cruel injustices ever
perpetrated by law in Australia. The case is
forcefully set forth in Mr. A. G. Stephens’
pamphlet, published by The Bulletin and post-
free from this office for 7d. in stamps.
The National Cash Register Co. has estab-
lished itself in Australasia under the able manage-
ment of Mr. W. A. Lingham. The central deprtt
at 393 George-street, Sydney, is handsomely fur-
nished, lighted, and arranged ; the facilities for
buyers are a most pleasing innovation on Austra-
lian commercial practices, and will afford a big
lesson to the Australian business public. The
National Cash Register itself, with its highly-
polished metal case and figured keys, is one of the
most useful and attractive inventions of the age.
It records the number of purchasing customers,
the amount paid by each ; gives out a properly-
dated and perfectly accurate receipt, and shows at
a glance the till’s total contents. It correctly adds
from to thousands of. pounds, makes fraud
impossible, saves labor, money, time, Worry, and
responsibility.*
The forthcoming number of the Anti-
podean magazine will be edited by A. B. Paterson
and Geo. Essex Evans, and published by Geo.
Robertson and Co. early in Dec. —price, one
shilling. With further reference to a statement
made in this paper’s issue of 20th November, to
the effect that “ the projected re-issue of the An-
tipodean was nearly falling through for want of
advts. tothepublishingguarantors’minimum,”THE
Bulletin is assured that as a matter of fact the
Antipodean, being published by the well-known
firm of Geo. Robertson and Co., does not depend
on advertising for its existence, and the fact that
it has secured a large amount of advertising is not
to be wondered at when it is seen that the list of
contributors embraces most of the best-known
Australian writers, many of them old Bulletin
contributors. In the forthcoming issue “Rolf
Boldrewood” discourses on “Black Trackers”;
Brunton Stephens, who has been so long metri-
cally silent, furnishes a poem, “The Chamber
of Faith ” ; Henry Lawson details his experiences
as master of a Maori native school; “The Banjo”
furnishes a further instalment of the adventures
of “Saltbush Bill”; Ethel Turner has a story,
“ Golden Syrup ” ; Clement Wragge narrates in
simple language how to tell your weather for
weeks ahead ; Essex Evans furnishes verse; and
several other well-known writers aid in making
up a publication that should achieve the success
which The Bulletin cordially wishes it.
Before last week’s no confidence motion in
Q. Assembly, Labor-leader Glassey: “I have no
hope of carrying it, but I must put myself right
with the country.” Words best left unsaid. No
leader of a forlorn hope tells his men that the hope
is forlorn.
