“R.H.” to The Bulletin :
Miles of Urana, and Narandera stock-routes are
at present being cleared and ringbarked by Govt, camps
of Sydney unemployed ; but unless these routes are
securely fenced off or other stringent measures taken to
protect the struggling drovers and prevent pastoralists
running stock on them, the money so expended will
have been absolutely thrown away—even if this isn’t
merely another roundabout way of playing into the hands
of squattocratic Fatman.
T.P. : “You pointedly show in a recent
issue that Auckland has contributed nought, or
next to nought, to the statesmanship of Maori-
land. With shame, I, an Aucklander, admit the
justice of the gibe. Auckland, the qiieen city of
Maoriland, the seat of a university, the centre of
culture - Auckland, hopelessly mediocre and
* retail ’ in sentiment, is content now to be repre-
sented by Crowther (a retired livery-stable man),
Holland (a retired carpenter), and Thompson (a
retired grocer). And Auckland, Pecksniffian,
bourgeois, narrow and lodge-ridden, deserves no
better representation than it lias got.”
; It is refreshing to note from the new
biography of Tennyson that in his earlier years,
ere he was spoilt by adulation, the poet had a
frank contempt for titles. Monckton Milnes, in
18.17, asked him to contribute a poem to a sort of
keep-sake volume the Marquis of Northampton
(who, by the way, has just died) was editing.
Tennyson wrote back refusing, and in his letter
said, “ To write for people with prefixes to their
names is to milk he-goats ; there is neither honor
nor profit.” (Vol. 1., p. 158.) Monckton Milnes
wa j* J" I *'*’ re f usa l)” says the biographer,
and Tennyson sent him a poem to soothe him.
And yet he became a titled he-goat himself !
A very important matter to the N.S.W.
public is the recent modification of the informer-
rewarding system, so that any person for the
f"t u . re giying information about smuggling or
illicit distillation will not have to chance receiving
a P°rti°n of a money penalty, but will be certain,
in addition, of a specific reward, even where the
offender elects to “ take it out.” This arrangement
means a death-blow to the local manufacture, by
clumsy amateurs with defective appliances, of
hell-water so horrible that its producers can never
be induced to put lip to it themselves—even in
gaol. The great bulk of smuggled spirit, also, is
simply too vile to be worth paying duty upon, and
altogether, though the informer may be an evil,
the wholesale brain-blasting of the alcohol-using
community is an infinitely greater one. The first
s y-groggery nailed under the new regulations in-
cluded in its plant a contrivance intended to super-
sede the “ worm ’’—apparently no longer “the
worm that dieth not.” The informer’s unobtru-
mve finger, by-the-way, may, in this instance, be
distinctly traced in the fact that a batch of Syd-
ney policemen were able to go straight to where
the poison was being made in the solitudes of
Cowan Creek, Hawkesbury River.

you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found

DYSPEPSIA and its treatment is receiving
much more than a share of attention from the
medical journalistic world. The British Phar-'
maceutical Journal has published an article of
about four pages by the well-known Professor
Finkler, going into a series of experiments com-
paring papain as a digestive ferment with pepsin,
and, in nearly every case, the papain results are
the most favorable. After showing the results of
the experiments, he winds up the article as fol
lows :
Pepsine acts in. the stomach, and not in the
intestines, as in the latter the reaction is neutral or
alkaline. Papain has little effeot in the stomach,
as the reaction there is acid ; when, however, the
stomach is neutral or alkaline, papain will pep-
tonise, while pepsine will be useless. The degree
of acid reaction in the stomach varies greatly,
especially in the case of a stomach out of order, in
which case the reaction can be so highly acid or
alkaline that pepsine would be of no value at all.
I’or the treatment of stomach catarrh we will,
however, find that as a general rule the contents
of the viscus have been rendered artificially neu-
tral or alkaline by the administration of remedies,
so that in these cases pepsine will have absolutely
no effect. Papain, on the contrary, will act ener-
getically. Regarding the time available for the
digestive ferment to act in the body, in the case of
pepsine this is necessarily very short, as the action
ceases when the food enters alkaline reaction of
the intestines. In the case of papain, which can
act well in alkaline reaction, the time is prac-
tically unlimited, as it continues acting on the
food during the whole time it remains in the body.
For all these reasons the conditions for the effect
in the human body are far more in favor of papain
than pepsine, and especially as under existing
circumstances (high degree of concentration) pap-
pain has been shown to act more energetically.
It is only too clear that the preference should be
given to papain as a digestive ferment for the
treatment of dyspepsia. Besides for dyspepsia,
the papain is of great importance in the treatment
of diphtheria. Its effective power consists in the
circumstance that no free acid need be present in
the application, and, further, that the moisture D f
the diphtheric membranes is quite sufficient to
allow the ferment to dissolve the solidified sub-
stance of the membranes. No other ferment has
so far been able to obtain these results. It is on
this account that many competent experimentalists
and specialists have lately made observation as to
these points. From all these circumstances there
is no doubt that in papain we have a ferment
which has a great future before it, and it is for
this reason that I have made a special study of the
manner of preparing the papain. I have had the
satisfaction of obtaining the most favorable results
from its use in the treatment of disease.”
The Extra Pharmacopaeia, page 305, says
that Papian is the dried juice of the Paw Paw
(Carica Papaya,) and goes on to show its medioal
value. Ihe Medica& Annual of the current
edition also publishes a long article on the fruit
ferment Papain from the Paw Paw, and quotes
from an American work the Poltclinic, of June,
1896, showing the very beneficial results obtained
from the use of Papain in cases of indigestion,
proving that its digestive power, while in the sys-
tem, is greater than any other ferment known.
In a supplement to Chambers Encyclopaedia
Papain is described as follows“ Papain is a
Nitrogenous Body, isolated from the juice of the
tropical Paw Paw. ... The juice has for a
long time been used in the West Indies for
making meat tender; but it has only recently
been shown that Papain possesses, like Pepsin
and Trypsin, the power of digesting meat fibre,
and this digestion will go on in an alkaline, a neu-
tral, or an acid solution. Hence, it belongs to the
group of Digestive Ferments, and, like them, is
employed in cases of Dyspepsia, or employed for
the predigestion of food.”
The proprietor of the Cairns Preserving Works,
noting the great digestive ability of the Paw Paw
Fruit, which grows prolifically in North Queens-
land, has manufactured a delicious table sauce
from it. All the principal grocers sell it. This
digestive tropical novelty is receiving much atten-
tion.*

“Macassar” tells The Bulletin, apropos
the fulgurites (glass tubes lightning-fused out of
sand) now on view in Sydney, that there is a
small island of the Sulu Archipelago, the beach of
which, above high-water mark, is completely
riddled with such tubes, the glass being in many
cases of beautiful iridescent colors. A length of
40 odd feet w'as once traced, without any sign of
termination.
French savant Jayle lately (according to
the Presse Medicale) showed Paris Anatomical
Society a collection of rabbit and guinea-pig does,
whose present ovaries were not their own. Some
of the rabbits contained ovaries originally belong-
ing to other rabbits, while the ovaries of some had
been transplanted from guinea-pigs. Even so with
the guinea-pig does, some of which were wearing
ovaries transplanted from rabbits. He proposed
next to endeavor to artificially fecundate what
might be called “ rabbitised ” guinea-pigs with
rabbit semen, and “ guinea-pigised ” rabbits with
guinea-pig semen, and then await results. The
idea behind the experiments is to test the law of
the mutability of species, and to see whether
hybrids likely to be of great commercial use may
not perhaps be producible in this artificial way.
It happened in Barataria. The magistrate
was an enthusiast about skittles, and there was a
great interprovineial skittle-match on, but, un-
happily, there was a long and lingering case to be
heard, and the P.M. sat disconsolately, dreaming
of skittles, eating his finger-nails, and wondering
how on earth he was going to get away to that
game of nine-pins, whilst the lawyers and wit-
nesses fought it out among them on the floor of
the court. But Heaven does hear and answer
prayer: suddenly the P.M. saw his opportunity.
Defendant had committed an act of contempt—a
small, unimportant manifestation of derision, but
enough ; and the law rose up against him in the
person of a furious magistrate and smote him hip
and brisket. Never was a magistrate so righteously
indignant before, or so determined to uphold the
dignity of the court. Forty-eight hours in the
cells was the order. Protestations, auologies, ex-
planations, all were vain; the P.M. would not
forgive, neither would he go on with the case, and
send the arch-offender up on the rising of the
court. Instantaneous and complete vengeance
was called for, and defendant went sadly up.
Ten minutes later the court was placidly over-
looking the great interprovineial game of skittles.

LASER B€€R.
Barred.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ft’s precious hard, by thunder 1 to see that colt
come sailing
Down the straight, and land a winner, when I
should have had the mount,
If the stewards hadn’t fancied I’d a hand in Nero s
failing,
And stood me for a twelvemonth, just to settle
my account.
I oan feel the hot blood tingle as they sweep
around the railing;
How the gay silks flash and shimmer ! Just take
this from me, old pard,
I rode that finish fairly, and the colt was beaten
squarely,
Yet I’ve got to stand beside you and remember
lam “barred.”
Like the jockey barred from racing, there are folk
in every station
Who long with endless longing just to follow
Nature’s trend,
Who hate the toil they live by —but it’s that or
else starvation,
So they do their daily penanoe till life’s penal-
ties shall end.
But they often wonder dimly as they eat Care’s
meagre ration
Were they born ’neath astral bodies, with their
fortunes evil-starred.
They pass life watching ever for the day that
cometh never,
And asking why they’re branded as the God-for-
gotten “barred.”
It may be lack of money—it may be lack of
learning—
That fetters them forever to a life of barren toil.
That chains the men and women who for higher
things are yearning,
To a small and fetid workroom, or the blank and
barren soil.
Oan ye wonder Nature’s problem is too deep for
man’s discerning ?
Oan ye wonder that he pictures God a tyrant,
fierce and hard ?
If ye can, then ye have felt not those ioy chains
that melt not,
And bind in cruel bondage the ambitions of the
“ barred.”
Ah, it’s bitter, bitter, bitter, when the years are
swiftly rushing,
And day by day, and week by week, no brighter
chance unfolds ;
When all Youth’s dreams of rosy hue that Hope’s
soft clouds were flushing
Fade out, and skies of steely gray are all the
future holds.
They are banned, and jeered, and flouted, with the
mace of Plutus crushing
Each honest effort they can make, with tempers
wrung and jarred ;
With spirits seared and broken, they hear their
sentence spoken:
“ From the choicest joys of living ye are barred,
barred, barred ! ”
It is true they have their freedom, and they oan, if
they are willing,
Surrender toil and spend their lives in seeking
what they crave.
But the noble souls I sing of, to the calls of duty
thrilling,
Will tramp the dreary treadmill till it endeth
in the grave. .
Is it strange a sceptic feeling with its frosty
breath is chilling ,
The faith of those who quit this world with
marks of conflict scarred !
And they ask, when they have perished, if the
last faint hope they’ve cherished
Will be met with grim denial, and they still must
be the “ barred. ”
Maoriland.
C.HARVIE.
An Impressionist Outline.
(AFTER THE NEWEST MASTERS.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The Girl sat on the green slope of the bank
where it dipped down to the tennis-court. She
was eating’ Grass.
The Man stood beside her with the toe of his
right foot tapping impatiently against the heel of
his left. In fact, he stood on one leg. Or, to be
absolutely accurate, he stood on one foot. And
that his left foot.
The Grass which the Girl was eating was Prairie
Grass; it was thick, dull, green, stringy and
sharp-edged. The blades of it swelled out in the
middle and thinned towards their sides. She ate
the same piece of grass all the time. She did not
swallow it. She held the stalk of it in the fingers
of her right hand, and the pallid, green-tinted sap
oozed in infinitesimal drops along the bitten edge
of the stalk.
Her left hand rested upon the bank behind her
and she leaned her weight on it. Her face was
down-bent, and the red and black band on her
sailor-hat made a vivid touch of color in the green-
ness of the background, and the whiteness of her
serge frook.
(The man still stood on one foot.)
* * * •
Above, in the distance overhead, stretched
the sky, tender, rose-tinted, illimitable. Little
baby clouds went rolling themselves over and
over across its deepening fallows, little grey clouds,
with their western edges tipped with amethyst,
and rose, and amber. They played among them-
selves up there in the great sky-stretches, and the
sun sent a last smile over them as he passed down
lingeringly below the horizon’s edge.
Eastward, there were shadows that roamed
westward slothfully, and seemed loth to blur the
gleaming fields of the sunset sky.
And towards the city red lights not lit by
Nature began to make their star-like points of
color where streets and houses were colorless as
man had made them.
By-and-bye the sky faded ; the rose and amber
and amethyst melted into a misty mauve, the
mauve paled into a grey. When the stars came
out they lit the grey into a dark-toned blue. All
the red shades disappeared.
The Girl still ate her Grass.
• * * *
The air was utterly still, and after the sun -
had set it grew sweet with the evening odors of
flowers. The violets along the borders of the
garden-beds, and under the camphor-laurels and
Chinese lantern trees, breathed out a united frag-
rance that seemed to melt into the dusk and
sweeten all the air right up into the ether.
Down by the creek a mist was rising, and the
wattles, with all the light gone out of the golden
burrs, sighed and .sighed through the paddocks
till all the world was one vague, sweet, arrowy
wattle-breath.
And then there were the little red gums that
clustered together in the bush in the background ;
their odors were dreamy, wholesome, not so sweet
as the wattle, perhaps, but calming, and sugges-
tive of a wide country with great trees, and free
winds, and a sky made of one perfect turquoise.
* * * 9
All the birds were asleep. The coach bird
had ceased the musical ’swithl’ of his notes, so
reminiscent of a coach-whip’s lash, and slept in
the valley near the ground. The laughing jackass
was dreaming with his head under his wing. The
tiny blue wren, whose brilliant turquoise caught
every sparkle of the sunlight in the daytime, was
at rest under some gum-tree’s leaf. The parro-
quets, who had jabbered all day in restless flocks,
were silent at last. The little black and white
fly-catcher, with his flirty tail, had settled in still-
ness on the branch of a great eucalyptus. All
their notes were hushed.
• * * *
But the crickets were singing everywhere.
When the birds ceased the crickets began. Their
crisp brown bodies lay under the grass like little
bits of burnt stick. But though they were so
near, it was hard to gather them into one’s hand,
even ever so tenderly. They sing right under
your eyes, but you cannot see them. You search
just where their song was, but you cannot feel
them.
• • * »
A great, hairy, yellowy-brown spider, with
white and black and grey specks on him, was
throwing a net from one geranium-bush to another.
The Man hit at him with his tennis racquet. He
fell on the grass, and his ash-liued legs and arms
disappeared suddenly, and instead of a great hairy
yellow-brown spider there was a little motionless
dark ball. He was not dead. He was only
soheming a spider scheme.
* • * •
Flying-foxes smelt the peaches in the orchard,
and ’ flapped themselves with straight swirls
through the air. In the lovely fruit-bloom of the
peaches little cruel holes made their appearance.
The beauty of the bloom was shattered by the
beasts’ sharp teeth and hot, hungry breaths.
• * * *
Two men wandered down the road. One
was smoking a comely, rich cigar. He wore a red
velvet smoking-cap, with a tassel. These caps
went out of fashion six years ago. The women
who did the crewelwork on them hope they will
never come in again.
♦ * * *
And now, having exhausted grass, sky,
clouds, the air, the violets, wattle, redgums, the
coachbird, the laughing-jackass, the bluewren, the
parroquets, the flycatchers, the crickets, spiders,
flying-foxes, and the other people, we return to
our impressionist outline.
The girl was still eating Grass. It was nearly
finished.
The man stood on his other foot.
M.LOUISE MACK.
More than half the water which flows from
Beetaloo Reservoir (the second largest in S.A.)
into the mains is wasted in some unaccountable
way. The quantity for which no payment was
received last financial year was 270 million gals. !
Yet the public wonder why the discrepancy be-
tween maintenance and interest charges and
revenue is <£22,500 annually. After quietly sub-
mitting to this loss for years, Govt, is now trying
to find out where the water goes to.
Agricultural Northern S.A. is in a
droughtier state than it was last year, when seed
wheat had to be supplied to hundreds of farmers.
Many of them will not harvest even as much as
they sowed. It is seriously proposed that Govt,
and private benefactors should not prolong the
agony of these settlers, but should just let them
be starved out to more suitable country. To meet
that, Foster, farmers’ M.P. —also a storekeeper—
has gathered statistics showing that from the
affected country, between Petersburg and Hookina,
088,105 bushels of wheat were trucked in 18!) >, and
522,770 bushels so lately as 1894, And he .-isks, of
course, can the province afford to lose it ? What a
gamble it is, though, is shown by the other figures
that in 1889 the export was but 100,050 bushels,
and in 1890 but 203,781. In the far West of
N.S.W. too, this season promises to be droughtier
than last. Only enough rain fell there to start
herbage, which, unlike grass, disappeared with the
first half-dozen hot days.
Save that sold under one or two unim-
peachable trade-labels, there is no absolutely pure
olive-oil to be bought in Australia. Ninety-nine
hundredths of the so-called “olive-oil” of com-
merce is merely refined cotton-seed oil more or
less mixed with olive. The South Australian
article, in the opinion of cosmopolitans of wide
experience, is as good as any in the world, and
the London opinion that it is merely a “ good
lubricant ” proves either that the expert was an
ass or that, as often happens, the Australian pro-
ducer has been trying to cut his own throat by
not submitting a first-class sample in first-class
condition. Anyone who uses the South Aus-
tralian oil for salad purposes is quite unlikely to
designedly use any other.
“ Serang ” from Gippsland Lakes en-
trance :
Editor Bulletin,—The inducement of netting in the
Gippsland Lakes has caused men to settle with their
families at the Lakes’ entrance, and has made the place a
centre entirely dependent on the labor of fisher-
men. Minister Best, however, has recently issued a pro-
clamation closing the waters in the vicinity of the Lakes’
entrance, thus depriving the men of their living when-
ever it is rough outside. No doubt this is the result of
representations made by disappointed anglers—probably
unaware that the fish usually caught in the closed wafers
come in and go out with the tide, and only make their
appearance at certain seasons of the year. An exodus of
the fishermen will have to take place unless the restriction
is removed, as this place is, at best, a poor struggling
village locally known as “ Corpse Town,” and—minus the
fishermen—highly decomposed at that.

TRIUMPHANT.
(Full Investigation by the Newcastle Evening
Times.)
A very well-known and highly-respected resi-
dent of this district (says the Newcastle Times)
is Mr. Thomas Rowe, of 130 Darby-street. He
has been in the colonies about 40 years, a few of
the first of which were spent in Sydney ; then he
came to Newcastle and settled. He commenced
business as a general storekeeper, and opened in
rather a large way, remained in business for many
years, and then retired. The gentleman was
visited at his residence a few evenings ago; and
after a lengthy and interesting chat, it came out
that Mr. Rowe had been a great sufferer for a
number of years from the liver, and also that
troublesome complaint known as the gravel. “I
have been a terrible sufferer, as many people on
this road can tell you. X had distressing pains in
my back and right side, and had awful shooting
pains in my left shoulder. My digestion got very
bad, and the pains in my chest after eating were
unbearable, and again I would have an aching
pain in my left side. I suffered this way for
years, and was getting very weak and low-
spirited. I seemed to lo e e all life and energy, and
took no interest in what was passing around me.
I consulted the best physicians in this town, but
received no permanent relief. One doctor said I
was suffering with an abscess on my liver. In
answer to further interrogations, he said that he
could produce doctors’ bills for all sorts of
amounts, and, in fact, he did produce them.
You see,” he said, “ I was given an account of a
wonderful cure effected by Gould’s Tiny Tonic
Pills, told by a Mrs. Gilbert. I was so much
struck with the story told by the lady that I de-
cided at once to give the preparation a trial, and
without any delay I purchased a bottle. Well, I
am not going to tell you that one dose or one bottle
has cured me. I shall simply tell the whole story
and my experience.
“ F irst of all, however, let me tell you that one
of the doctors here told me distinctly that I could
not get rid of my complaint. I asked him if he
thought I was going to die. He said, * Die, no,
not for 10 or 15 years yet.’ * Then,’ I replied, * I
am to expect so many years of pain any worry, am
I?’ The doctor could not reply. Then I told
another gentleman that I intended to try Gould’s
Tiny Pills, and he only laughed. All the same, I
bought the bottle, and there was undoubtedly a
marked improvement in my condition. When that
bottle was finished I purchased another. During
this time I unfortunately contracted influenza, and,
upon my word, Gould’s Pills cured that ; there’s
no mistake about it, for I took nothing else.
Messrs. Hart and Firkin, my chemists, can sub-
stantiate my statements, for they know as well as
anyone how I suffered. I tell this story for the
benefit of others who may be sufferers from similar
complaints, and I recommend Gould’s Pills as a
certain cure. There is no gainsaying the fact
that mine was an extremely bad case, for it was ;
but, as I said before, I am a different man now,
and I tell everyone what this wonderful medicine
has done for me.
“Well, I don’t think I need tell you any pi ore
about the Pills, only that there is not the least
doubt about it they are a wonderful composition.
I say positively that it was Gould’s Tiny Tonic
Pills that have cured me, and I shall never, while
I have the means, be without a bottle."

The Professional Wanderer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When you’ve knocked about the country—been
away from home for years ;
When the past, by distance softened, nearly fills
your eyes with tears—
You are haunted oft, wherever or however you
may roam,
By a fancy that you ought to go and see the folks
at home.
You forget the family quarrels—little things that
used to jar—
And you think of how they’ll worry—how they
wonder where you are ;
\ou will think you served them badly, and your
own part you’ll condemn,
And you think of how you would be quite a
novelty to them,
For your voice has somewhat altered, and your
face has somewhat changed—
And your views of men and matters over wider
fields have ranged.
Then it’atime to save your money, or to watch it
(how it goes);
Then it’s time to get a “ Gladstone ” and a decent
suit of clothes ;
Then it’s time to practise daily with a hair-brush
and a comb,
Till you drop in unexpected on the folks and
friends at home.
When you’ve been at home for some time, and the
novelty’s worn off,
And old chums no longer court you, and your
friends begin to scoff ;
When “ the girls ” no longer kiss you, crying
“Jack ! how you have changed ! ”
When you’re stale to your relations, and their
manner seems estranged;
When the old domestic qnarrels, round the table
thrice a day,
Make it too much like old times—make you
wish you’d stayed away,
When, in short, you’ve spent your money in the
fullness of your heart,
And your clothes are getting shabby . . . Then
it’s high time to depart.
HENRY LAWSON.

Naeletua and Leata.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Naeletua and Leata belonged to the John-
sonian school which preceded the invention of
conversational mincing-machines. They ranked as
papalangi, because, they had successfully annexed
rich papalangi spouses, but in reality they were
only warm-blooded, hot-tempered, large and
luscious brown Samoans, with scarce enough
civilised veneer to gloss over their semi-savage
impulses.
* * * *
They were equally lucky women, as popular
feeling reckons luck. Each had a rich trader-
husband, a fine white man’s house, fine white
man’s food, fine white man’s clothing ; each had
antecedents which unkind tongues spelt prece-
dents. But there equality ended and disparity
began. Naeletua and her lord were notoriously
weary of one another ; Leata, if she chafed at her
legal bonds, took good care to give no sign. For
Leata had been to Europe, where she adapted
herself marvellously well to the ways of civilisa-
tion, and no Frenchwoman could now teach her
anything of the art of being au mieux avec Mon-
sieur son mari.
* * * *
Leata had picked up other European arts ;
her. house and her table were quoted for their ele-
gance and excellence. And this was perhaps why
Naeletua’s Herr was so often to be found eating
salt with Leata’s John Bull. Naeletua had never
appreciated the charms of sauerkraut, potato
niideln or marzipan, and, where platonic fascina-
tion is concerned, a man’s stomach is supposed to
be as susceptible as his heart.
*'* • •
Naeletua had no more faith in Plato than
in sauerkraut; her ideas, as her. husband apolo-
getically remarked, were limited like her talents.
Her husband’s devoted attendance at Mr. Leata’s
table struck her unphilosophic brain in the light of
a personal insult —and she mentioned it. Not
being polished by travel, she mentioned it ala
Dr. Johnson.
• * • •
Leata was exceedingly shocked, nay, her
feelings were hurt; she had supposed herself
raised above such vulgar commonplace. In the
European world, the world to which she now
claimed to belong, people made their own little
domestic arrangements without comment or com-
plaint. Naeletua was acting like an uncivilised
savage, and ought to be treated accordingly.
Leata mentioned it; having to deal with a savage,
she also adopted the Johnsonian method.
* » * *
Then Naeletua did a very unwise thing :
she committed her sentiments to paper, and her
sentiments on paper were not nice reading. Naele-
tua cast unkind reflections on the Leata family
character in general ;• suggested that the father
and mother, the uncles and the cousins and the
aunts, were none of them the least bit better
than they ought to be ; and, finally, classed them
all under the unpoetic heading of Swine. Now,
Leata was said to love her people with a proud
love.
» * * *
This sort of tiling could not be coped with
in a civilised way at all. Leata laid aside
civilised ways and felt all the. old native blood boil
in her veins. She had five stalwart brothers with
sufficient muscle to vindicate the family honor ;
she set off in search of them. On the way she met
Naeletua, and proceeded, with much directness of
P u XP? se ’ , to P oun d that lady’s face; when she had
sufficiently discolored it, she observed cheerfully,
.Now I m going to send my brothers to kill you.”
• * • .
Naeletua did not wait to be killed. She
was not at all clear that her placid Herr could pro-
tect her from the five stalwarts ; neither was she
certain that if he could he would. So she fled
away to the mountains ” —or, more precisely, to
the Samoan bush, where she could safely hide in
the thick, moist jungle, and live on sundry cun-
ning little baskets of provisions brought secretly
by sympathising relatives who did not believe in
platonic sauerkraut. Meanwhile the Stalwarts
stalked around with big knives and bigger threats,
clamoring for Naeletua to come to them and be
treated like a pig.
* * • •
Apparently her absence did not seriously
affect the case, which was prejudged. High
chiefs met in solemn conclave to consider
Is aeletua s delinquencies. The gathering was a
very dignified affair ; Dogberry’s experience when
written down an ass was nothing to it.
Write us down swine ; we have been written
down swine,” cried the brothers in scorn.
“ We have been written down swine, ye stalwart
men,” said the conclave ; “ye are writ down
ignoble swine, and the writer thereby shows her
acquaintance with the ways of swine ; let her be
summoned that she be treated according to her
swinish deserts. Bid Naeletua come here to re-
ceive her due reward as a pig.”
* * * *
Naeletua did not see it at all. She knew
that the Stalwarts were powerful in the council,
and her own people but of small account. She
was very willing to call other folks pigs, but the
term when applied to herself seemed less gratify-
ing. Moreover, she knew the exact rendering of
the expression, “ being treated like a pig.” Naele-
tua stayed in the bush.
* * * *
Then came the ordeal of the pig by proxy—
which is unfair to the pig. The squealing substi-
tute was caught, dirt was stuffed into his mouth,
his fore and hind feet were tied together, and he
was slung on a pole and carried in ignominious
triumph.
“ Thus,” said the Solemn Conclave, “ shall it be
with Naeletua and her children and her children’s
children to the end of their days. We cast them
out from their own people to the swine. Naeletua
no longer bears the name of her ancestors, she no
longer takes rank among Samoans, we proclaim
her children outcasts for all time. She shall have
no claims upon her chiefs, and we wash our hands
of her and her low-born descendants.”
Having reached this happy and desirable conclu-
sion, the Conclave gave the proxy a short shrift
and feasted on its baked remains.
* * * *
What Herr Sauerkraut thought of the
proceedings has not transpired. He accepted the
position allotted to his descendants with Teutonic
calm, and discussed Samoan peculiarities with Mr.
John Bull over lager-beer.
♦ * * *
Having been publicly and formally “ writ
down a pig,” Naeletua had nothing more to fear ;
she was beneath contempt, and therefore beneath
insult. She quietly returned to her papalangi
home, and took up the thread of her life as before,
goinjj about in fine raiment and running-up house-
keeping bills with impunity. But a zest had gone
out of her life and a curl out of her passionate lip.
For when you have been writ down a pig you can
no longer hold your head so high, even if you are
m legal possession of a white trader-husband.
* • * *
But Leata, her virtue and family honor
vindicated, drives matters with a higher hand
than before, and holds supreme court as the one
fashionable papalangi wife with intelligent appre-
ciation of papalangi ways. In proof whereof
Herr Sauerkraut still frequents her table to devour
his niideln with a flavor of piquancy, and satisfied
society smiles approval. Which just shows how
very civilised Samoan society has become.
* * * •
And Naeletua, when she sits on her door-
step, as you may see her doing any evening,
reflects bitterly that it is not enough to own a
papalangi husband, a papalangi bouse, and a
papalangi purse. In order to harmonise your lot
you must speed to papalangi lands and len v n the
ways thereof. Only thus can you unravel the
mysteries of pi a tonics. Only thus can you acquire
the knack of writing down your neighbor a pig
with impunity.
Apia, Samoa.
ROSE DE BOHEME.
S.A. horses, used to eating hay, have, in
some cases, owing to drought, been put on chaff,
er ,m ,P ort ® d from Vic. or brought down from
the .North. Some of these animals died, and post-
mortem disclosed hard balls, apparently of coir
matting, in the intestines. The explanation
i 18 twine with which the sheaves
had been bound.was not thrown out before it was
put through the chaff-mill, and that they had
eaten it.
A prominent feature of Col. Home’s irri-
gation report to the N.S.W. Govt, is the tender
yray in which existing schemes are dealt with.
Where companies or corporations haven’t acquired
rights the Colonel damns cordially ; but where
they have—-at J:lay, Halranald, Wentworth, and
Menindie, for instance—he spares the interested
parties his denunciations. In fact, he says the
carrying out of the schemes would be valuable
from an educational point of view. Yet as to the
Menindie project (from which Broken Hill once
expected so much), it was obviously hard work to
escape saying that it was a species of lunacy.
When certain people fall out, it is alleged
that honest folk get their due. Interesting, there-
fore, to note that Rev. Rentoul, in advocating the
purchase of a new Dayspring for mission purposes
m the South Seas, instead of a subsidy to the pre-
sent shipping co., relieved himself of the following :
That eminent authority upon morals and theology, the
Argus newspaper, was opposed to the scheme. When the
Argus. supported this shipping co. for the carrying out of
the mission work, they might be quite sure that vested
interests were at the back of it.
Will Dr. Rentoul admit that similar reasons
prompt the Argus advocacy of, say, the Scripture
Education League claims ?
